THE BED PAGE OF AUSTRALASIAN NOTES AND QUERIES.
[lt is desired to collet* information from all manner of
readers and observers on all manner of curious and in-
teresting subjects. A not-exclusive preference will be
given to notes and queries especially Australasian, or kin
with Australasian, in their origin and reference—parti-
cularly to those dealing with aboriginal life and language,
natural history, white-Australasian language, customs,
exploits and typical developments, characteristic old-
time anecdotes, and so on. Brevity of statement is
essential. Writers must observe the ordinary correspon-
dence conditions. The help of Bljlle in readers every-
where is asked in recording some of many valuable facts,
impressions, or experiences which may otherwise be lost
to our country. Notes may be forwarded on any interes
ing topic not necessarily relating to something alread;
in print. Queries may be suggested; and any relevai;
information will be welcome in reply, even if not precise!
answering. Contributions must be endorsed 'Notes anc
Queries/ and if dealing with matters previously referred
to the number of note or query and date of issue must b
added. All references to books should state title, author
publisher, date of publication, place where publishec
and pages quoted from ; and all records of persona
experience should give date and place of occurrenc
(province, district, miles from nearest post-town), anc
other helpful details.]
40.— Aboriginal Hock Paintings .
So far as I am aware, the first account of th
discovery of rock paintings in any part of thi
continent is contained in Capt. M. Flinders
‘Voyage to Terra Australis,’ vol. ii., pp. 188-189
On the walls of a cave at Chasm Island, on th
western side of the Gulf of Carpentaria, Flinder
states that on the 14th January, 1803, he “ founc
rude drawings made with charcoal and something
like red paint, upon the white ground of the rock
They represented porpoises, turtles, kangaroos
and the human hand.’ There was also ‘the repre
sentation of a kangaroo with a file of 32 person
following after it. The third person of the banc
was twice the height of the others, and held in hi
hand something resembling the waddy, or wooden
swprd, of the natives of Port Jackson.’
Aboriginal rock-paintings are executed in three
different ways, which may be called the stencil
the impression, and the outline methods respec
tively. In the stencil method, the palm of the
hand was placed firmly on the rock, with the
fingers and thumb spread out, and the requirec
color—generally pipe-clay, red ochre, or charcoal—
was squirted or blown over it out of the mouth o:
the operator. This manner of drawing was also
adopted in many instances in representing imple
ments of the chase, such as boomerangs, toina
hawks, waddies, &c. In the impression method
the color to be used was mixed with a liquid in i
native vessel, into which the palm of the hanc
was lightly dipped and then pressed against tire
surface of the rock. On the removal of the hanc
the colored imprint was left clearly defined. Ob-
jects to which neither of the preceding methods
Would be applicable were drawn in outline, in the
required colors. In some cases the objects were
merely outlined, in other cases they were colored
all over with awash, whilst in others the space
within the margin of the outlines was shaded by
strokes of color.
In the annexed plate, Fig. 1 represents fpur
hands stencilled in red, two snakes, three lizards,
and part of an iguana, the remainder of the latter
being supposed to have disappeared into a crack
in the rock. The head of one of the snakes is
hidden in a crack in a similar manner. The rest
of the figures comprise two six-legged objects
which I cannot identify, and four straight lines—
three being in one group, and another close
to one of the hands. Fig. 2, which is in the
same cave as Fig. 1, shows two drawings which
may have been intended to represent the sun, an
iguana, a death-adder, four footmarks of birds,
and a small cross. Fig. 3 represents a group ol
25 impressed hands. All the paintings in Figs. 1,
2 and 3 are done in red color on a large rook about
three quarters of a mile southerly from Portion
No. 4, parish of Wilpinjong, county of Phillip.
Fig. 4 delineates three birds, one of which is on
the wing ; a kangaroo., and a dingo or native cat,
all drawn in black colour, in a cave near Portion
No. 33, parish of Wareng, county of Hunter.
Fig. 5 contains three human figures, a kangaroo,
and four objects resembling eels. They are all in
black colour, and are situated near Portion No.
16, parish of Macdonald, county of Hunter. Fig. 6
represents a man and woman apparently dancing
a corroboree, and is drawn in black, red, and
white colours, in a cave near Portion No. 42,
parish of Tollagong, eounr.y of Hunter. The
other drawings represent a human foot, a hand, a
native tomahawk, a boomerang, and a waddy. all
done in stencil, in white color. Fig. 7, which is
locally known among the white settlers as the
‘Devil in the Rock,’ is drawn in deep black in
a small cave close to the Macdonald River, in
the parish of Kindarun, county of Hunter. Mr.
Joseph Merrick told me he had known of this
drawing for the past 70 years, and that it was
therewhen he first came into the district. If any
readers who take an interest in the pictorial art
of a primitive people who are rapidly passing
away, should happen to see or hear of any notable
aboriginal paintings or carvings on rocks, I trust
they will spare half-an-hour to write to ‘The
Bulletin,’describing the drawings and their loca-
tion.—R. H. Mathews, Parramatta.
4. —Marsupial Reproduction.
The following quotation is from an abridgement
of K, E. Jung’s ‘Australia’ (Lond., 1884, pp. 63,
64). —G.G.
Professor Owen observes: ‘I have always connected
with the long droughts in Australia—with the extensive
traots where there are no water, with the difficulty of
obtaining that necessary element of life, the singular
peculiarity of organisation which prevails amongst the
quadrupeds of Australia. No matter whether they burrow
like the wombat, climb like the phalanger, jump like the
kangaroo, trot like the bandicoot, or fly like the petauris
—all these creatures are marsupial. They are creatures
carrying their delicate, prematurely-born young about
with them wherever they go. They have this condition,
viz., a soft, warm, well-lined, portable nursery pocket, or
“ peranebulator.” Take the case of one of our wild
quadrupeds—a fox or a wild-cat; they make their nest,
they have their litter. Suppose it should happen that
they must travel one or two hundred miles to get
a drink of water, and are obliged to leave the little
family at home, where would that family be when the
parent returned from its journey? Why, starved to death.
In order that quadrupeds should be fitted to exist in a
great continent like Australia, where the meteoric con-
ditions are such as to produce the dilemma I have in-
stanced, those quadrupeds must possess an organisation
suited to such peculiar and climatic conditions. And so
it is ; that form of mammalian quadruped in this great
continent, native to it, and born so as to make these
migrations to obtain that necessity of life, has the super-
added pouch and genetic peculiarities, enabling them to
carry their young ones wherever they go. And since we
find that marsupial animals have lived in Australia from
a very remote period, so we may infer that its peculiar
olimate has prevailed during as vast a lapse of time.
ABORIGINAL ROCK PAINTINGS (Paragraph 40).
B.— Ants.
1 have seen blacks on the Maranoa, the hot-bed
of ants—shifting away from the little pests ; and
I heard one old black complimenting the atten-
tive emmet in the real colonial. The odour of the
average black is a mixture of ’possum and euca-
lypt; and as the ant has a natural taste for
’possum and a strong inclination towards habitats
in the hollows of gum-trees—from the bark and
leaves of which it also obtains part of its food-
why should it be antipathetic to the eucalyptus-
’possum-saturated aborigine ?—Werrimee.
• * * *
There is a small red ant known to Q. bushmen
which has a remarkable power of foretelling
floods. Before very heavy rains, the precursors of
big floods, every colony of these ants on low
ground will be seen shifting camp to a higher
level; and even ascending tre.es and carrying their
property with them. The finest weather may be
prevailing at the time ; but when they say ‘ look
out ’ it is wise to heed them. Has this minor
prophet a definite name ?—E.S.S.
41.— The Eagle Hawk.
It is nob generally known that the eagle hawk
constructs a table on which to eat his food. I had
often mistaken these tables for old nests, till one
day I found one in a gidgea tree on the Warri
Warri. The bird was perched on top, feeding.
Under the tree was a mass of bones and feathers.
I climbed up, and was surprised to find that the
supposed nest was a structure of stout, thick
sticks, closely and skilfully interlaced, with a top
almost square and as flat as a board. On it was a
small and half-devoured wallaby. I have climbed
up to and examined many such structures since,
and, as I haye seen several nests of the eagle hawk,
some of which contained young birds, I may
safely assume that these tables are specially con-
structed for eating on. The eagle hawk generally
builds its nest in the top of the highest tree. I
have always found the ‘tables’ in very low trees—
such as the gidgea.—E.S.S.
22.— The Crow.
While riding home one evening I heard a flock
of crows making a great noise in a clump of
mulga. As I approached they took flight, crying
loudly as they flew west. I examined the spot
they had left, and found crow feathers strewn
about for a considerable distance, and the fresh
head and beak of a crow in the midst of them.
From my previous experience U the carrion crow,
I summed matters up thus : They had found their
messmate sick or disabled; had called their fel-
lows, who thereupon examined and tried him, and
found him guilty of the one great offence in crow
law, for which the penalty is death—viz., not being
in a fit condition to commit depredations. Where-
upon they duly and dutifully pecked him to
death, and then ate him with great jubilation.
It seemed to me that they flew off with a prouder
cry than usual; but the incident convinced me
that the crow is the lowest bird in the bush, bar
none.
The crows in the N.E. of N.S.W. have very
dark eyes, and are exceedingly shy. I never saw
a white-eyed crow till I came near to the S.A.
border. Bushmen in the N. W. call these ‘South
Australians.’ Their cry is different from that of
the eastern crows, and they are very bold, and
afflicted with an appetite that nothing in creation
can appease. Queenslanders in the far west abhor
the souud of the crow, hate the sight of him, and
loathe the very mention of him. I have seen Q.
drovers leave their dinner to pelt sticks at one
that dared to quark in their hearing.—E.S.S.
50.— Deaf Adders.
I assume that, thanks to M'Cooey and others,
most people are persuaded that the adder has no
sting m its tail. But it has, I think, never been
explained why the adder doubles up at the
slightest touch—diving, as it were, both with head
and tail at the interfering object ? This habit is
largely responsible for the one-time popular belief
that adders had stings in their tails. To my
knowledge no other snake does it. On in-
quiry a year or two ago at Dubbo, N.S.W., snake-
hunter and showman Antonio, well-known in the
back-country, gave me this explanation : “ Deaf-
adders differ mainly from all other Australian
snakes in their muscular system. When black,
brown, tiger and other snakes bite or are ready to
bite they flatten their heads, and thereby put a
muscular pressure upon the little poison-bags un-
derlying each fang. This pressure is required to
force the poison into the pipes of the teeth ready
for injection. Deaf-adders lack these head
muscles, and consequently have to tighten ( i.e.
double up) their whole bodies to put the required
pressure upon the poison bags and squirt the
substance into the fangs.” “ Professor ” Antonio
had, by the way, a fine collection of live black,
brown and other snakes. One old man black snake
with red belly was 7ft. 4in. long. “I camped
three weeks near Tumut, N.S.W., to catch that
fellow, remarked the man of serpents. —6 xB.
34.— Squatter.
Webster’s Unabridged,’ 1886, derives ‘squat-
ter from the verb ‘ squat, 5 to sit down with hams
on heels, mentioning Old, Middle and Provincial
English verbs ‘quat,’ ‘squat,’ &c., with same
meaning. Squat’ undoubtedly is from Lat.
coacto through the Old French quattir. In the
United Kingdom a squatter is a man who settles
on or jumps without title common or moorland.
Hence the U.S. usage of ‘squatter’ for a man
who settles on new or public land without a title.
Ihe Gentury Dictionary ’ and the 1892 ‘Webster’
note the special application in Australia of ‘ squat-
ter to the renter of a big tract of public land.

31. —Australian Slang.
6xB says that the application of “ point it ’’ to
a bone, in bush slang, is new to him. In Samuel
Lover s play, “The White Horse of Peppers,”
one of the characters got much fun out of feeding
an evil-tempered Dutch General on “potatoesand
point the potatoes being pointed at a stale
herring in order to give them a flavor.—T.D
* * * *
Linger, applied to the fastest shearer in a shed
may be borrowed from the itinerant shooting-
gallery, in which the shot which strikes plumb-
centre passes through a small hole in the centre of
the target and rings a bell placed immediately
behind. ouch a shot cannot be beaten, and is
termed a ‘ringer.’ The application is evident.
Drummer,’ as applied to the last or slowest
shearer on the board, may have reference to the
position of the drum in the ordinary brass band?
Gobbler,’meaning the last sheep in the pen,
smacks of the classical, and may refer to the last
beyond which, according to the well-worn Latin
j lO ® e^'re ®P eo^n ß cobbler should go ?
Putting Kincliela on ’em ’ is evidently inspired
by the fact that one Kinchela, some years ago,
wrote and published a pamphlet on the art of
sharpening and ‘ keeping ’ shears. The expression
was at first oonfined exclusively to shear-sharpen-
in £6 but in time came to have a wider application.
—Mungindt.

52. —‘John Dunn. ’
Many Australians have heard a one-pound note
called a ‘ John Dunn.’ The term originated with
John Dunn, the founder, and for many years
managing director of the Commercial Bank,
Hobart, who signed his name in full on all notes
issued by the bank.—M.D.
I. —Red Hair.
Among the early Scandinavian pagans, red-
haired people were considered to be especially
favored by the Sun-God. 4 Sun-kissed ’ ife still ap-
plied to pretty red-haired girls by the East-coast
Scots, who to some extent are of Scandinavian
origin. On the introduction of Christianity the
red-haired ones fell from their high estate, and
were held in general detestation by the enthusiastic
propagandists of the new religion. From the
golden tint being a mark of divine favor it speedily
became a brand of the devil; and the unfortunate
wearers were supposed to possess many qualities
of their infernal patron. I have known many
red-haired girls, and found they all had a dash of
the devil in them ; but they were very nice, espe-
cially the blue-eyed, pretty ones.—D.H.S.
27. — Aboriginals.
Hugh Harding says he has never seen a W.A.
native pick up anything from the ground with the
hand. So far as Coelgardie is cencerned the hand
is always used in picking anything up, and after
an observation extending over three years I have
only seen the foot used once—to pick up a stick.
The men always use the hand in picking up any
weapon, but they do object to carrying anything.
I recently watched a distribution of blankets and
rations. The men and women took their share,
but the men immediately turned their share over
to the gins to carry to camp. They walked along-
side. The official who conducted the distribution
vainly tried to invoke a cheer in honor of Queen
Vic. ; but they simply couldn’t grasp the idea.
They tried ; but it was no use.—A.J.B., Broad
Arrow, W. A.
28. — Phenomenal Water-Springs in Drought.
There is a spring on Mt. Canoblas (Western
N.S.W.) not a quarter of a mile from the summit,
which gave a renewed flow at the same time as
various springs from 10 to 30 miles in various
directions. If Q. Geologist Jack’s ‘long pipe’
theory is correct, these springs, having a com-
mon source, could scarcely give an increased flow
si mul taneously. — Brendinyabba, Toogong.
Gordon’s Relatives.
36.—According to a statement in ‘Australa-
sian ’ (Nov. 30, 1805) Gordon married a Miss
Maggie Park at Mount Gambier in 1862. One
child was the result of this union—a girl, who
died while yet an infant, at Ballarat, in a little
house facing the stable-yard behind Craig’s hotel.
On Gordon’s death, I believe his widow married
again. -

A scene, which anywhere else in the
world would have resulted in a duel or two, oc-
curred a little time ago in Melb. District Court.
An Italian vocalist, disputing the fit of a suit of
clothes, was sued for the cost, and turned up with
a titled fellow countryman, say the Count da
Maquoroni, as chief witness. Prosecutor, a push-
ing little Hebrew, was describing how he delivered
the goods. “ I handed the suit to Mr. Blancho,”
he said, “and his valet, Mr. Maquoroni, was
also present.” Instantaneously there shot up
from the rear of the court two immense mous-
tachios, followed by the handsome form of the
Count da Maquoroni. “Ha ! Ha ! Do I looka
lika one valet ? Ha ! Do I looka lika one valet ? ”
he yelled, with indescribable vim. At length the
infuriated nobleman was calmed by the united
efforts of the bench, the witnesses, and the police,
and, during the rest of the case, he contented
himself with muttering maledictions and shaking
his clenched fist at the floor at intervals. “I was
determined to get home on to the Maquoroni,
quietly remarked the witness on leaving the box.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

The plaintiff in a recent Sydney District
Court case, giving evidence about the difficulty of
collecting debts, was asked by the Judge, apropos
of something or other, whether N.S.W. Ms.P.
were “ prompt pays.” “ Members of Parliament!”
cried plaintiff; “ they’re the worst in the coun-
try. You can’t touch their salaries, and what-
ever they have is in their wives’ names, or tied up
somehow. They’re a rascally lot.”

by norologer): “ What’s wrong with this watch ?’•

whistles loudly): “ Whew ! Mainspring’s broke/'

and ostentatiously dislodging small obstacle pur-
poseiy and previously inserted in minor cog-
wheel) : “Whew ! (still more loudly) Mainspring’s
sound again ! ” Exit, with ten digits to nose.

[FOB THE BOLLKTIN.]
A STRANGER AT THE U.
He was guileless in his manner—’twas a style the
boys admired,
And he told, in simple language, how his feelings
had been fired
By the news of nuggets waiting, in numbers not a
few,
For any simple stranger that might strike the
1.0. U-
. golden 1.0. U.,
Where they put the wash-dirt through
The rattling, creaking shakers in the gullies round
the U.
With a smile of easy confidence he told the boys
around
That his native place was Albany upon King
George’s Sound,
And he’d left his father’s flocks and herds, and
poison grass, and such,
To know the world in general, and come in closer
touch,
At the diggings on the U,
With the hardy men and true
That caught the unobtrusive weight in sieves upon
the U.
It was moved by Mick M‘Carthy, hailing from the
Sydney side,
That O’Doolan be the stranger’s friend, philo-
sopher and guide;
And that every information be furnished him
about
Our little recreations when the week had given
out—
For we hoped to put him through
In a manner that would do
Lasting credit to the boys that were assembled on
the U.
He was led by invitation to the two-up on the flat,
That was decently conducted by a pug. from
Ballarat;
And he said, in wild astonishment, he thought
that kindly Fate
Had other joys in store for him than looking for
the weight;
Yes, he would gladly do
A modest hand or two
In the interesting school that was established on
the U.
It was sad to see him betting in the confidence of
youth,
For the grey was rung upon him—though with
disregard for truth
I could palliate my statement, yet the morals that
I hold
Force me to the free confession that he was com-
pletely sold
By the boys upon the U.,
Who thought to make him rue
His first attempt at two-up, as played upon
the U.
We were flush with paper money when it got too
dark to play,
For the brumby changed his fivers in a wildly
reckless way ;
Though it didn’t seem to strike us, in anxiety to
win,
That we’d all been taking flimsies, and were
giving change in tin
To the stranger at the U,
With the crispy notes and new
He circulated freely ’mong the diggers on the U.
Paddy Grady’s “ Hessian Palace ” was a scene of
wild delight,
And we drank the shypoo deeply, till the lateness
of the night
Suggested a retirement; but O’Doolan swore a
round
Should be drunk in grateful honor of the latest
patch we’d found ;
And he paid for the shypoo
With a crispy note and new,
He had earned by tossing pennies with a stranger
on the U.
Grady took the note and scanned it, then in
measured words and cold
Said the “Bank of Hope” was dying—would
O’Doolan pay in gold ?
And, sarcastically soothing, said he hoped the
patch would give
A slightly better prospect when we put it through
the sieve,
To find if one or two
Of the notes so crisp and new
Would buy a first-class coffin for the stranger on
the U.
There was wailing in the shanty, and a hurried
search was made
To find the gentle stranger who our kindness had
betrayed ;
But he’d vanished, taking with him as a solace to
the mind
Half a hundred quid in change for all the notes
he’d left behind;
And the boys of 1.0. U.
Now with dark suspicion view
Every too-confiding stranger putting pegs in at
the U.
W.A.
J. CRAWFORD.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 25, 1897.
The Judgment of Solomon Peacock.
One of the especially cheeky minorities in
Australian politics is the Bible-in-schools
faction, or, rather, the set of factions which
makes up that faction. This body rises up,
more or less, at every general election in
Maoriland, New South Wales, South Aus-
tralia and Victoria, and demands a lot of
things not very well defined. It is solid on
five main points, however : (1) That it con-
stitutes a majority of the people. (2) That
it really constitutes a majority in Parlia-
ment, only malign influences keep it back.
(3) That if it doesn’t constitute a majority in
Parliament it is only because the issues are
so badly mixed at elections that the matter
wasn’t put fairly and squarely before the
electors, but if a referendum were taken on
this issue alone it would wipe up the floor
with the ungodly. (4) That, when it does
get its referendum and is hopelessly licked,
as was the case in South Australia, that
doesn’t prove that it is in a minority—in fact,
it rather proves that it is in a majority ; but
for some reason that the monkey-shave men
are quite positive about its adherents didn’t
come to the poll. Also (5), That even if it
is a minority —a small, mean minority that
needs to stand on an envelope to see over
the top of l£d. —it ought to rule because its
cause is the sacred cause of the Lokd.
This latter fact is advanced on the
unsupported assertion of the gentlemen with
the monkey-shave, and so is quite incontro-
vertible, for these same gentlemen state, also
on their own unsupported authority, that they
know exactly what are the views of the
Deity on the subject. If anyone asks how
they came to know, they generally reply that
the question shows him to be utterly Lost.
« * * *
Quite a number of politicians have tried at
different times to secure the vote of this
aggressive minority without losing that of
the secular or godless majority, and they
have mostly regretted it. Yet the Bible-
readers are not so very small a minority
after all, and their combined vote would be
worth having if any one could get it. Only
nobody can get it, or, at least, nobody can
keep it. Out of IUOO average people there
are, perhaps, 300 or 400 who want Scriptural
instruction in schools. They are prepared to
throw in a solid vote against the man who
believes in godless education, only, unfortu-
nately, they can’t vote against one man with-
out voting for another. When it comes to
voting for the man who doesn’t believe in
godless education they subdivide into the
people who want the Catholic or the Pro-
testant version of the “Word” read in
schools, the people who want it read without
explanation or with it, and the people who
want the Protestant version explained on the
principle of baptism by pouring water over
part of the candidate when he is too young
to resist, and those who want it set forth
that the whole candidate should be par-
tially drowned when he is grown up.
Also, there are the people who want the
Scriptures expounded according to the
doctrine of Free-will, and those who want to
teach rigid Calvinism and the damnation of
unbaptised infants; likewise people who
believe in one Deity, and in three, and Jews,
and long-haired people, and people who don’t
want the State to teach religion at all, but to
hand over the whole education business to
the churches with a handsome subsidy. The
result of all this is that the Bible-in-schools
vote is an awkward thing to be without, and
is no good at all when one gets it, because,
as soon as a politician secures it, he hasn’t
got it. The best that he can hope for is to
lose the sectarian vote, and to get the sup-
port of one fragmentary sect while all the
others prospect for his scalp.
* * * *
The latest politician who has thus fallen
in is Peacock, Minister for Education in
Victoria. With a general election in pros-
pect, Peacock seems to have carefully
dropped his bit of string and his worm into
the troubled waters of sectarianism to see if
any votes could be fished out, and the results
were disastrous. The Vic. Minister tried his
experiment in an astute fashion. He didn’t
propose to introduce the Bible into the edu-
cation system, or to have it read, or explained
on any particular basis, nor did he propose
to subsidise denominational schools, or in-
troduce any theological matter into the
school books. He merely printed in the
School Paper, a monthly publication which
is supposed to give the scholars information
about current events, a small and perfectly
harmless Psalm. The Psalms are mostly non-
committal Hebrew poetry of the least con-
troversial description; and the paper in
which this one was published was a very
little affair which could be suppressed and
put in the fire at any moment ; and the
teachers were not instructed to explain it or
to say anything at all about it ; and it was
only one Psalm, anyway, and a very diminu-
tive Psalm at that. Then Peacock con-
cealed himself in the Federal Convention at
Sydney to watch results. The results came
in the shape of a blast from the Holy Roman
church to the effect that it wasn’t going to
have any Psalm, not even a very small one,
explained to any small Roman scholar except
by an orthodox Holy Roman teacher. And
as the Victorian State School teachers are
not all Holy Romans, the Church objects to
the whole business.
9 ♦ * *
Judging by the consequences of this one
small experiment, it is probable that two
more Psalms would raise a R.C. block vote
against the Turner Ministry, and leave it
with a very small majority, or perhaps no
majority at all. At all events, it is to be
hoped that Peacock now sees the futility of
fishing for the Bible vote any further. The
Judgment of Solomon was nothing compared
to the difficulty which Solomon Peacock
will have before him if he carries on his ex-
periment. The Solomon of Jerusalem had
only to decide between two women who
wanted one infant, and one of them was not
very particular about the infant after all.
The Solomon of Melbourne would have to
tackle about twenty sects who all want the
earth, and want it with desperate intensity.
The average sect would much rather have no
religion taught in the schools than have the
other fellow’s religion taught there ; also the
average sect is firmly opposed to the teaching
of any vague, non-committal faith, but wants
its particular blast of theology adopted in
full, and no other blast of theology whatever.
Therefore, the only possible result would be
to aggravate the secular majority and to rouse
up the bitter hostility of every sect save one.
9 * * *
The Bulletin wonders what would have
happened if Peacock had started his experi-
ment in a really vigorous fashion. Suppos-
ing he had commenced with a slab of
straight-out Unitarianism, or had angled for
the Calvinist vote by an energetic support
of the worst kind of material hell—the one
where the Lost canter round the cinder-track
for ever at a temperature of 10,000,000 in
the shade, or if he had started his career by
mentioning that no truly orthodox person
could believe in the baptism of adults by
dropping the entire believer into water, then
it would probably have been necessary to
throw the too-active Minister overboard on
the instant to save the Government. So far
as the evidence goes, all the Bible-in-schools
patties put together don’t constitute half the
population, but, even if they do, it is evi-
dently impossible to do anything for them
until they come together among themselves
and decide what kind of Scriptural teaching
they want. If they can arrive at any definite
agreement without tearing each other's hair
out by the roots, or each knocking the
other’s sacerdotal hat endways, then they
may get something. The Scriptural party
is probably more in earnest than its oppon-
ents—the people who want to do something
for conscience sake are generally far more
earnest than those who want to leave some-
thing as it is for the sake of convenience ;
and when it is able to come to an agreement
there is a fair chance that it will carry its
point. There is also a fair chance that it
will come to that agreement about the year
one hundred billion. When the churches
have got their orthodoxy so bad that the
sight of one Psalm rouses them to fury, it is
difficult to imagine how the sight of a whole
Scripture would affect them. The most pro-
bable consequence would be gory murder,
and by this time the judgment of Solomon
Peacock probably is that the less Scripture
the Ministry prints till the elections are over
the better it will be for its chances.
Last Year’s Plowers.
Now that Premier Reid, of N.S.W., has re-
turned from the land of jubilation, and is about
to make another financial statement, and to
announce once more that bis obvious deficit is a
surplus, a certain amount of interest attaches
to his last budget, and a fine opportunity pre-
sents itself to explain some of the flowers of
rhetoric contained therein. Most of these
assertions couldn’t be verified at the time, be-
cause the Government statistics for the period
alluded to were not then published, so Premier
Reid was able to splash himself around on his
favorite topic to any extent he pleased, and
play the giddy financial ox generally. The fol-
lowing, from the S.M. Herald of 10th July,
1896, is interesting reading, in the light of
recent information :
The Premier : There is the fact that At Hudson's, Which
Was said to be ruined and about to close up, they had A
better lookout now thail they had had fot a lonar tiitlle
past. They also said that the boot-trade Was going tb
ruin, but what are the facts? During the bdonl-time of
1892 there Were 2708 hands engaged in the boot-factories,
and on the 13th Decent bet last there were 3752 hands, an
increase Of 1000 hands. . . . There is no better test in
Arty industry than the growth of population no better
test of the decline of industry than the decline of
population. Well, during the last four years, from
1891 to 1895, the population of Victoria increased by
24,000 souls. During the same time the population
of New South Wales increased by 112,000 souls,
nearly five times the Victorian increase.
There is a little item in the manufacture of woollen and
cloth goods. Last year —in 1895—in New South Wales we
turned out 33 per cent, more woollen and cloth tweeds
than in the previous year. I can give you the number of
yards if you wish. We turned out 300 per cent, more
than in 1892, the Protectionist boom year. (Hear, hear.)
Now Mr. Lyne, in his speeches, and some of the news-
papers, point out that there has been a great deal more
cloth imported during the last two years than befoie,
That, they say, is the fault of Freetrade. it never
occurred to them that the people were buying more
things. Now in clothing factories the hands employed in
1894 were 1466, and in 1896 1770, To dispel the fallacy
that the increased import of any given article necessarily
implies less employment in the colony, in 1891 and 1892
the value ot clothing was £1,000,000 more in value than
in 1893 and 1894, yet the hands employed in 1891 and 1892
were from 1000 to 1600 more than those in 1893 and 1894.
According to Mr. LYne, in 1891 we were All ruined, and in
1893-4 there were a lot of new clothing factories started,
yet in 1891 and 1892 there Were 1000 to 1500 more hands
employed than during the years of the small imports.
* * * *
.Since these remarks were uttered the old-
established firm of Hudson Brothers, above-
quoted, has come hopelessly to grief. This is
a result against which there is no arguing, so
apparently the statement that its look-out was
better than for a long time past was pure
invention, or pure delusion, or moonshine, or
something to the same effect. As regards the
hoot and shoe business, the fat optimist with
the eye glass stated proudly that the number
of hands employed had increased from 2708 in
1892 to 3752 in 1895, and he distinctly inferred
that the increase shown in the latter figure was
a solid proof of the advantages of Freetrade.
Considering that the boot-trade was a protected
one from the beginning of 1892 till the end of
1895, his figures only demonstrated that Pro-
tection was a remarkably good thing for this
industry, and the gall of a man who could quote
the progress made in four years of Protection as
a proof of the advantages of Freetrade is some-
thing unique. Since then the boot-trade has
had one year of Freetrade, and the number of
employes, even though the small factories
which were formerly omitted are now reckoned
in, has decreased to 3499. This is an item
which George Reid should make some attempt
to explain away in his coming financial state-
ment.
• * * •
According to the same amiable 18 stone of
humanity, “ there is no better test of the
decline of industry than the decline of popula-
tion.” As 1896, the first year of George
Reid’s own particular brand of Freetrade—the
most advanced system of Freetrade which New
South Wales ever enjoyed—was also the first
in a series of thirty-four years when the
province lost population through the number of
departures exceeding the arrivals, The Bulle-
tin takes the Cornstalk Premier at his word.
By his own showing the year when his boasted
policy came into vogue was the very worst,
so far as industry was concerned, in the pro-
vince’s history. To say anything else would
be to say that Premier Reid was a Mun-
chausen and an Ananias, and this paper
wouldn’t like to call him these names, except
under extreme provocation. In the same year
the increase of population (there was still an
increase through excess of births over deaths)
was the smallest since 1881, that being the
earliest year for which the writer has figures
available. Possibly it was also the smallest
for a much longer period. Therefore, it again
seems, on Premier Reid’s own showing, that
the fir3t year of Freetrade was the worst, in the
last 16 at all events, for New South W 7 ales in-
dustry. To say anything else would be to call
the genial deficit-man another gross perverter
of the truth, and The Bulletin regards him
with a certain amount of kindly feeling as a per-
son who has supplied it with more genuine
amusement than any other politician of recent
years, and is reluctant to call him hard names
unnecessarily. As Premier Reid truly observed,
during the protective period (he didn’t mention
that it was the protective period, but that is a
mere detail) the population of New South Wales
increased at the rate of 28,000 a year. And,
during the first year of Freetrade, the increase
fell with a thud to 19,770.
* ♦ • »
Further, this man of much blubber and men-
tal confusion remarked that from the beginning
of the protected year 1892 to the end of the
protected year 1895, the New South Wales
output of woollen goods increased threefold.
He didn’t mention that 1895 was a protected
year—in fact, he quoted the figures proudly as
a proof of the blessed advantages of Freetrade
—but the fact remains unaltered by that genial
misrepresentation. Since then there lias been
one year of Freetrade, and the production has
diminished considerably. Mr. Reid hasn’t
mentioned this latter fact, but he doubtless
will. He is also quoted as staling that the
number of employes in clothing factories in-
creased from 14(30 in the protected year 1894
to 1770 in the Freetrade year 1896. As a
matter of fact, according to the official statistics
just published, the number increased to 1777
in the protected year 1895, and fell to 1255 in
the Freetrade year 1890. Therefore, if Premier
Reid said, as lie is alleged to have done, that
the number quoted were employed in 1890, his
own Government Statistician directly accuses
him of some very steep falsehood. And if, as
is more probable, what he really said was that
1770 people were employed in 1895, and he was
merely guilty of creating the wrong impression
that 1895 was a Freetrade year, and that the
increase was a proof of the advantages of
Freetrade, instead of the direct opposite, then
the offence boils down to simple misrepresenta-
tion and mean concealment of the truth. It
was tolerably unscrupulous misrepresentation,
possibly, but the mere adjective is not worth
cavilling about.
* * * »
Still, when Parliament meets, and Premier
REid rises to point out that, bjr receiving
,£9,309,269 ahd spending £9,480,214 he has got
anothet handsome surplus, he might find a
favorable opportunity to explain these few cit-
cumstatices. Also in the same utterance of a
year ago he mentioned with pHde that he Was
placing locally a large contract for steel rails,
“because we could get them manufactured
here without any of your coddling,” and that
“one of the biggest men in England had ottered
to put down £15,000 as security for the manu-
facture of 150,000 tons of rails from our own
native ores ” ; and the financial statement of
1897 should present a favourable opportunity
for explaining where that very big man, and
his very big deposit, and the 150,000 tons of
rails, and the new' industry which wasn’t to be
coddled, have all gone to. In its own small,
forlorn way The Bulletin has asked G. ReID
at different times to explain quite a lot of
things like the above, without getting ahy
answer, Still it keeps asking, upon the off-
chance that some kind of reply may be forth-
coming by-and-bye, There is ho especial hiirty
about that reply, The Bulletin calculates Oil
being aroUnd to ask these questions and others
like them as long as George Reid is politically
above ground.
PLAIN ENGLISH
Employers’ Liability.
The soreness that at present exists in Melb.
with reference to the utter uselessness of bring-
ing actions in the county courts to recover
damages for accidents to workmen in the
sewerage works, emphasises the absolute need
there is of having something like a decent
and simple Employers’ Liability Act on every
Australian statute- book. Technicality after
technicality baulks the maimed and the orphans
of the killed ; notices of accident and notices
of action prevent them from getting to court
at all in a great many cases. Inability to strictly
prove that the person directly responsible for
the accident was a superintendent, prevents
them in other cases getting to the jury, even
when they get to court. A jury with a stiff
property qualification generally manages to
prevent them getting justice when they get to
the jury, and when, as occasionally happens,
they obtain a verdict, the present Act is so
absolutely cumbrous that it is a matter of ease
for the penalised contractor to get a new trial
ordered for some defect of procedure, or
wrongful admission of some paltry evidence.
Why should one law be applied to accidents
occurring while digging shafts and tunnels
for sewerage purposes, and quite a different
one in the case of accidents while digging
shafts and drives for mining purposes? Why
should not all workmen in dangerous occupa-
tions—and, for the matter of that, all workmen—
be placed in the same position as the miner?
When an accident occurs in a mine, the owner
is prima facie guilty of a negligence which gives
a right of action, and, according to a recent
decision of Queensland Full Court, as given by
Chief Justice Griffith, is, in the absence of
evidence rebutting the presumption of negli-
gence, practically an insurer of his workmen’s
freedom from accident. The late English
Workmen’s Compensation Act extends this
principle to most workmen, and the frequent
occurrence of sewerage and other accidents
shows the crying need of some such plan in
Australia, divested of the present abuses, caus-
ing employers to be at least as careful of their
workmen’s lives as of their machinery, and
giving some small and easily obtained solatium
to the wounded soldiers of industry. In short,
every employer should be by law an Accident
Insurance Company for the benefit of all his
workmen who meet with accidents while in the
scope of their employment.
A Small Tin God.
It is a little depressing to listen to the Austra-
lian workman making himself hoarse in applaud-
ing Ben Tillett’s expressions of superior con-
tempt for what has been done on the Austra-
lian workman’s behalf. Not a word of generous
acknowledgment of what has been accomplished
by men who, in some cases, have personally
suffered a great deal more than Mr. TiI, LETT
has done. Not a word to encourage gratitude
for labors which have not been followed -by


fairly fat secretaryships and organising agencies.
But a flood of words telling of the enormous
strides that have been made in Great Britain —
where, of course, the movement has had the
advantage of Mr. Tillett’s guidance. Nor is
the case improved by the remembrance that
some of the keenest suffering on the part of
Australian Labor men and Democrats, and
some of the most disastrous failures, have come
from following too closely the methods inaugur-
ated by Messrs. Tillett and Co. For instance,
the demand of unionists that no non-unionists
should be employed with them, which was at
the bottom of two or three of those staggering
strikes from which the unions have not yet re-
covered, was first enforced by the London
leaders ; probably the first manifesto which em-
bodied the further demand that unionists must
not handle non-unionist-made goods was signed
bv Tillett and Mann in connection with the
Hay’s wharf dispute in Jan., 1891. And, as to
the power of the Australian workman in mat-
ters of Government—well, it hasn’t even been
thought worth remembering that when Til-
lett’s dockers were on strike the N.S.W.
Government of the day (and this was before the
Labor-party came in) arranged that the Aus-
tralian unions might send cable messages to
Tillett as their general secretary at specially
reduced rates?
Deadlocks.
The Federal Convention struck the worst snag
of ils career when it tackled the clauses dealing
with the settlement of deadlocks. The more
the subject was debated the more obvious it
became that there was no wholly logical solu-
tion to be found anywhere. The following is a
condensed statement of the case :
(1) The Lower House is to be elected on a population
basis. If the Federation consists of the five provinces
represented at the Convention, N.S.W. and S.A., or
N.S.W. and Victoria could form a solid majority.
(2) The Senate is to consist of an equal number of repre-
sentatives from each province, so there S.A., Tasmania
and Westralia, with less than one-fourth the population,
could form a solid majority. The rights of neither of
these Houses are to be infringed.
(3) In case of hopeless disagreement, it is proposed that
the Lower House should bo dissolved. But, as it would
probably come back in the same mind as before, this
would leave things as they were.
(4) It is also proposed that both Houses should be dis-
solved. But, if the nutter at issue was one where the
three small provinces, representing the majority of
States, were mainly on one side, and two large ones,
representing the majority of population, were mainly on
the other, they would probably both come back in the
same mind, leaving the deadlock just as it was.
(5) It is proposed to have a referendum in which the
majority of votes would decide. In this way two States
could easily vote down three. But in this case every-
thing would be ultimately decided by' N.S. Wales and
Victoria, or by N.S. Wales and S.A. if they chose to com-
bine. The object of having equal representation in the
Senate is to prevent the small states being practically
annexed and obliterated. A referendum where the
majority settled everything would make the big States
and the Lower House absolute, and the Senate might as
well not exist.
(6) It is also proposed that there should be a referen-
dum where the majority of the States and the majority
of the people must vote in the affirmative before any-
thing can be done. In this way 380,000 people could
keep things as they were, despite the demands of nearly
three millions for reform. So far as concerns the kind of
deadlock which it is most desired to guard against, this
would settle nothing. If the Lower House voted one
way because the majority of the people thought that way,
and the Senate voted the other way because the majority
of the States thought the other way, it would be impos-
sible to get a majority both of the people and the States.
Therefore the deadlock would go right on. This referen-
dum is useless anyhow, for if the majority both of people
and States was of one opinion there would be no serious
deadlock. It would only be useful to settle deadlocks
when there weren’t any.
(7) Either way, if the referendum was the sole means of
settling disputes the advantage would be with the
Senate. To take a vote of all Australia would cost pro-
bably £50,000. The Lower House has to find the money.
If the referendum suggested in paragraph 5 was adopted
it would mean that in the case of disputes between the
large and small States the majority would have to pay
£50,000 every time to carry its point. If the referendum
proposed by Premier Turner and described in paragraph
6 was adopted, it would have to pay the £50,000 without
carrying its point. If the referendum decided anything
it might be worth the £50,000 in great and urgent cases ;
but some quicker and cheaper device is wanted for
everyday use.
The question at issue, therefore, boils down to
this : Supposing the Lower House demands
one thing and the Senate demands the direct
opposite, it is desired to settle the question
without infringing the sacred rights of the
majority of the people as represented by the
former, or the equally sacred rights of the
States as represented by the latter. And
it obviously can’t be done. When there
are two women and only one baby, and Solo-
mon has to let each woman have the whole
infant all to herself without in any way inter-
fering with the equally sacred right of the other
woman to have the infant all to herself, it is
obvious that Solomon is in a dreadfully tight
place. And it is equally plain that Solomon
has no way out. It is because of these difficul-
ties that The Bulletin sticks to its idea of
letting the two Houses decide deadlocks by
sitting and voting as one. The trouble is that
the matter can’t be referred solely to the con-
stituency of the Senate without infringing the
rights of the majority of the people, or solely
to the constituency of the other House without
infringing the rights of the majority of the
States. Therefore the best way to strike an
average seems to be to lump the representatives
of the two constituencies together and have a
little of each.
A Song from Italy.
The Italians of Sydney met together the other
day to banquet their Consul-General, and every-
body of any importance from the land of the
olive and the organ-grimler was present and
plugging in manfully at the victuals. It was
an occasion of much grease, and the Consul-
General spread himself greatly as follows :
The Italian colony of Sydney was one of which he was
proud ; the members of it were industrious and orderly,
and this latter fact could be emphasised by the authori-
ties, who had very seldom to institute criminal prosecu-
tions against members of their community. As a result
of the policy of this colony, he noticed from the statistical
records that the population of N.S. Wales was increasing,
while that of Victoria was stationary. The railways of
N.S. Wales were paying ; those of Victoria were not. The
numbers of vessels'and of travellers arriving in N.S.W.
were increasing, while they were decreasing in Victoria.
The imports and exports of N.S.W. were increasing, while
those of her neighbors were stationary. Finally, all the
principal establishments, whether industrial or com-
mercial, as well as the residences of foreign representa-
tives were gravitating towards Sydney. In his opinion,
the prosperitv of this colony must continue. Many
people were crossing the border from the other colo-
nies in order to escape being called on to pay
duties ranging from 15 per cent, to 50 per cent,
advalorem on imports. His firm belief was that
whether federation of the colonies took place or
not, the other colonies would eventually have to adopt
the policy of N.S.W. There was, however, a problem to
be solved here —that of settlement. This continent was
as large as Europe itself, and contained only 4,000,000 of
population. If it was not to be overrun by colored races,
it would be the duty of the Government to encourage the
immigration of European populations of the proper
quality. One essential was that of an agricultural popula-
tion, and the proper kind of immigrants could be found in
Italy. He looked upon Mr. Reid as a gentleman of breadth
of view ; he looked upon him as the Cavoitr of Australia.
(Cheers.) By the way. he resembled Cavour in person,
and it would be the mission of Mr. Reid to found the
unity of Australia on the same stable basis as that of
Italy—by free interchange and the cutting up of large es-
tates. It was due to the first of these that they now owed
those flourishing industries in Italy which favorably com-
pared with those of other countries.
This about takes the cake for inaccuracy. The
Italian colony in N.S.W. supplied last year
three times as many criminals in proportion
to its numbers as the cornstalk popula-
tion, so the brag about the gentle
manners of the apple and banana man seems
rather uncalled-for. The increase of N.S.W.
population is apparently not owing to the
present policy of the province, for there
has been a smaller increase since that policy
was introduced than for many years previously.
The railways of N.S.W. are not paying, but
show a heavy deficit even now. The number of
arrivals is decreasing, last year being the worst
in this respect since 1892. The movement of
people crossing the border has for some time
shown a tendency to exit from N.S. Wales, not
the other way about. Australia isn’t as large
as Europe by a long way. It doesn’t contain
4,000,000 people—in fact, it is a good way off
that figure yet. And Italy, whose flourishing
condition the Consul talks about so enthusias-
tically, is notoriously, by reason of its war-
debts and its bloated armaments, one of the
poorest and most “busted-up ” lands in Europe.
The Australian is much addicted to getting the
prominent stranger to stand up on his—the
stranger’s—hind-legs and ofate to him, and he
lionises the stranger a good deal and believes
what that dreadfully ignorant person tells him.
Consul-General Corte is a fine, oily sample of
that kind of stranger.

The Withdrawal of Capital.
Melbourne Age item :
Leading financial agents in Melbourne are beginning
to speak in serious terms of what they describe as a
general failing off in their transactions on behalf of Eng-
lish investors. It is stated that ordinary loans upon first-
class securities have now practically become unprofitable
through the combined effects of the “double income tax”
(i.e., the Bd. tax imposed in England upon income alread3’’
taxed to the extent of Is. 4d. in the pound in Victoria)
and the reduction in the rates of interest. The several
charges and taxes are said to absorb one-tenth of the
earnings upon investments, with the result that, as mort-
gages become due, .the capital is, in most cases, being
promptly recalled. Some firms are disinclined to disclose
the total of the withdrawals by their clients, preferring to
speak generally of them as amounting to “hundreds of
thousands,” while others make definite statements. For
example, Messrs. Madleson, England and Stewart give
the aggregate of their actual remittances of capital
to English investors since January, 1896, as £350,000, and
the amount withdrawn and under not'ce of withdrawal
through Messrs. Moule and Seddon fs some £200,000,
or possibly a little more. A third firm not a
legal, but a purely financial one in this case —has paid
back £112,000 to two clients within the last few months,
and states that four out of every five of the English capi-
talists with whom it does business withdraw their money
when the mortgages upon which it has been lent fall due.
Messrs. Blake and Rigoall mention several large with-
drawals by clients of theirs, and Messrs. Gibbs, Bright
and Co. state that, though no money has yet been with-
drawn through them, the double income-tax difficulty is
an undeniable impediment to further loaning of English
capital. This latter firm has not made a new investment
of English capital in the colony this year. Other firms
complain that their business is being cut into by the
Credit Foncier system.
This is the best item of news that Victoria has
produced for quite a long time. The “ with-
drawal of British capital ” —which is said to
amount to <£2,000,000 within the last twelve
months means that the province owes so
much less and has to pay at least £lOO,OOO a
year less tax to J. Bull. The object of the
Government in establishing the State Land
Bank was to supply cheap money to a com-
munity which has long been suffering under
the burden of exorbitant interest ; the fact that
money has become so cheap that the Bull mort-
gagee finds his industry becoming unprofitable
is the best possible sign that the State Bank is
doing good work. The lamentation over this
matter seems mostly wasted grief. When a
long-embarrassed citizen at last manages to
refund the 40s. which Isaacs advanced on his
watch, and stops paying interest on the loan,
the general idea is that he is getting into better
circumstances. But when a country manages
to refund part of the money which. Bull ad-
vanced on it, and stops paying interest thereon,
the process is called the “ withdrawal of capi-
tal,” and is spoken of in tones of great woe as
an omen of something dreadful and ruinous and
morally combustible to come.
A Voice from Papua.
Extracts from a private letter (dated 17th
July) that has just readied the hands of a Bul-
letin man in Brisbane :
Eighteen months both on the mainland and Woodlark
Island. I write you so that you can form a notion of
what things are like at the present, and help to stop any
friends from going there if you wish.
At present, the population of the island is about 190, at
the very outside, scattered over Saloga, Woni, Wakados,
Kuravacoon, M'Kenzie’s, Riley’s, Dier’s, Yankee, Cole-
man’s, Elliot’s, Bain's and Wyakim gullies. The quantity
of gold being got is very small now compared with that
obtained 12 months ago. Some are making wages, whilst
others are only earning tucKer and a few weights over.
The health of those on the island is not the best.
About one-half are more or less sick, chiefly composed of
southern men. My experience is that it wants North
Queenslanders— fresh from the North—to stand against
the fever and dysentery that attack them after they
reach the island. Even the Northerners are not proof
against fever, but still their attacks, contrasted with
those of Southerners, are as a fleabite compared with an
alligator’s crunch. For any but old hands to come here
now is foolishness ; there is nothing to come for.
Yet I will not say that the place has duffered out. I
believe that there will he small rich dabs found in many
places on the island, in the sago and other swamps. At
the head of Coleman’s Gully a leader has been opened by
O'Connor and party, the stone from which is exceptionally
rich. They have not done much work on it up to the
present, having only sunk about 24ft., and driven a small
tunnel about 70ft. into the shaft. The leader averages
from 4in. to 12in. in thickness and, I believe, the four
partners can dolly from 2oz. to 4oz. per man per week
from the stone already raised.
Anyone with a couple of thousand pounds, who
would care to speculate the same in trenching the hill
from which Coleman’s, Elliot's, and Yankee gullies head,
and from which exceptionally rich specimens have been
got, would, no doubt, receive a handsome dividend if he
but found one leafier that would give him 5 tons of stone
one-half as rich as some of the specimens that have been
got in those gullies. The majority of the men working in
the gullies are depending solely on the specimens in the
creeks, there being very little alluvial.
There is plenty room on the island yet for prospecting,
if the weather would hold up. From January to the
latter end of June this year I have not known of more
than 10 consecutive days of fine weather. It rains on an
average about three days out of every seven.
Now for the mainland. I left Woodlark last August
(’96) and arrived on the Mambare on Ist September, after
recruiting 24 boys. I reached M'Laughlin’s Creek on the
14th of the same month. After stopping about seven
weeks, owing to the heavy wet and the flooded state of
the river I returned to the coast, suffering from dysentery
and fever—the latter very slightly. After paying-off
boys, and keeping them until a boat came to take us
away, I returned to Woodlark.
I expect you have read very conflicting reports in the
papers as to what has been and is being done. That gold
exists in the interior there is no use in denying, but proof
as to whether it is or is not payably obtainable will never
be forthcoming until New Guinea has a change of Govern-
ment. The present Government is a millstone round the
neck of New Guinea. Miners are not the class of settler
Macgregor wants in the country, and in no way has he
tried to assist them to develop the riches of that island.
Up to the present moment he has not made the slightest
effort to cut tracks to the already-known existing golden
creeks, so that horses and mules can travel and carry
stores. Protection to miners is pretended to be given ;
but, in reality, it is the nigger who is being pro-
tected—both the savage and the semi civilised nigger.
Threats of punishment are held over the heads of
pioneers who choose to go inland. Thej r are informed
that, if they use their firearms before a spear is
actually thrown, they will be tried for their lives. Your
carriers can desert you, and there is not. the least effort
made to bring them back ; they can steal your rations
and whatever trade they can lay hands on, and you will
have to travel 40 or 60 miles, and take the culprits with
you, to prosecute them, but you cannot get any compen-
sation by deductions from their earnings for what they
have stolen or destroyed on you. And woe to the miner
who dares to take upon himself the responsibility of
punishing his carriers for theft, if those carriers—and
they are wide-awake enough to the advantage—lay a com-
plaint against him.
The first thing that Macgregor did when he crossed the
range (Mt. Seratohley) last year was to issue an Ordinance
prohibiting' the employment of native labor until the
month of April. Following this was a tax of £1 on every
firearm you carried. Is that what the three Governments
that subsidise the Papuan Government intended? Do
they think this sort of thing is assisting to open up the
country? As an excuse for his first Ordinance, Mac-
Gregor put forward the plea that the natives had to put
up with too much hardship. If he had put this restric-
tion on himself the excuse might have been colorable, as
the natives that he obtained from us on the river were
the ones that really suffered. He lost proportionately
five times as many lives on that trip as any of the miners
lost. His carriers were the worst-fed of any, as could be
proved if an enquiry was held among those natives. His
niggers were the most heavily-packed of all.
I hear that Macgregor’s excuse for the £1 firearm-tax
was that it was imposed for the protection of native birds
from wholesale destruction. Yet he employs a bug-and-
bird hunter out on the Mt. Scratchley range, with about
half-a-dozen guns. Macgregor cannot get labor to cut
tracks, but how easily can he get it to carry rations for
his bug-hunter ! When going through Port Moresby I
enquired from Mr. Allen where was Julieneat, the
naturalist, and was told that this bug-hunter had just
reached the foot of Mt. Scratchley with SO police hoys and
160 carriers, and was on his way to the top.
Why doesn’t Gov. Macgregor utilise his prison labor in
cutting a track from the mouth of the river to the pre-
sent working, instead of hiring out that labor toa trading
company on Sud Est to cut a road to the co.’s reef ? Not
50 yards of a track has been cut even for those on Wood-
lark, which has contributed the principal increase in Cus-
toms duties to the New Guinea revenue during the last
two years. If Billy Mac. would only do something
towards opening up roads into the interior after the
pioneers, he would ease the burdens of Queenland by
attracting some of its surplus labor. I could give a heap
of information about all these matters, starting from
Clarke and the two Cooldown parties, who spent their
earnings in trying to develop New Guinea.
There is one spec, in New Guinea worthy of attention—
the rubber industry. At present the gutta-percha is only
obtained by the reckless destruction of the tree and vine,
of which I will write you an account at some future time.
Thebe are some curious possibilities about the
proposed method of fixing up the Federal Legis-
lature. It is suggested that the House of
Representatives shall contain twice as many
members as the Senate, and each State shall
have its share of the membership according to
population, with the proviso that the seven
existing provinces shall never have less than
five members each. If the provinces of great
area—Westralia, Queensland, and S.A. —are
subdivided, however, as they almost certainly
will be some day, there will be trouble. Sup-
pose Westralia splits in three or five, and the
middle fragment is still called Westralia, while
the others assume new names and start out by
themselves. The new provinces will certainly
not come under this proviso, and they will only
be entitled to as many members as their popu-
lation justifies, whether the number be two, or
five, or a dozen. But can anyone tell whether
the bit which is still called Westralia will be
the “ existing province of Westralia ” as defined
by the Constitution, and so be still entitled to
five members, regardless of population ? Does
the mere possession of the name make it the
“ existing province of Westralia ” even though
it is not the province as it existed at the time
the Act was drawn up, or does the “existing
province ” vanish altogether, in which case none
of the fragments will be entitled to the
minimum of five members ? If the right to
a special amount of representation goes with
the name, and, after the division, the name was
retained by some waste region of two men and
a dog and a nigger, in the far north-west, while
the populous region about Perth called itself
something else, then it appears that the least
populous place would have five members while
the more populous one had, perhaps, only
three ? Unless an “ existing province ” is
better defined, there should be a good deal of
healthy ruction about this matter some day or
other.
The Reid Government is making capital out of
the Lucknow strike in a way which clearly
shows that the astute stout gentleman with the
glazed eye is once more personally at the helm.
Witness the remarks of Brown, M.L.A., at
a recent Lucknow meeting :
He compared the attitude of the present Government
with their experience of Governments in the past. At
Newcastle, a former Government had introduced Norden-
feldt guns and the military. In Sydney, seven or eight
years ago, during the maritime strike, the Riot Act had
been read and the military called out. At Broken Hill,
there were whole armies of police, with their paid pimps
and spies. During the shearers’ strike, bands of police
had escorted men through the city and through the coun-
try districts, but time went on, and education had exercised
its influences, and the Government were trying, as far as
they could, to hold the balance fairly. They had gone so far
as to refuse to issue free passes under any pretence to the
western districts, lest some of the applicants might inad-
vertently come to Lucknow. He was in one of the
Government departments the other day when several
men, who appeared to be Domain loafers, asked for passes
to Lucknow, and were refused by the official in charge,
when they threatened to report him to the Minister. The
official said to them, “ You are the meanest set of
scoundrels I ever clapped eyes on. You seek to get the
advantages conferred by the community for the purpose
of taking the bread out of the mouth of starving wives
and families.”
In a way of its own, the Reid Government is
holding the balance fairly enough, First it
earns the solid gratitude of the Lucknow co.
by still not issuing that famous lease, so that
the mine is not put under the legal labor con-
ditions and compelled to keep some 200 men
employed. In the absence of the lease the co.
need not employ any men at all, and this en-
ables it to starve out the miners at its leisure.
Then the Government earns the gratitude of
the miners by not sending up to the company
sundry “ Domain loafers ” who would probably
be no earthly good as miners if they were sent
up, and who aren’t much needed even if they
were miners, because in the absence of the lease
the co. has no very urgent need for anybody.
In this way Reid keeps on good terms with
both parties, and gain.s some additional credit
in between times by offering to arbitrate if
both parties are agreeable. Of course both
parties are not agreeable ; that is just what the
strike is about.
4.
A new “ non-sectarian ” benevolent society was
established by a number of sectarian gentlemen
at Richmond, Melb., one evening last week,
and the meeting, consisting of Anglicans,
Catholics, Presbyterians, Jews, and others, long
discussed the burning question—should the
meetings of the society be opened with prayer?
All thought that meetings should be opened
with prayer, and plenty of it, but each wanted
his own brand of prayer, no other being
genuine, and the meeting failed to knock up
any kind of petition to the Most High that
would be acceptable to all parties. The tastes
of the Most High were not considered. In the
end it was decided that meetings must be
opened without prayer, the individual saint
being satisfied that no prayer at all would be
more acceptable to the Deity than a prayer
that was not constructed according to the
dogma of his particular creed, and which did
not contain a curse levelled at the dogmas of
all the other saints. The Bulletin respect-
fully suggests a reconsideration of the matter,
that meetings may be opened with prayer, all
the saints praying at the same time, each his
own particular supplication, and each being left
to eclipse the other, if he can, by the length,
depth, strength, and volume of his prayer.
This paper has long wondered that public pray-
ing competitions have not been inaugurated by
the churches.
At a recent meeting of Melbourne Trades Hall
Council the following illogical wail was put up
by some person who is still at large :
The workers themselves are the worst sweaters. The
workers themselves are the curse of the whole com-
munity. Their wives buy the stuff that comps from the
sweaters’ dens.
This sounds like a serious indictment, but, after
all, what is there in it ? The workers are
sweated and underpaid, therefore they have to
buy cheap goods—probably made by people
who are sweated and underpaid —to make ends
meet. That is one item. Another is that,
while goods made at sweated rates in Aus-
tralia, and imported at sweated rates from
abroad, are sold so plentifully, no one can
tell with absolute certainty wdiat goods are
or are not the product of sweating. If
goods are very cheap they probably do come
from the sweaters’ den. If they are dear
it may be because the makers are
paid a decent wage, or because the seller
grabs for a larger profit—he may be an
even worse sweater in some cases than the
cheap man, for the retail price is not neces-
sarily any index at all to the wages paid. If
the working-man’s wife bought goods only at
places where the prices were high enough to
allow of a living wage being paid, that fact
alone would make it very little more certain
that the living wage would be paid. Anyhow,
the first and hardest kick should be directed,
not at the working-man’s wife, who mostly buys
cheap goods because she has to, but at the
other fellow’s wife, who buys cheap goods from
choice alone.
“ Shark ” to The Bulletin :
Judge Simpson, a fortnight ago, laid down a new prin-
ciple in N.S.W. Divorce Court—a principle which, if acted
up to, will have the effect of closing the doors of that
Court against any but well-to-do suitors. In an applica-
tion by a wife for an order directing her husband to pay
a sum of money into Court to enable her to defend her-
self in a suit brought by him against her, the Judge prac-
tically refused to listen to any evidence as to the husband’s
means. “ I am going to lay down a principle which will,
in the future, govern my actions in applications of this
sort. I have to, and will, only consider what sum is
necessary for a wife’s defence.” It was pointed out to
His Honor that to act thus would be to alter what had
been the practice of the Court for fifteen years. “I am
laying down the principle which I will follow,” was the
reply, “and in future a husband bringing a divorce-suit
against his wife must consider the cost of his act.” If
Judge Simpson acts up to his present notion few men will
be able to apply to his court for redress. It is a statutory
rule which makes a husband find means for his wife to
defend herself; but common-sense demands that he
should only have to contribute according to his means.
Surely the husband should only be compelled to provide
his wife with funds sufficient to put her in as good a
financial position to defend herself as he is in to prose-
cute his suit. Acting on his new rule, Judge Simpson
would order Hodge to pay as much into court for his
wife’s defence as he would order, say, a member of the
Ministry.

The recent commission of inquiry into the con-
dition of the Fijians has had the courage can-
didly to saddle “Christian civilisation” with
the moral and physical ruin of the race.
“ Paganism prevented sexual immorality,” and,
as Christianity only forbids it, the result is so
much human nature that “the chastity which
was~ formerly almost invariable in a bride is
now the rare exception.” The physical dete-
rioration of the people also is attributed, partly
to a filthy habit of living which Christianity
doubtless reprehends, but which benighted
paganism punished as a crime ; and partly to
the harder lot of the woman undefthe “ family
life ” which the gospellers have worked so hard'
to introduce. Polygamy made it compulsory
that an infant-rearing wife should receive the
utmost attention from the other wives ; the
single spouse’s maternal duties are so seriously
interfered with that the infant-mortality rate
is now all but half of the birth-rate. The
whole miserable business resolves itself into
one of two alternatives. Either Christianity—-
when judged, like other things, by results—is a
howling failure, or the Christianity of our sable
brother is the merest humbug, and the whole
mission business an impudent fraud.

Thomas A. Edison, the great inventor, has
just produced the “ HOME ” phonograph, which,
as the name implies, is intended to supply the de-
mand for an instrument for private use having all
the vital points of the expensive exhibition phono-
graph combined with a low price; This new
“ talking machine,” costing only =£l2, will soon be
found in every house in the country. The manager
of the Edison Electric Parlor (162 Pitt-st.. Sydney)
reports a strong demand.*
“ The action of Glassey, leader of Q. Par-
liamentary Labor-party, in not Voting against the
Government anent Stephens’ charges of corrup-
tion,” writes “ A Democratic Voter ” to The Bul-
letin, “ has caused intense indignation amongst
his Bundaberg constituents. Everybody who has
hot some financial connection with the present
Govt, believes sincerely that the appointment of
the Royal Commissions did partake of the nature
of bribery and corruption. Even the Coukiee
says the appointments were ‘diplomatic.’ If
Glassey had to face his Bundaberg electors to-
morrow it is highly probable that the result of a
ballot would give him a severe shock. The chief
planks in his election platform related to the
smellfulness of the Q.N. Bank and the corrupt-
ness of the Nelson Administration. Both these
questions have been raised in the Assembly, and
on each occasion Glassey has swallowed his alleged
principles.”
