Alfred Dampier’s re-appearance at Sydney
Lyceum was a truly great event. The manage-
ment started to stem the torrent at half-past 7
and to refuse further admittance, and by a quarter
to 8 the tide of battle had carried it off its feet
and swept it backwards up the stairs into oblivion.
Now and then George Buller’s top hat appeared
above the storm, like a glimpse of someone swim-
ming in evening-dress in a heavy sea, but in a
general way the crowd had the best of it. There
never was such an audience in the Lyceum since
it first existed, and the reception that was thrown
at Dampier nearly blew that grand old hero off
the stage. It was a great, gaudy, happy night
for Maginnis and O’Hara and Warrigal and
everybody concerned. Evidently there is money
in running a show with much horse in it,
even at prices ranging from 2s. down to
sixpence. “ Robbery Under Arms ” went with a
cheerful bang all the time. The old drama has
been adorned with new scenery, and more horse
than usual has been inserted in it, and generally
no effort has been spared to keep it well up to
date.
• * • *
Dampier himself looks no older than he
did 10 years ago, and his wonderful voice is as
perfect as ever. Captain Starlight goes as success-
fully as usual, and all through his career that
pieasan Q moral bushranger has given every satis-
faction. For the. rest of the co. O’Hara, as repre-
sented by George Buller, Alfred Rolfe, Miss Lily
Dampier, Miss Russell, and Howard Smith’s War-
rigal were obviously the principal favorites, though
most of the bushrangers, cattle-stealers, aboriginals,
and other malefactors take well. The rest of the
cast includes Whistling Bill, Billy, and Arizona
Bill; also there are Ben, Jim, Dick, Dan, Black
Jack, and Lucky Jack. Some of them are not
especially brilliant, in an artistic sense, but they
all plug through their parts in an affable fashion —
all except George, who is a long, hesitating blot of
woe, and calls for severe censure.
* * * *
The Dampier season lasts till the end of
next week, and on Friday (Oct. 1) it is proposed
to organise a benefit to Miss Lily, prior to her de-
parture for England. As she hasn’t had anything
of the kind for 10 years, the occasion seems to call
for it, and a lot of influential assistance is forth-
coming to make it a big success.
** * *
The veriscope, brought here by an enter-
fjrising Amurrikin gentleman who arrived through
ns nose a few days ago, should coin money at
Sydney Royal. The veriscope is the cinemato-
graphe drawn out long. It lasts about an hour
and a quarter, and as its solitary subject is the
Corbett-Fitzsimmons fight it is an hour of gory
carnage and soul-filling biff and raw meat. There
is almost certainly no other subject on earth that
would attract so much money in Australia just
now, and the Amurrikin gentleman who runs the
business should find it a small gold-mine. The
veriscope is reasonably successful artistically. The
pictures are very large, and if they do wobble
a good deal at times that is jjrobably un-
avoidable. Anyhow, the fight is all there, and for
14 rounds the two ghostly combatants welt each
other in silence, except when the audience does
the yelling, until at last Corbett goes down with
both hands full of anguished stomach, and holds
the place where he was spanked last till his king-
dom departs from him. There is a painfully historic
look on his face as he struggles to rise—the sort of
look which Bajazet the Thunderbolt might
have worn as he held his stomach in
Asia and saw Tamerlane annex his empire.
But up till that last moment it seems
like anybody’s battle, and the two phantom
pluggers plug each other without making a sound
like lost souls blackening each other’s eye on the
plains of Acheron. If the management would
hang up a piece of beef somewhere, and smite it
with a bat every time a hit is made, it might make
things more realistic, always provided the beef
was smote at the right moment. But the blood is
all there, and the split ear, and the damaged nose,
and the audience gets just as frantic as if it didn’t
know how the matter was going to end. One
bookmaker, on Saturday, so far lost his head as to
offer to back Corbett at a certain thrilling moment,
but nobody had time to take him up. Australia
hasn’t seen its Fitzsimmons in the bruised flesh
and the gore for quite a long time now, and it
noted on Saturday night that he looked gaunter
and balder, and more hairy about the arms, and
more like a- shapeless streak of fury than ever.
As for Corbett, he looked too beautiful and too
b ccurately hair-parted for the business—until he
sat down suddenly with his digestion on his
hands, for then he looked not at all beautiful and
his hair-parting went anyhow. The veriscope
has at last filled The Bulletin with the Christian
sentiment that it has much to be thankful for and
that its lines have been cast in pleasant places.
At no period until the blessed reign of Queen Vic-
toria was it possible to see a brawny pug suddenly
sit down 5000 miles away with both paws on his
stricken innards, and the spectacle makes this
paper realise, for the first time, the advantages of
living under the benign sway of that truly august
monarch.
The “French Maid,” at Her Majesty’s,
shows no falling-off in the boom, and no news of
any change of programme rises in the east so far
as this paper is aware.
# * * *
The Tivoli is gathering in the ripe shekels
much as usual —nothing seems to make very much
difference to the Tivoli, somehow—but a certain
tailing-off becomes visible at the Palace. With
Her Majesty’s continuously doing well, and the
Dumpier boom at the Lyceum, and the huge
attraction of two famous pugs pounding each
other’s shadow at the Royal, something has to go
somewhere, and it seems as if the unlucky Palace
was suffering. The beautiful Biograph pictures
are undoubtedly the strongest attraction there,
and the Biograph, as a peaceful show, naturally
suffers by comparison with a very similar exhibi-
tion which mauls somebody for 75" minutes, and
leaves him draining international nose-blood on to
the floor of the diagram.
* * * *
The Tivoli now includes in its programme
a series of very tine Jubilee pictures shown per
medium of the inevitable cinematographe, and for
those who are not so unspeakably tired of the
whole business that they Would gladly drop the
Jubilee into the bottomless pit this exhibition is
worth seeing. All the same, this paper desires to
mention that—what with the only original cine-
matographe, and the improved ditto, and the
only authentic ditto, and the vitascope, and the
veriscope, and the biograph—it is just possible to
have too much of a good thing. Having got
which remark off its chest The BULLETIN goes out
for a well-earned drink.
* * * *
Prof. Norton B. Smith, the “ Emperor of
all Horse Educators,” starts his little show at
Sydney Exhibition Building on Oct. 2. All per-
sons owning buck-jumpers, pig-jumpers, kickers,
biters, jibbers, bolters, and other undesirable
animals are invited to send them along, to be
treated absolutely gratis, and reduced to such a
state of calm that a local preacher would be a
howling dervish by comparison.
* * * *
Pinero’s “ Sweet Lavender,” which never
grows less in Australian estimation, cropped up
again at Melb. Bijou on Saturday, when the latter
end of the rush had to be turned seatlessly away.
Comparison of this show with previous perform-
ances, that were each of them better in spots, will
chiefly tend to strengthen everybody’s good
opinion of the play. “Sweet Lavender ” is a
self-acting play, and as long as it is cast with
proper respect for appearances, and the author’s
instructions are carefully attended to, th'e effect is
bound to be pleasing. Some members of this
east are not equal to their responsibilities,
but they move in the right direction and
allow the author’s intentions to make themselves
felt. Minnie Cilfillian and her ’Murkan pursuer
are about the least satisfactory items in the ac-
count ; also Clem, and Lavender, and Lavy’s
mother do many of the natural things they ought
to do in a distinctly artificial manner. It is the
vivid reality of the characters that gives the un-
convincing mummers away, and still the true effect
can be seen shining through their perform-
ance. The story never loses its charm. Lavy’s
illegitimate papa, and Minnie’s mother (the
historical female who bewails Min’s careless-
ness in nearly getting run over by a ’bus) are
at least free from staginess, and Thornton plays
Dick Phenyl with so much more force than of
yore that he atones for a lot of the failings else-'
where. His lapses into Rev. Spalding’s vocal
tricks are very few, and he mostly lives, moves,
and has his being in the loveable character of
Phenyl aforesaid. Following on the heels of the
“Bookmaker,” the broken-down barrister-at-law is
a quite remarkable exhibition of Thornton’s talent
for character-acting, and it deserves all the grist it
will bring to the mill. After “ Sweet Lavender ”
will come a brief revival of “ Miss Brown.”
* * * *
The Paulton season is coming to a spirit-
less end in Melbourne, and the Firm has probably
registered a vow to have no more of farcical
comedy under the Princess’s sliding roof. It has
been more or less of a failure there any time
within the past ten years. The theatre is too
large, and its reputation and prices are too high
for a thin entertainment of the early B. and B.
style, and a mere comedy of errors, with no human
interest or music and dazzle in it seems particu-
larly thin when the two leading members of the
co. are not public favorites. “Niobe” showed
Paulton and Miss Alma Stanley to advantage, but
their revival of that excellent old joke didn’t
attract much audience, after all, for people as a
rule had turned their backs upon the present
course of things at the Princess’s. “In a
Locket,” produced on Saturday, was another light
comedy, very much in need of further rehearsal.
Written by Harry Paulton and his brother, “In
a Locket ” is rather cleverly contrived, dainty and
humorous above the average, yet not strong
enough to draw big houses nowadays. The central
figure is an unremarkable middle-aged husband,
played by Paulton in his usual droll-prosaic
fashion, and the other characters revolve around
him. It happened on Saturday that Miss Stanley
took ill, and Miss Emily Hughes had to fill the
vacancy at a few hours notice, consequently Miss
H. couldn’t do more than struggle through her
part with credit. A tame, uncertain first per-
formance suggested that “ In a Locket ” would go
pretty briskly under favorable circs.—and that’s
all about it. The climax is feeble, anyway.
Paulton and Co. will most likely wind up the
season this week with a revival of “My Friend
from India.”
* * * *
Last week’s Bulletin spoke of Miss Alma
Stanley’s Niobe as a “ hugesome ” vision, whereas
hugsom.fi was intended by the writer. But, on
second thoughts, the printer’s error seems good
enough. The lady’s rather gigantic stature makes
either term admissible.
** * *
Melbourne Alexandra is making a fair bid
for patronage with “ Held by the Enemy,” and
the state of the auditorium on Saturday was en-
couraging. A first-class drama, mounted and
acted for almost as much as it would be worth at
a popular house, is a new experiment at an un-
lucky pitch, where admission to the stalls costs
one shilling. This journal has already observed
that the latest Alexandra management deserves
to get on. “Held by the Enemy” justifies a
loud reiteration of the same solemn truth.
** * *
Quite a capable acting force is gathered at
Melb. Alexandra just now—Hus Glover, young
George Majeroni, Edwin Lester, Wilson Forbes,
Frank Norton, ancient Harry Daniels, Rosa Con-
roy, Ada Lawrence, and others who can speak a
few lines respectably. But there seems to be no
hope for the Aleck. The public that would appre-
ciate a better class of show can’t be lured into the
theatre, and the crowd that will support nothing
but blood-and-thunder drama is not sufficiently
numerous and prosperous to keep the “ghost”
walking for long. In Damnier’s palmy time the
Aleck gallery alone used to hold 1200 sixpences on
Saturday nights. .£3O galleries are a dream of the
past.
** * *
“ In the Ranks ” will run for another night
or two at Melb. Royal, and then George Rignold
will proceed towards Sydney with his new leading
lady and the co. The new leading lady—Miss
Lilian Wheeler—is an astounding success, all
things considered. Many tall and graceful
women have been able to walk easily through a
melodrama part after very little tuition in the
business. Miss Wheeler’s talent for taking to the
stage as naturally as the feathered kind of duck
takes to water is not astounding. It is her grow-
ing power of dramatic expression which surprises
one. Miss Wheeler, as Ruth Herrick, sets the
present writer thinking that there is more joy in
Heaven over one gifted novice than over 99
hardened professional actresses who need no en-
couragement to act—because they ought not to
have been allowed to enter the profession in the
first instance.

A Bulletin correspondent: “ Yes, it is indeed sad to
notp that circus-tnan Harry Burton is on his last financial
legs. He started his National Circus travelling in 1851,
and kept it going over 31) years. His grand principles
were keeping faith with the public and the publican -that
is to say, the lodging-housekeeper with whom he had to
do. Perhaps his most striking experience was taking the
circus up the mountains to Walhalla, where the people
thought it must have dropped from the clouds. Who is
it that is taking in hand the matter of helping good old
man Burton ? If the Fitzgeralds, Tom and Dan, were
here, they would help in organising a proper benefit,”

As usual, Caleb Porter entered the lists in “ The Sign of
the Cross ” newspaper correspondence in Adelaide, which
was admirably worked up by W. & M.’s manager. Caleb
is the boss hypocrite of the lot. As Nero he nightly con-
signed bushels of fresh Christians to the lions, but in the
newspapers as Caleb Porter he defended Christianity and
ably quoted Scripture.
Two dear old ladies, who had evidently called at the
Sign of the Jug on the way, went to see ‘‘The Sign of the
Cross ”in Adelaide. They had a parcel of meat-pies and
several bottles of beer, and they wept copiously and ate
pies and drank beer during the first act. In the second
they slept soundly until a particularly harrowing scene
awoke one of the pair, and, without warning, she look a
dive, accompanied by three pies, into the front gallery
row. They rescued the lady, but the pies went on and
broke with a loud report in the stalls. For some minutes
the people below delighted the gallery by their frantic
attempts to get the potato out of their hair and rescue
the kidney and onion which had nestled inside their col-
lars, and then matters became normal again.

After Agricultural Show week there was a decided
slump in Melbourne theatre business everywhere except
at the Opera House. These earnivalic rushes are not a
real gain to showmen People who ordinarily go onoe a
Week to the theatre go twice at holiday times. Then, hav
ing over-feasted themselves, they need a three weeks
interval for recovering their normal appetite.

A small, discouraged theatrical co. ohce struck Egef-
ton, Vic., and put. up a moral drama under tryihg circum-
stances. The hall held a fair audience, and a plav cut out
of “ East Lynne,” with only one fetnale character, Was
rattled off, hut early in the second act the patrons had
grown .Weary of the heroine, a loose, shambling female
W’ith big feet and a bass voice. Otice ti'c management
appealed to the audience, with a pathetic aSsertioh that
the heroine Was the only femalte they bad in the Com-
pany, but the audiehce grew more hilarious ahd un-
gallant, and at last the lady, stung beyond healing,
blunged Over the piano orchestra, and charged a largfe
Obstructionist on the front forni, fetching him one on the
point with a vigorous left. The party irt question was a
man Of feW prejudices, and he replied in kind, and the
two fought a clever little three-round mill in the gang-
way. After the battle the obstructionist was gentlv led
away by kind friends, the heroine returned to the stage,
and the moral drama was resumed before an appreciative
house, the general opinion being that, although the
heroine's performance might have been a poor effort fora
Woman, it was excellent lor a man—especially a man who
fought so well.

The Terry v. Thornton case is due to come up for hear-
ing sometime towards the end of October, and the plaintiff
seems to be seriously waiting for the event.
In the Queen's Bench Division (Eng.), before Mr. Jus-
tice Hawkins, Charlotte Cooney, a well-known music-hall
singer professionally known as Lottie Collins, sued the
proprietor of Society for a libel contained in a notice of
her performance which imputed to her “ a touch of vul-
garity” in her rendering of a song called “The Little
Widow.” The jury awarded the plaintiff a verdict of
£25. But the verdict was dearly bought, as Miss Collins
is now under a newspaper boycott, the Court’s decision
being regarded as “ the death of all but greasy criticism.”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Since thearticle, “ A Year of Freetrade,” which
appeared in last week's issue, was written, The Bulletin,
by much industrious delving, has found the necessary
data for some kind of a comparison of N.S.W. industries
as they were in 1895 under Protection and in 1396 under
Freetrade. The question at issue is the number of per-
sons employed in factories during each year. Up to the
end of 1895 the official definition of a factory was a place
where things were made with the aid of machinery, or, if
no machinery was used, it was necessary that at least
five hands should be employed before the place could
qualify. Also, tailors’ and dressmakers’ shops were not
included. In 1896 a new system started, under which
these two kinds of establishments were included, and the
minimum number of hands required to make a factory
was reduced to four. Results are somewhat as follows :
Persons employed in 1895 under old method of
computation ... ... ... ... 43,833
Add persons in factories employing only four
hands eaoh (Statistics for 1895, page 624) ... 732
44,565
The result is that, not including tailors and dressmakers,
there were 44,565 persons employed in N.S.W. factories,
in 1895, under the new method of computation. In 1896
the results were these :
Persons employed in 1896 ... ... ... * 49,055
Less 1843 tailors and 1749 dressmakers ... ... 3592
45,463
• * * •
This means that the increase for 1896, so far as
the two returns can be put on the same basis, is 898 em-
ployes. Of course even this is not a matter of certainty.
If the number of tailors and dressmakers employed in 1895
could be ascertained —which it apparently can’t —the
total increase might bo more, or less, or there might be
no increase at all, but a falling-off. The N.S.W. industrial
statistics for 1896 leave things in a woefully uncertain
state.
* * * *
The Victorian Government has now closed the
accounts for the year ending 30th June, and the result is
£12,000 better than the estimate announced a few weeks
ago. If the payment of £250,000 off the old deficit is
reckoned in the expenditure the result is a shortage of
£184,095 ; without it there is a surplus of £6s,9os—that is
to say, the aggregate shortage has decreased by that
amount. Under the new circumstances the results pan
out thus on paper :
Victoria Deficit £184,095
N.S.W. ~ 170,945
This is reckoning, in each case, the amount paid off the
old deficit as part of the expenditure, and Victoria pans
out worst because it paid £250,000 off its deficit, while
N.S.W. paid only 8150,000. Leaving amounts paid off old
shortages out of the question the results are :
N.S.W. Deficit £20,945
Maoriland Surplus 125,000
. Victoria ~ 65,905
Tasmania „ 47,732
Queensland „ 8,886
Westralia ~ 3,298
South Australia „ 2,140
* * « »
For the first quarter of the new financial year
Maoriland realised £42,000 more customs revenue, £l7OO
more from the post-office, and £2300 more from telegraphs
than in the corresponding quarter of 1896. The local
Tory still wails that Socialistic government is driving
Maoriland to insolvency, and that capital is leaving the
country, and a huge deficit is imminent, but good for-
tune keeps steadily by the Seddon Government all the
time.
* * * *
The Modern Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Society (Melb.) is feeling a shade better this year,
as the loss for the twelve months is only £7511. Last year
it was £11,431; in 1895 it was £2630; and in 1894, £13,800;
so the present year is a distinct improvement on last one,
anyhow. Out of the £7511 loss, £6318 was through the
: sale of property at less than its paper value, and the other
£1193 arose through general inability to make ends meet.
The Modern has £610,215 advanced on mortgage and
£345,000 of this is represented by foreclosed property,
197,000 by vacant land, and only £163,000 is live mort-
gages. In October, 1894, it got a reduction of interest to
2| per cent., and even at this low rate it hasn’t once con-
trived to make ends meet. Its alleged 8345,000 worth of
properties in possession barely return 3 percent, gross,
.and after allowing for repairs, etc., they return barely
lYi per cent. Neither they, nor the vacant land, nor the
£78,000 worth of office premises have ever been written
down ; judging by the above figures, however, they want
something of the kind badly.
Still, the prospects of the Modem look rather
better than they did. It has £198,897 capital and
£25,179 reserves still existing on paper, which leaves
a considerable margin for depreciation. The income
(£21,460) though miserably small, is £l7l better than last
year. The loss, as already mentioned, has diminished by
£3900. Within the 12 months £37,000 worth of deposits
and debentures and £lO,OOO of mortgage have been paid
off ; and about £49,000 of property has been got rid of,
which transactions reduce the interest a little, and par-
tially shift the stupendous dead-horse. The property
was mostly judiciously swapped for deposit receipts—still
it was got rid of. At the present rate, the reserve will
stand all losses for three years more, and by that time
something may turn up—or down. The main trouble is
that the reduction of interest is only temporary, and the
Modern is now applying for a new re-construction or an
extension of the old one. If it doesn’t get it, the only
probable result is another little tombstonein the cemetery
of dead banks, and the*rain dropping gently on it from
the cypress tree. Still there seems a fair chance that it
will get it, and, in that case, the Modern should pull
through with a long, hard struggle.
« * * *
The Victorian Permanent Building Society
(Melbourne) got through the year with a loss of £6946, as
compared with losses of £7439, £BlO7 and £15,809 for
three previous years. Despite this reduction on the an-
nual deficit, however, things don’t look good. The great
trouble is the continuous falling away in the gross income
—as shown by last four years’ receipts:
1894 £15,828 1896 £7991
1895 10,788 1897 6392
The difficulty now is that the year’s receipts are £6392
and the expenditure, principally interest on deposits and
municipal taxes on properties in possession, is £13,338.
When the gup is so bad as this the work of filling it up is
very difficult. The Victorian Permanent has never
attempted to write down its properties to present values,
and as £630,000 worth of alleged assets only yield 1 per
cent, per annum gross, or about 10s. per cent, after paying
taxes and repairs, they must be a truly awful mass of
bricks and mortar. If it is a fair thing to assume 3 per
cent, gross interest on the present value, which is low
enough in all conscience, the Vic. Permanent’s assets are
worth about £210,000, and it is utterly insolvent; on a 5
per cent, basis ttiey are worth £126,000, and it is only
good for 6s. Sd. in the £ to its creditors.
Moreover, as the properties seem to be mort-
gaged to the extent of £109,000, it is an open question
whether, in case of immediate realisation, the plain,
ordinary depositor would get anything in the £ at
all. The one big- asset is “ Loans on mortgag-e,
£575,797,” but, judging by the large expenditure
for rates and repairs, it would appear that this
is mostly foreclosed properties which yield nothing or
thereabouts. The society reports that the property
market shows practically no improvement, and as the
Modern Permanent, whose balance-sheet is also just out,
says things are looking up, this probably means that it is
the market for the sort of assets the Victorian has to sell
which is rapidly getting no better. Apparently nothing
but a very large and very sudden revival can save the
Victorian Permanent from going flop.

The Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building
and Investment Association (Sydney), whicn reconstructed
in 1894, is trying to make another re-arrangement. The
Metropolitan M. P.B. I.A. agreed to pay 5 and 6 per cent,
to its creditors, but owing to the steady fading away of
the profits it proposes asking for a reduction to 3 per cent.
This is said to be the largest amount that the institution
with the dreadfully long name can screw out of its assets
at present, and, as the assets are £296,000 and the liabili-
ties to the public on which interest is to be paid only
£151,000, the result seems to be that the Metropolitan
only expeots to realise a little over lJj per cent, on its
alleged funds in the immediate future.

* • * 0
The Queensland National Bank question has
had some more obscurity cast upon it. The Parliamentary
Investigation Committee, which was inspecting the insti-
tution for further losses and horrors and revelations, has
drawn up a report, hut refuses to allow it to be published
until it gets formal Parliamentary protection, lest it should
be sued for defamation and blasted with heavy damages.
This demand is so unusual that people now assume
the report to be a staggering publication. Further, there
is trouble about “ a gentleman who protests against the
publication of his affairs.” It is understood that this
gentleman will raise Sodom and Oomorah if the report
is made public until he has been examined by the com-
mittee, and has bad an opportunity to explain, or explain
away, certain allegations. Also, he is out of Australia
just now, and doesn’t see his way to come back for
some time, so he wants the report hung up indefi-
nitely till he is ready. It is openly stated that this
gentleman is Thomas 41‘Ilwraith, a member of the Minis-
try which is asked to protect the committee against
Thomas M‘llwraith. Therefore the public assumes, rightly
or wrongly, that there are some dreadful things about
M‘llwraith and some very bad information about the
Q.N. Bank in that unpublished report, and that some
Ministerial complications are imminent, and that, the ap-
pearance of the document (this is written while a
motion for its production pends in Parliament)
is still a good way off. Perhaps the strangest item
in the whole affair was the off - hand remark of
M'Donald, M.L.A., that, with a view to hurrying
things up, the Government should pay M'llwraith’s
passage back to Queensland if he had not, sufficient means
to do it himself. What a fall there must be when anyone
talks of the old leviathan, the bank magnate, land-boomer,
gridiron-railway boss, and once alleged millionaire, as a
poor old gentleman who possibly can’t get back to explain
things for the want of the wherewithal to buy his ticket!
* * * *
There is probably not a man in Brisbane who
in his heart doesn’t suspect hanky-panky at the bottom
of the latest manoeuvring in connection with the produc-
tion of that committee’s report on the Q.N. Bank. But
there is nobody game to say out loud just what he sus-
pects.
• * * *
All three Brisbane daily papers united to advo-
cate further delay in re the production of the report of
the committee of investigation into Q.N. Bank matters,
in the interest of “ the gentleman not now in Australia.”
It was a piece of good luck that they had the taking
excuse of “fair play” to account for their phenomenal
leaguing together, and they waved their banner aloft for
all it was worth.

The reduction of Alfred Graving Dock dues has
resulted in increased activity at that erst deserted spot.
Williamstown, in fact, is quite lively. Another argument
for the abolition of all port-dues on shipping. And it
will come in Victoria.

The English shipping co.’s have, through a
decision of the Tasmanian C.J., failed to get out of pay-
ing to Tas. Govt, income-tax, or the £5O a year equivalent,
for ’94 and ’95, on business done in Tasmania. If, instead
of having agents there, they had done business in Mel-
bourne with airents of the shippers, they would not,
apparently, have to pay, though they would have made
as much.

Galvan’s hotel. He was then very weak with fever. —
Chas. Hicks.
Queensland now boasts the deepest bore in the
Southern Hemisphere. Bimerah, near Longreach, has
sunk to a distance of 5976 ft., and challenges Australia to
beat that for a record. In N.Q. you will sometimes find a
bore giving you 1)4 millions of gallons per diem at a
depth of about 1500 ft., and only 10 miles away, a party
will be desperately sinking to 4000 ft. with never a sign of
water. In parts the strata are terribly uneven and
broken.
* * * *
The smart man who proposed to start that
Melb. detective-agency explains that as his experimental
advt. had not resulted in sufficient inducement to start
in Melb., he has decided to commence in a more extended
sphere. The agency, by the way, is understood to have
returned all the 12s. Gd. fees sent to it.
* * * *
Macquarie Harbor (Tas.) bar is to be deepened,
and £'50,000 is wanted to do it with. It is proposed to
build a wall from a point on the southern shore of the
mouth seaward in a N. VV. direction, or thereabouts, pre-
sumably to break the force of the sea, thereby permitting
the waters of the harbor, supplied by the Gordon and
King Rivers, to do the work of scouring unimpeded. This
will be a long and dangerous undertaking, and the
£50,000 will go far short of the distance required to allow
of the scourings being swept clear of traffic. Someone
has proposed to place a dredge on the bar and to start
scooping up the sand ; just as sensible an idea would be to
begin chipping down Mount Wellington and piling it on
top of Hobart.
* * * *
Tas. Government (writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent) has spent hundreds of pounds looking for routes
from Hobart to the West Coast, notwithstanding that the
present Sinda track is as good as they will find. And in
the face of the possible construction of the Great Western
line, it is now proposed to erect a wooden tramway over
the distance. A man is actually starting out to look for
a route. A very wooden scheme.
• * * •
The Great Western Graball Railway syndicate
(Tas.) has started the preliminary survey. By the time the
clearing of the route is through the engines of the Great
Northern, from Waratah to Zeehan, will be rushing ore
down to deep water at Emu Bay; and when the former
crowd has completed its line it will find there isn’t much
left to carry back to Hobart but empties.
* * « *
Quite a number of poor people down South, who
have been “reduced” by the depression in real estate,
bank and building society failures, are having their loins
girded up by speculative Gents One to have a shot at the
well-to-do trustees who haVe—m the light of two recent
legal decisions—improperly invested the funds held in
trust for said poor folk. If the Vic. Full Court’s two re-
cent “big” decisions go for anything, those Gents pro-
mise to strike it rich, whilst such Trustees Co.’s as have
not exceeded their powers of investment will benefit
enormously by the exposure of those who have.
* * * *
At the usual half-yearly row amongst the
directors of the Mutual Store, Ltd. (Melb.), the query
why the late manager Langford resigned was unanswered,
and the questioner referred to Mr. Langford himself for a
reply. A certain director of the Mutual Store who has
invested a large amount of trust funds in that co., accused
Langford of having spread reports so detrimental to the
store that the value of the shares had been depreciated
some thousands of pounds in ’Change value. Langford
not only denied the statement but demanded the name
of the director’s informant; same not being forthcoming,
he gave the director the lie direct, threw up his billet,
and thus opened a much-needed snug berth for an ex-
draper and J.P.
» * * *
The first two steamers built in Glasgow for the
new interprovincial Melbourne shipping co. will be Out
directly. They are said to be years ahead in construction
of anything purely coastal now afloat. Signs are not
wanting that the rival oo’s., whose backbone has hitherto
been coal-carrying, are about to add new vessels to their
fleets. Apropos coal-carrying, The Bulletin is patiently
awaiting the next balance-sheet of a certain shipping co.
whose coal-contracts have much diminished (and will
further diminish in December) during the last six months.
An opportunity will then be afforded of testing the truth
or otherwise of the coal-carrying steamship companies’
oft-repeated statement that N.S.W. coal is always car-
ried to Vic. at a heavy loss.
* * * •
A Vic. official receiver is just now wrestling
with the estate of an “ inspector,” whose declared assets
consist of “ two cages and four canaries.” But, to be
quite characteristic of the great boom city, it ought to be
four cages and two birds.
* * * *
The price of silver seems to have settled down
for the present in the neighborhood of 2s. 2d., but there
is no special reason why it should stay just there. The
authorities (if there are any authorities) on the subject
were just as confidently saying, a few months back, that
the normal price was 2s. Pd.; and Japan, when lately it
introduced gold to its currency, adopted a ratio which
made silver equivalent to 2s. 5J4d. It all goes to prove
that nobody knows. Lead retained its upward tendency
till it passed <£l4 ; but the sharp rise seems to be only
warranted on the assumption that silver will not have
much of a recovery. Further, the continued low price
for silver seems to indicate that the fall wasn’t a ’Murkan
ruse to help the M’Kinley bimetallic commission ; and it
also seems to indicate that that commission isn’t doing
very well. But the facts about it are not easily ascer-
tained. When the three members left Paris for England
it was said they had got so much encouragement that the
French Ambassador was to co-operate with them in Eng-
land ; on the other hand, Ottomar Haupt, a Paris
authority on the whole subject, declares that they had
little or no hope held out by the French Govt.
The only thing that looks at all substantial
which the M’Kinley bimetallic commission has done in
England is to persuade the Bank of England to keep one-
fifth of its reserves in silver—and that is contingent, the
cables say, upon France and America adopting bimetal-
lism. Pretty safe, seeing that the agreement upon a
ratio between gold and silver is well-nigh impossible now,
and seeing also that anything short of universal inter-
national bimetallism must almost inevitably result in a
drain of gold from the bimetallic to the gold jnono-
metallic countries. Yet the Bank of England movement
shows that at least a pretence of dealing with the question
has to be made; and on the strength of it the silver-
market firmed. And as it firmed, the lead-market quite
naturally weakened.
* « * *
There are wheels within wheels. The slump in
silver was partly caused, no doubt, by the closing of the
Indian mints, and it is doing a good deal to set India in a
blaze. The Hindoo keeps most of his little pile in silver
ornaments. The famine forced him to turn these into
money, and when he learned that his gracious Queen had
closed the mints and wasn’t buying any, and that her
action had grievously reduced the' selling value of his
only available asset, he naturally said, in his ignorant,
illogical way, that his Queen was a pig-devil, or some-
thing like that.
Just when they were getting used to the revised
quotation for silver, B.H. Prop, shareholders had a Mon
day morning surprise in the news of another fire, upon
which shares tumbled from 465. to 355. When, two year*
file broke out in block 11 of the same co.’s mine (it
has three blocks—ll, 12, and 13, naming them from South
to North) several most valuable hours were lost in calling
all hands in for rescue work. This time the mistake was
not repeated. Within five minutes of the discovery well-
understood signals commanding the whole settlement
were made, and the “enemy” was sprung at. The
position was worse than on the former occasion ; the fire
was in almost the exact centre of the mine. Within six
hours, however, its exact situition was made out, though
at the cost of three lives, while at least 100 men were
partly suffocated, and were borne to the surface insen-
sible ; and within 48 hours there was reasonable expecta-
tion of early victory.
* * * *
There was no inquest or judicial inquiry of any
sort into the fire which broke out in the block 11 portion
of the B.H. Prop, mine two years ago, notwithstanding
that the interests of thousands of people were involved
and that the origin was more or less mysterious. It is
likely that a different course will be taken this time,
chiefly because of suggestions of incendiarism ; at any
rate, it is freely alleged that a recent labor-organising
visitor made a stupid casual remark in a private conver-
sation that it was a wonder the mines hadn’t been fired
long ago, if what he had been told about the companies’
conduct during the strike was true. Anyway, the story is
being circulated industriously, with the accompanying
injunction to observe that the fire broke out on the very
first occasion when the mine was fairly idle underground
—the first Sunday morning, that is—after the visitor’s
departure, and that the outbreak was in the very heart of
the mine. Of course it is not put forward that the casual
observation was intended as a suggestion—i t was nob
made to the right people for anything of the kind—but
that some exasperated or hysterical idiot has accepted it
as a suggestion.
* * * *
It was written a fortnight or so back that the
manager of the South had been unusually plain-spoken
in his half-yearly report, declaring that exploratory work
had been neglected. In a supplementary report he has
now at greater length explained (for the “ information
and consideration ” of the directors, he says, though he
probably might have more accurately written “satisfac-
tion ”) that his bold words referred only to the past.
Anyway, he now gives “ figures to show that the vigorous
policy of development recommended in the report proper
has been and is being carried out.” And the figures do
show it; though why in the “report proper” it was
necessary to recommend a policy which had not only
been adopted, but, as he now says, pursued for some
months, is beyond an ordinary understanding. At the
bottom of the whole business is the want of harmony be-
tween the Melbourne and Adelaide directors ; the man-
ager’s “ supplementary report” is apparently intended to
show' the superiority of the policy of the Adelaide “ mine
management committee ” over that adopted up to May
by the Melb. board. To the casual observer it would
seem that one report was written for one crowd, and
bother for tother. It isn’t all beer and skittles to manage
the South.

B.H. Junction directors wired to London share-
holders that divs. must be suspended for some time, as
the creep was more serious than had been at first sup-
posed—about as serious, in fact, they might have added,
as Tiijb Bulletin said it was. Which'illustrates once more
the advantage of being a Britishah, seeing that mere
“ colonial ” shareholders, who did all the call-paying graft,
received no such frank intimation.
* * * *
The Lucknow gold-mine trouble is not being
boomed overmuch just now, but the bottom is not
knocked out of it by any means. It is still a healthy-
going concern—as live as at the start, seven weeks ago.
There is little room for doubt as to the ultimate issue. If
manager Lock is the man they crack him up to be, he is
bound to win. With Might versus Bight in the respective
scales, the latter, no matter how solid, must kick the
beam. But Might is not going to win handsdown. From
the jump Supt. Lock has only managed to get in about 50
men with whom to run the mines. Not more than a
dozen of these are real underground miners. Mr. Lock
has said that he could get as much outside labor as he
wanted and just when he wanted it, but that is mostly
talk. You can’t, collect first-class miners by the hundred to
go blacklegging at 7s. a day, when most of them can earn
7s. 6d. “on their heads.” Supt. Lock says he has not
tried yet to introduce outsiders ; but he has had an agent
at Bendigo doing his level best to get men. Advts. have
been appearing at Bendigo for miners at 75., but, so far,
only nine toed the mark for Lucknow, and this despite
the fact that two Bendigo men were among the first
to go in after the strike. Where is the balance
of men to come from ? Two hundred, at the very least,
will be needed very soon if the labor conditions
of the mine are enforced, and there is no cer-
tainty of securing a suspension. If it were applied
for and refused—the soi-disant democratic N.S. W. Govt,
should certainly refuse it under the circumstances—Mr.
Lock would undoubtedly have t,o' back down from a
position for which he has really no justification. His case
is rotten : he grinds the men down by 6d. or Is. a day in
order to punish them for alleged thefts of gold which he
is not able to prove ; then he gives them all “ the grand
bounce,” and makes the unfortunates who take their
place pay for sins in the commission of which they cannot
have had the slightest part.
Of course, if driven into a corner, the superin-
tendent has several high cards to play. His co. owns
practically the whole of the land on which the miners’
houses are built. There are only four small allotments in
Lucknow alienated from the Wentworth people. The
large majority of the tenants have been served with
notices to quit, and although these have not yet been
enforced, they may be made the means for harassing the
miners. It would take a lot to shift some of the men.
Several have for years defied the co. to turn them off, and
their roofs still remain to cover their heads. The search-
ing conditions insisted on by the co. are not enough in
themselves to prevent a termination of the strike if the
standard rate of pay were conceded, Indeed, the miners
would at one time have accepted the proposition which Mr.
Newman, their member, was going to make to manager
Lock had he had the chance, viz., to go back on the new
terms provided a promise were given that if things looked
up in the near future the old rate would be reverted to.
Even now they would agree to anything that did not look
like a humiliating defeat.

SOCIETY
The very last Jubilee item : The inhabi-
tants of a little Tas. township celebrated Her
Majesty’s 60 years by erecting a pump on the
village swamp. Amidst hysterical cheers the
chief magnate in the land gravely worked the
handle to the tune of “God-Save” until with a
sad sob about a quart of dirty water oozed up and
trickled away into the swamp, and the function
closed with Prayer and Buns.
* * * *
More naval paymaster in book-and-cash
trouble. There must be something rotten in the
system that gives these officials so many chances
of unlawfully supplementing a miserable screw.
Dismissal from the service in the latest Sydney
case, and 18 months gaol! That same old British
Property-god evidently bosses the navy. The
salary of naval-paymaster is about
30s. a week.
* * * *
More than one case has recently occurred
in Australasia in which public servants have em-
bezzled money entrusted to them—-without any
apparent reason except the desire to possess
means. In one Sydney case, that of a married
man with a family, he had pocketed many hun-
dreds. Part of this he had expended in a house ;
the remainder had been carefully banked; and
nearly all was recovered.

estimable paper a gentleman or a cur, but it has to be

several cwts. having escaped the order for general
destruction issued at one time by Old Jack
Robertson.

The new N.S.W. Chief Judge in Equity is
going the right way to become a great favorite
with that happy band known as the —haw—
“ Equity Bar.” They are the curled and gilded
darlings of the profession, formed of very different
clay to the members of the “ Common Law Bar,”
whose appearance within the sacred precincts of
the Equity Court they greatly resent, and upon
whom they look down contemptuously. This
attitude of the Equity crowd has always been a
source of amusement to the Common Law brigade;
but a different complexion has been put on the
matter since Judge Manning has taken up
the cudgels on behalf of his pets. Last week a
barrister —not an Equity man—was appearing
before this Judge, who took the opportunity to
remark : “I view with great disfavor the practice
of counsel from other jurisdictions appearing in
this jurisdiction of the court.” In other words,
it would seem that the Chief Judge in Equity
wants to reserve his court strictly for “ Equity
counsel,” and now warns all other barristers to
keep off the grass. Solicitors in future, when
briefing counsel in equity cases, must take care
that they and their clients do not incur the dis-
pleasure of the presiding judge by chosing a
common-law man to represent them.
* * * *
Sydney suburban police-court. A woman
proceeded against her neighbor for calling her a
“ mean reptile.” Magistrate (to prosecutor) :
“ Cannot this matter be settled?” “Not unless
she apologises, your worship. She called me a
reptile.” Defendant’s solicitor: “Well, your
worship, my client is willing to admit that the
plaintiff is not a reptile.” The beak : “ You hear
that, Mrs. X., the defendant admits that you are
not a reptile. Will that satisfy you ? ” “ Oh,
yes, your worship.” Charge withdrawn.

* * * *
There is a movement among N.Q. publi-
cans for the repeal, or at least the modification, of
the Q. Gambling Act of 1895. This completes a
curious historical cycle. The publicans actually
coalesced with the parsons to Put Down gambling.
Each set was guided by similar reasons. The
publicans thought that the persons who patronised
Adams’ sweep were robbing themselves of money
that should have gone in buying good brown
tanglefoot. The parsons attributed the low tide in
church collections largely to the awful Curse of
Adams. So Whitechoker and Bung became con-
spirators banded together in the Cause. The
exile of Adams cost Q. Treasury about =834,000 a
year, and the malevolence of fate would have it
that, when a Treasurer found himself short, the
first article that he proposed to tax was the beer
of the anti-gambling publicans.
* * * •
Q. Bung is not quite nerved yet to make
a desperate effort to pass on the extra 3d.-a-
-gallon beer-tax to the consumer. The brewers
have raised the price, and the Q. United Licensed
Victuallers’ Association has actually passed a
resolution adopting a scale of charges. The writer
saw a leaflet circular sent round among Brisbane
bunghood of the new prices, but speaks here only
from memory. Half-pints of English were to be
4d. ; certain bottled sixpenny beers were to be
raised to 9d. and Is. respectively. But—up to
date of writing, no publican has dared append his
signature to the leaflet circulated to receive signa-
tures, and, in spite of the Association’s “ resolu-
tion,” it seems likely to miscarry.
* * * *
One Brisbane (Q.) publican declares that
he will be a loser to the extent of .£5O a month by
the extra 3d.-a-gallon beer-tax.
* * * *
An exciting episode recently happened at
a city Coffee Palace when a stern-visaged, athletic
person entered the crowded dining-hall in the
middle of a meal, and, strolling up to one of the
tables, lifted a person in clerical costume from his
seat by the ear. He then coolly proceeded to
kick and hustle the gentleman along the aisle,
until he reached the front door, when a mighty
punt sent the fiend-scarer down the steps. The
athletic person was a married man, jealous, and
with a very bad temper.
* * * *
That Sydney hotelkeeper’s reach-out for a
heavy verdict against a certain doctor who,
he claimed, had degraded his pub. by bringing
thereto a woman who was not his (the doctor’s)
wife, realised only a sovereign damages, and was
therefore much less successful than a similar claim
made some time ago by a N.S.W. pleasure-resort
publican. The latter “whacked-in a writ ”on a
gentleman whom he accused of having “carried
on ” upon his premises with a lady of some posi-
tion. The case came to court, but was squared
by defendant, who, without making any admis-
sion, cashed up some hundreds out of considera-
tion for the lady.

A Hobart church-scandal, wherein it was
alleged that a gentle shepherd walked along
suburban lanes with strange females, was settled
in court the other day. The parson sued a lady
for £lOOO damages for scandal. The evidence was
extremely paltry, yet a hard-faced crowd of Primi-
tive Methodist women crowded the court all
through the case and eagerly drank in details of
how the good clergyman guided the footsteps of
his little ewe lambs in pleasant pastures. The
jury found for the plaintiff parson, damages one
farthing. Also, each party pays its own costs.
This verdict, considering that the parson was sus-
pended of his church authority, strikes the lav
mind speechless with admiration for the law and
its wonderful ways. Plaintiff had previously been
EPHEMERAE.
They play, and then they mate. But love is death,
And though they worship dotingly the sun,
They turn away from summer's joyous breath
And choose the night (for love and death are one).
They are the emblem of a mystery,
The incarnated answer to your Why :
Though all is bright to them, and fair, and free,
They mate, to live again ; and mating, die.
Alas, my sweet! the iron law that locks
All Nature into never-changing rules
Has ordered thus. Love is a paradox;
Its triumph is its death. May empty fools
Proclaim the everlasting reign of her
Whom none escapes; I tell you, the caress
That yesterday set all my life astir,
Left nothing but a taste of bitterness.
VON KOTZE.

Sydney : “Halted in front of the Chow
Mission to-day, and, under the screen which
fronts the door, I saw a male boot stealthily
touch a rather neat bit of feminine footwear —the
pressure was returned. Many Bulletin corres-
pondents, by the way, have floundered in search
of reasons why the Chinaman scores so frequently
with his white ‘ sissetah,’ but ’most every medico
knows ’em.”—T.D.
* * * *
It will only cost £3OOO worth of Sunday-
school pennies per year to maintain the 35 “ plitty
lady missionalies ” whom the 75 Chows assembled
to farewell the other day in Melbourne. Among
the missionaries were several trained nurses, who
might have been forced on account of the scarcity
of sickness and poverty in Vie. to pack up their
medicine traps and piety for fresh fields and pas-
tures new.

Melb. : “The sweating at the Salvation
Army Workmen’s Metropole, over chopping scwt.
of wood for a meal which costs about 2d, is one
end of the scale. The other is afforded by the
delicious Government stroke at Pastor Edgar’s
Wesleyan wood-yard. Away with this hollow
humbug, and let us have State Socialism instead
of the religious side-show.”
Parson-run Brisbane Telegraph discour-
seth gravely upon the prevalence of “ certain
evils ” owing to the non-lighting of railway-
tunnels. The Telegraph probably means “ un-
certain evils,” for it is the “ sprightly infusion of
chance ” that helps to make these little matters so
interesting. By the way, a certain N.S. Wales
M.P. makes a speciality of tunnel-stories —only
fit to be told in a tunnel.

* * * *
A Croydon (N.Q.) editor actually calls on
the police to prosecute two undertaker men who
were humping along a coffin on footbaek, because
they laid the shell down outside a pub., went in
and had a drink, called for the bacca-cutter, cut
some weed, and filled and lighted their pipes, and
joked about the weight of the Remains before re-
suming their jog-trot. Croydon must be getting
squeamish.
Edward Marks, of
Melbourne, puts for-
ward a sensible religi-
ous objection to the
marriage of Jews and
Jewesses with “ Chris-
tians of easy faith ”
who join the Syna-
gogue from purely mat-
rimonial motives.
“The evils of indiffer-
ence and callousness
which the Christian
churches are lamenting
are equally rampant
among certain sections
of the Synagogue,’’says
Mr. Marks, “and
what can be the result
■of a commingling of
insincere Christians
with indifferent Jews?’
As a champion of his
ancient Faith the wor-
thy Hebrew is natu-
rally alarmed at its
gradual decay, but he
may at least derive a
little racial satisfaction
from the fact that
whilst all religious
faiths are dying, Juda-
ism dies gamest of all.

vulsions” climbing up
her verandah-posts.

E. Wenck, schoolmaster at Durham (N.Q.),
the other night, rode towards Croydon where his
brother was reported as lying at death’s door in
the hospital. His horse butted him against a tree,
threw him, and got away. Wenck walked four
miles back and got another horse, and then rode
into Croydon, a stranger to its bearings by night.
Seeing alight, he made for it, and came upon an

The Bendigo loyalists, not having raised
enough Jubilee funds to erect the projected local
statue of H.M., have amended their scheme to a
picture to cost £6OO. An idea for Melb. Public
Library Trustees. If they could but unload their
undertaker building a coffin. It was for the
brother he had come to see, and who had died
four hours previously.
* • * *
An astounding scandal, which should cer-
tainly be ventilated in Parliament, has been care-
fully pigeon-holed in a certain Australian State
Treasury. It relates to the passage-money from
England of a lady understood to be related to a
high military official.
• * * *
The Cowra (N.S.W.) baker who suicided
by throwing himself down his well, taking with
him his child (which, strangely enough, escaped
uninjured), was the man who, about four years
ago, went down another well a short distance from
his own, and took therefrom the dead bodies of a
publican’s wife and child, she having suicided.
The long arm of coincidence.

is scattering a number of portraits to ‘ the glory of
God and Gwendoline’ Gwendoline being Mrs.
‘ Commandant ’ Booth. Mrs. Booth is a moon-
faced, weighty lady, who fills her uniform almost
to breaking strain, and in these photographs she
is pictured, with the heavenly smile, buoying up a
shattered sister in a mangled hat and a tattered
skirt, or a ‘pore’ fallen creature with a black

eye is hanging on to her and gazing into her
heavenly smile, or a representative of gilded
vice in fashionable garments is kneeling to the

go with these pictures : ‘My God ! You are
the first woman who has kissed me since

part. The poster will follow, with Mrs. Booth
in seven colors and a halo.” How beautifully
modest it all is !
A fine example of what some witnesses
will swear to, apparently in absolute good faith,
appeared at the Tasmania wreck inquiry. One
testifier, with an open countenance and truth
shining in his eye, swore that the boat he escaped
in was only twelve feet long, and had four thwarts,
so apparently it was an eight-oar boat twelve feet
long. Also he assured the court that they came
away from the ship with no plug in the boat—they
discovered the absence of the plug an hour or two
later through the water beginning to come in,
which was about the slowest thing in the way of
water ever recorded. As a rule, when a largo
crowd goes out in a twelve-foot boat with no plug
in it there is just about time for one wild blanky
before the show goes over and everybody is with
the sharks.

of last year a Chinaman’s cart and b; s’cets are

this rate the nurse-girl’s perambulator will pro-
bably fall in the same category, and Mary Jane
will rejoice accordingly.

* * * *
Narrabri (N.S.W.) Council has, just as
summer is coming on, pruned the ornamental
trees in the borough. Said pruning consists of a
total demolition of every leaf-bearing branch.
Result —bare stumps, shadeless streets, and a
blasphemous public.
» * * *
Briton Riviere’s picture, “ The Dead
Knight,” just bought by the N.S.W. National
Art Gallery trustees, was shown at Hobart Exhi-
bition, and could there have been bought at a
price which would favorably compare with the
sum alleged to have been “parted ” by the trustees.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —You mention a case of a
man getting 15 years for sacrilege. It was a boy
of 16, who broke into the R.C. chapel at Carlton,
Melb., and stole some communion-plate. He was
tried before Judge Quinlan, and sentenced to 17
years’ imprisonment, with hard labor ; but, as it
is more than 17 years ago since it happened, he
must have been liberated long since. Anyhow, it
was a most iniquitous sentence. —Moonee Ponds.
[Can anyone tell The Bulletin how long he did
serve ?]
• * * *
In a Sydney court last week a man with
18 children was hauled up for neglecting to support
five of them. It was alleged that the mother had
taken the five children away with her. Three of
the family attempted to say something on behalf
of the old man, but one was hustled out and the
other two had violent hysterics and had to be re-
moved by the police. Then the house-father was
ordered to be imprisoned until he paid <£l6, his
last words as he was hauled away being that he
was prepared to take the whole eighteen to live
with him. Perhaps being locked up was the less
evil of the two.

A pickpocket operating at Melb. Zoo.,
when chased by the police, rushed to the emu-cage
and threw over the fence a purse he had stolen.
But the emu for once wasn’t hungry, so the corro-
borative evidence was immediately forthcoming
after all. Six months.
THE BRIDEGROOM’S SONG.
(After Gilbert and Sullivan.)
When a merry maiden chances
Marriage with the “ boy” she fancies,
Soon her heart begins to ache :
Love has made a grand mistake.
From that day, until he leaves her,
Conjugal embracing grieves her,
Though she sought the wedded state,
She would fain avoid her fate.
Hubby finds his wife dejected :
Love is not what she expected
Ere she put it to the test.
Single blessedness was best.
Disenchanted, sadder, wiser,
Hubby snaps the tie that ties her.
Now he knows, of course,
Girl is like a horse—
One should try before one buys her.
Filly’s apt to shy—
That’s the reason why
Strephon ought to try before he buys her.
TO THE PLAYWRIGHT.
[FOE THE BULLETIN.]
Heed not the sneering eritic-sage
Your plots deriding ; angel eyes,
Sub-guardians of a greater stage,
Look on with envious surprise.
Still act the love that never is,
Love deathless; love that knows not stint.
Give vice despair; give virtue bliss —
Who knows but God may take the hint?
M.L.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
Penitent, about to swing,
Let us kneel and pray together;
Then a joyful hymn we’ll sing.
Ere, with sturdy bonds of leather,
Hangman binds your limbs in union
You’ll be fit to “ take Communion.”
Penitent, about to swing,
Sure salvation you are seeking ;
See that to the Cross you cling
(In a pious way of speaking).
Bound of yore for pit infernal,
Now you’re booked to Life Eternal,
All through doing what you did—
Murder, followed by repentance.
Swarms of souls on Satan's grid
Envy you your lawful sentence.
Lost are they beyond retrieving :
They lived and died without Believing.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, September 21, 1897.
Mt Dear Myee—
The Convention has been brightened by some
romantio episodes. One was the meteor-like rush
into matrimony of delegate Glynn, of S.A. He,
so goes the story, proposed by letter to the lady,
whom he had only seen once, and with whom on
that occasion he had merely a ten minutes’
chat. But the ten minutes had done the
business, for he was accepted (by wire) and
abruptly went off (by express) and was
married and returned to Macquarie-street and
business all within a few days. The happy pair
are now honeymooning sedately in the fierce light
that beats upon Federal excursions. On the re-
turn journey from Newcastle on Saturday, the
bride was chatting with friends in one car; the
bridegroom, in another, sat calmly reading from a
pocket volume. Another domestic incident is the
birth of a son to a delegate from Westralia, who is
said to contemplate blighting the babe’s future
career by naming him “ Sydney Federal Conven-
tion. Apparently “ Melbourne Adjournment
Deadlock Surplus Referendum ” would be even
more expressive.
A Federal engagement has also been announced.
The girl generally voted “the belle of the Conven-
tion ” has had her affections annexed by a press-
man from the same province as herself, who came
over to specially correspond on the Convention
proceedings.
On Saturday last, when delegates and a dust-
storm struck Newcastle simultaneously, the guests
only comfortably filled' the train without any
crowding, and on reaching the grimy city were
taken on a harbor-trip, fed at the citizens’ expense
and rushed off hastily to see a memorial fountain
initiated. At the banquet neatly-got-up souvenir
menu-cards were distributed. The design was
artistic, but the spelling was very “off”: guests
were offered “Mayonaise,” “entrements,” and
“desert.” At this, the last of the Govt, trips
arranged for the Convention, only one Premier
and few ladies had been snared, and it was the
least rushed of all by outsiders. The city helped
to keep up its hundredth birthday by strewing
ashes on its visitors’ heads. Mayor and Mayoress
Miller and other magnates met the party and
went round with them. After going through the
day’s list of events, the expedition washed its face
and returned to Sydney.
The Mayoress of Newcasw created a sensation
during the centenary corroboree by falling into
the harbor. On Friday night visitors going to a
reception on board one of the men-of-war were
surprised to see the lady step off the gangway and
disappear. She was fished out and dried and the
party proceeded.
For many of last week’s numerous dressy
“swarries” smart gowns were encored by their
wearers whose wardrobe couldn’t keep pace with
the demands for an entire change of raiment
for each diversion. On all race-days at Rand-
wick, tailor-made suits were sprinkled plenti-
fully round; perishable glories of chiffon and
muslin were kept by their owners for garden-
parties and such. Some authentic London clothes
warranted worn at Buckingham Palace in Jubilee
June have been adorning their returned wearers
and paralysing other women with envy. These
dresses are frail, cobwebby creations like all up-
to-date frocks. As usual, to have' an imported
gown was a sign-manual of style this year. Local
trades-folk don’t mourn as much as might be ex-
pected at being passed over in this way by the
fashionable, as the exotic garments have nearly
always to be altered at great expense by a local
frockist, who thus makes her modest haul out of
them, and very often has a workable notion to
copy.
Melb. visitors, outside political jaunters, were
fairly numerous at all the race-meetings at
Randwick this trip. Thursday, the off-day,
attracted many well-known amateur sportswomen
from Victoria : Mrs. Bowes Kelly, blaok-elad ;
Miss Fisken, striped in pink and black ; Miss
Cumming, sister of Lady Clarke the second, in
dark green with cock’s feathers rampant and
exaggerated neck-bow ; Miss Carter, in business-
like and skirt; Miss Buckley, in unremark-
able attire, were all on the stand. The wife of a
noted ringman broke out violently in silk pleat-
ings on her green cloth shoulders, and bore a vivid
hat. Mrs. Harry Rickards fluttered elegantly in
palest mauve silk, and rattled a long chain from
her waist with a metal heart like an overland-
baggage-check attached. This, I believe, is
fashion’s latest remark ; her husband was under-
studying his Tivoli photo. The sensation costume
of the off-day was one of the creations of Melb.’s
“sassiety ” modiste, bright “Victoria” blue silk
veiled in glorified Madras muslin with a tangled-
skein pattern in white, and effervescing at the neck
into multitudinous filmy tucks.
The woman who is determined to “get there”
in sassiety has to swallow much gall and worm-
wood and smile all the time as if she liked the
dietary. Guests saw a crushing vice-regal snub
administered on board the Sunbeam at Lady
Brassey’s .tea-party on Thursday afternoon by the
hostess to a gushing person who rushed up and
broke into conversation between Her Ex. and
another. It is hard, though, for a newcomer
among the Nicest to attain at once that thick-
skinned insensitiveness which enables any well-
seasoned old-stager to take unconcernedly smiles
and snubs, whichever it pleases them to deal out,
from those on loftier pedestals than her own.
At a recent big Australian ball, a very pushing
person coolly marched his partner up the room
and took a position in the vice-regal set, from
which he was promptly “ chucked out ” by one of
the ball officials. The action had little effect on
the cast-iron hide of the amateur swashbuckler,
but the humiliation of the poor girl, who had
regarded him as being all-powerful on account of
his gorgeous caparisons, was pitiful to behold.
Vice-regal and other entertainers on a large
scale have had to protect themselves against the
uninvited prowlers who wedge their way into
every large festival given by pasteboard invitation.
For the first time on record, the cards for last
week’s Govt. House ball had to be produced at the
entrance and examined by a gold-braided func-
tionary ; and authorised persons lurked in the
background ready to pounce on any self-invited
callers.
To carry a woman successfully through life
there is no more valuable motive-power than
“quiet push.” How admiringly this was noted
in the glowing account of one of the Jubilee
Drawing-rooms clipped from a London daily by a
Sydney paper. Some duchesses and countesses,
seeing a better place than theirs in front of
them, took it, got into “ the presence,” and were
off into their carriages while timid stragglers were
waiting hours in the ante-room. During the ball-
season, watch how the cloak-room attendants
hustle when the imperious dames whom they
know well by sight sail into the cloak-room with
a train of daughters—note how wraps are off and
exchanged for tickets or found and handed out
again in less than no time, while poor meek
nonentities wait shivering by the barrier, and
their men folk storm outside in draughty passages.
Who gets a show at the waiting-room mirror if a
pusher is prinking at the glass. Who gets most
attention from bowing shop-walkers ? The woman
“ with a manner ” may only give a trifle on her
large outstanding account when it suits her, but
if she be a woman of the world she can push up to
the counter past poor abashed littl'e Mrs. Nobody
who is buying school frocks for the children, never
goes into sassiety and pays cash for everything.
A steadily purposed woman with an object in
view “gets there ” every time.
The Art Society’s annual evening-party, last
Thursday, consisted chiefly of the same sad-looking
audience trying to see the show on the cheap, that
turns up each year—Percy Spence energising as
host, the pretty girl with the big blue eyes, whose
portraits in many poses adorn the walls, and some
living pictures. The lighting on the models was
well managed, except when they bobbed ; an old
woman, as Rembrandt’s mother, was encored, and
A. Hanson was rigid as a Vandyck ; also, the
artist, disguised as a portrait of a gentleman, well-
acted pictured calm. Among the guests, Miss
Kemp, the original of Mr. Bennett’s picture, diffi-
dently evaded comparison by keeping away from
it. Light food was laid on. I saw an adven-
turous small boy devouring an ice and drinking
scalding tea in alternate mouthfuls, and shuddered
for his digestion.
The “Washington Post” craze has spread to
Govt. House. The dance was jigged there at the
balls last week. It is a merry enough whirl, but
dreadfully severe arm-exercise for a small man
prancing behind a tall partner, and stretching up
to grab agonisedly at her fairy fingers. One gruff
observer criticised it as “the plain woman’s
chance,” because the man-partner gazes at the
lady’s back-hair all the time. Celebrities were
plentiful in the ballroom on both nights. When
the State Lancers were being evoluted, a solid
crowd watched the dancers, and afterwards the
dais-occupants were subjected to some steady
staring. The garden, with its mammoth tent and
•other attractions, reduced the crush in the dancing-
room, but waltzers seemed languid, and only a
few couples were inclined to be energetic through-
out the evening. In the dressing there was a run
on bright rose-velvet trimmings and waistbands.
Some of the newest frocks there —Mrs. Ferguson’s,
Mrs. E. Rundle’s, Mrs. C. Fairfax’s —were thus
adorned. Lady Brassey wore yellow satin and
many flashing trinkets. His Ex. from Brisbane
•could be picked out by the twinkle-little-star on
his shirt-front.
The ben. to Miss Yivianne Dagmar at the Royal
on Friday afternoon was depressed by the large
voids in the back seats ; also the audience became
restive at the end of a longish sing-song. The
charity matinee has lately been worked for all,
and a little more than, it is worth ; and the interest
which was attempted to be inserted into this last
one by running it as an opposition ben. to a highly
successful one of a few years back fell very flat.
Coleman warbled his latest ear-catcher. Peachy,
in an overwhelming frock coat, sang “ Somebody ”
\vith Juliet Wray, who, confectioned in black and
silver, looked much more substantial than of yore.
Shand and Ada Willoughby duetted, and Ada
brought her celebrated smile in working order.
A young lady looking as if she had been made up
with a yard-broom hopped into the programme in
a yellow dress worthy,of a better cause, and sang
ditties of the “domestic affection” brand, which
style of lay has become general of late, and seems
as if the old-fashioned “ hearth and home ” senti-
ment had been resuscitated with a poke-bonnet.
Lilian Tree, who has come back as large as life,
and is nightly warbling at the Palace, manages to
get a comparatively slim effect by the floral trim-
mings on her black gown. She ends up her song
with a kind of pop-gun snap, and exits abruptly
on a high note. Lilian has confided her age to an
interviewer—2s come Xmas—she must have been
well-grown for her age when she left years ago.
And she. couldn’t ;ring-in a newer sensation than
the unknown admirer from the back-blocks with
fervent epistles and offers of all his wordly goods.
Miss Fannie Wentworth’s turn at the same show
is the most talked-about item of the evening ; her
musical burlesques have caught on, -and are as
good as anything male amusers have done in the
same line, always excepting the shining example
of Frank Lincoln, whose French comic-song and
'German ballad hold the record for humor against
all-comers.
The “Society of Dante”—that is not the right
name, but the Italian title is too copious to repro-
duce here—is to be opened to-morrow (Friday)
night in the useful Y.M.C.A. Hall, which sees so
many and such diverse shivoos within its walls.
Sig. Corte, the Melb. Italian Consul, has been in
Sydney lately, and has helped to work up enough
enthusiasm in the mellifluous language of his
native land to found a sister club to the one in
Melbourne. Oscar Meyer, whose.name doesn’t
smack much of “ lingua Toscana,”'is the hon. sec.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Girls who aspire to a cap and apron in Mel-
bourne Hospital are likely to have the edge rubbed
off their enthusiasm by the case of nurse Knight
the other day. _ Miss K. was a probationer, and,
within a fortnight of entering the hospital, she
died from blood-poisoning.
Ex-inmates describe night in Melb. Hospital
as a foretaste of purgatory. Some sufferers gr.oan,
others grumble or blaspheme sotto voce ; the most
inoffensive snore, wheeze, oough, or sigh like
broken-winded bellows. Nevertheless, more girls
than ever take up nursing as a profession,
encouraged, perhaps, by the matrimonial prizes
secured by some of the sisterhood. Besides, even
if a woman in the whole course of her professional
career never encounters a single marriageable
‘case,’ grateful offerings and useful friendships
crop up for the earthiest of ministering angels.
TheJady head of a Melb. private hospital has just
married an opulent Scot, a former patient, years
younger than his bride. No wonder that ‘ Sister
Clare ’ and ‘ Nurse Muriel ’ prefer men invalids,
whom they pamper up to the nines and then extol
as being far more patient and grateful than she-
sufferers. The wife, mother, sister or daughter
has yet to be found who ever had the luck to dis-
cover the smallest pretensions to ‘ patience ’ in an
indisposed male relative.
“ The widow of a former Master of the Melb.
Mint and ex-M.P. for Glasgow is to be married
again as soon as her year of mourning is out. The
lady was 16 when she began married life before,
with a bridegroom of 60, so she is still fairly
young ; though her daughter, who went straight
from her convent-school to the boards, is now
appearing in minor parts on the Melb. stage.
“Dr. Strong, of Australian Church (Melb.),
preaching on Show-week Sunday, quoted Mon-
oure Conway’s definition of a good sheep—‘a
maximum of wool and minimum of leg to jump
over fences with ! ’ The_preacher said that Dean
Stanley was the identical creature in human form.
To look nearer home, Dr. Bevan has masses of
thatch-fleece and brief enough legs. By the way,
the old man, many will be sorry to hear, has come
back to Melb. in an alarming state of health ;
also, fiery little Prof. Rentoul is in trouble with
his eyes—cataracts on both, and no great hopes of
successful operation held out by the doctors. The
Australian Church is losing its brainy assistant-
shepherd, Davies, and can’t afford a successor,
which means double work for the pilot-in-chief.
“ Next month that abode of dullness and piety,
Govt. House, Adelaide, breaks out into quite a
riot of gaiety and worldliness. Among other
entertainments, amateur theatricals are to be
given. Although the vice-regal family strongly
disapproves of anything savoring of the godless
theatre, one of the goody-goody sons has so far
forsaken the paths of piety as to consent to enact
the part of Cecil in Jerome’s “Barbara.” Mrs.
Cornish, of Melb., and her pretty niece and Capt.
Wallington will take the parts of Barbara, Lillie,
and Mr. Finnicurn. Mrs. Cornish, an excellent
amateur actress, is the danghter of the late Judge
Noel, and for some time studied tor the stage with
Mrs. Kendal.
“ The Melb. banking fraternity grows quite gay
and festive again. The city and suburban officers
of the London Bank of A. lately presented one of
their distinguished colleagues with a ‘ handsome
tea and coffee service and other plate’as a wed-
ding present. During the reconstruction crisis,
and for a long retrenchment time afterwards, there
was no marrying in bank circles. Angelina was
either broken off, or told to wait for the restora-
tion of public confidence.
“ At last the Shakespearian Society has begun
to gird itself up tor a performance of 4 Much Ado
About Nothing ’ somewhere about the beginning
of the year. Dr. Neild is organiser, showman,
wire-puller, the Corypheus of the society. The
magnificent grounds of ‘ Royston,’ Malvern, have
been offered by the owner, Mr. Clarke, for an
open-air treatment of the comedy in which Mrs.
Potter ventured to show her—shall we say legs ?
But the pros and the cons of the question are
being deeply pondered by the Shakespearians ere
committing themselves to the breezy arrange-
ment.
“One day in ’94 more than one Vic. society
damsel lamented the sudden departure ’Ome of a
handsome, fashionable and apparently independ-
ent young gentleman. His lettres de conge, posted
in Adelaide, announced 4 the sudden death, in
England, of his father, whose large estates he was
proceeding home to possess.’ Much to Society’s
regret he has not been heard of since, and more
than one fair Australian Jubilator’s enquiries
about him in London were unsuccessful and dis-
appointing. Society, however, may be pleased
to know that he is now out again—
not from his ’Ome in England but from Pent-
ridge, where he has been taking a well-earned
three-years’ vacation under eminent legal advice.
He was convicted of forgery under another-,name,
appeared in court somewhat made-up (his hair had
been permitted to grow), and the secret has been
well-kept.
