being made as to the success, or even the where-
abouts, of a very lofty chevalier, beloved of
Vic. chaperones, who departed almost two years
ago for London, to float a number of gold-
mines syndicated almost entirely by ladies.
“The high-class retail storekeepers of a certain
city are complaining sorely, under their breaths,
of the excessively long credits taken by 4 the
quality ; ’ but then, this most Convenient fashion
has been set, and society must follow the fashion,
even if it takes a year to square up.
44 Ari enterprising local chemist now advertises
that he will ‘ make-up ladies ’ while they wait.
Judging by the popularity of the rouge-pot with
Melb. blockites, and their utter inability to
master its mysteries, this apothecary should have
a vast fortune awaiting him.
“ Superstition dies hard despite universal educa-
tion and all the other eye-openers and imagination-
deadeners. Many people shuddered at the 4 blood
rain,’ which fell last Xmas holidays, as the har-
binger of war and pestilence. Surely the 4 sulphur
showers ’ of some weeks ago are even more
ominous. In the eastern suburbs after the down-
pour, tiny drifts of pure brimstone could be seen
and handled on steps and verandahs. When-
ever unusual things such as mud, tadpoles,
wattle-blossom or cats and dogs come down
from the clouds, scientists tell us they have been
caught up from their native districts by winds, and
held in reserve up above. The headquarters of
the brimstone supply has, as we all know, a theo-
logical reputation none too good.
“John Curtain, ex-M.L. A., one of the 4 whitest’
of old Melbournites, is so obviously down on his
luck that a number of his former friends have
remembered the good turn they owe him. A sub-
scription-list, started with <£2o and <£3o cheques,
is progressing favorably.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
The Gentleman Who Is Away.
From Melbourne Australia received the Man Who
Is Dead ; from Brisbane she gets that no less in-
teresting personage—“ The Gentleman Who Is
Away.”
That is what Brisbane Courier calls him, with
a nice regard for the proprieties, and a solemn
disregard for the fact that everybody knows him
to be Thomas MTI wraith—ex-steel-rails specula-
tor, ex-land syndicator, ex-Q.N. Bank share-
holder, ex-Q. Treasurer, ex-protagonist of the
Griffilwraith gang; and that his identity has been
affirmed and re-affirmed in Parliament. But
Brisbane Courier has never had a sense of humor
since Carl Feilberg died. So in leader after leader
last week it wordily argued all sorts of beside-t lie-
question hypotheses regarding “ the Gentleman
Not Now In Australia,” “ the Gentleman Who Is
Away.”
These are some of the facts :
For years past the connection between certain
influential Q. politicians and the Queensland
National Bank has disgraced the province. These
politicians and their allies, being shareholders in
or traders with the Q.N.B. —a private concern in
which, despite its title, the province had no direct
public interest—used the credit of the province to
borrow huge sums of money, amounting to mil-
lions, on public account. This money was mostly
deposited with the Q. N. Bank, and was used to
conduct business operations which paid dividends
to these politicians and their allies, or was directly
advanced to these politicians and their allies by
way of overdraft with or without sufficient
security—frequently without. It was boodle on
the grand scale—public robbery and private cor-
ruption. According to some statements in Par-
liament, the question now before Queensland is to
ascertain whether or not one of these politicians
is—the Gentleman Who Is Away.
The question arises thus—
In 1893 the Q.N. Bank stopped payment, while
owing the Govt, over two millions of public money
deposited with it. There was controvery as to
whether Govt, should force the Bank into liquida-
tion, or allow it an extension of time for payment.
Liquidation was the more honest course, for the
Bank’s losses seemed irretrievable, and it is prac-
tically in slow liquidation now. There would
probably have been temporary business disloca-
tion ; yet mines would have yielded, wool would
have grown, cattle would have bred, and crops
would have been harvested ; and many people
thought dissolution by a short, sharp shock was
preferable to dissolution by a lingering illness.
But the Bank has so much influence, and has lent
money to so many people, and controls so many
votes in the electorates and in Parliament, that it
always felt itself safe from liquidation, so long as
the exact character of its dealings with the public
money, and the exact amount of its losses, toere un-
known. So a Relief Act was passed through Par-
liament, and after that, and very reluctantly, the
Govt, consented to a public investigation of the
Bank’s affairs. The investigation was made,
and disclosed reckless trading and immense
losses; but no details ivere given. A further
investigation was clamoured for, and very,
very reluctantly, Govt, consented. A Com-
mittee of four was appointed, consisting of a
director of the Q.N. Bank, a former shareholder
of the Q.N. Bank, the Auditor-General, and
another ; and after ten months’ struggling the
Committee the other day announced itself ready
with a report.
Judge of the nature of this report when the
Committee declared that it would possibly be
found so defamatory that, acting on legal advice,
they required a Parliamentary guarantee against
consequences before they would hand it in !
Four days after writing thus, and before the
required guarantee was given, the Committee
wrote again to say they had received certain cable-
grams from —the Gentleman Who Is Away. This
Gentleman they had already heard from by attor-
ney, and he now demanded to be additionally
heard in his own person. And the Committee
thought that “ if his evidence was really supple-
mentary and material,” he should be heard. In
the meantime they withdrew their report.
The Gentleman Who is Away is Thomas MTI-
- now an old man, strong neither mentally
nor physically, and living at Heidelberg, Ger-
many. Brisbane Courier proposes to send two of
the Committee to Germany after him, and damn
the expense and delay. Brisbane Worker en-
quires—
Will the Courier name an instance in which what was
practically a court of justice was removed 10,000 miles in
order that a man who is practically On his trial may delay
the declaration of a verdict ?
Would the same allowance be made in favor of a poor
man ?
And Glassey, leader of Q. Labor-party, was to
move on Tuesday (too late for reference in this
issue) —
That, in the opinion of this House, the Government
should obtain the report of the commission on the Q.N
Bank, Limited, and that such report should be laid on the
table of the House without further delay.
It is an interesting situation, both for the tax-
payers who are present, and for the Gentleman
Who Is Away.

Personal Items.
J. T. Neale, Sydney’s big ready-money
potentate, who died last week at Potts Point, was
the holder of a thousand mortgages.
F. : “However we may regard the inten-
tions of the late Judge Windeyer, we must all
admit that he was good in execution.”
The return of Majah Reay to work on the
leading articles of Melb. ’Evenin’ Erald is shown
by the notes of exclamation that he leaves all
along his track.
The Mercadool land-case fees will make
N.S. Wales open its eyes. Mr. C. G. Heydon’s
bill for appearing for the Crown before the Land
Board alone totals J 21107 14s.
Queensland has had eight Speakers and
four Presidents of Council. Palmer is in his 16th
year of office as President. Maurice O’Connell
held the President’s chair for 19 years.
Mrs. Perkins, who died at Bowenfels the
other day, aged 80 years, came to N.S.W. in 1829,
and for nearly 60 years lived in the Lithgow
Valley. She left 91 grand-children and 30 great
grand-children.
The death of ex-Justice Windeyer leaves
but one N.S.W. Supreme Court pensioner—
Justice Poster. There are still three D.C. Judges
on pension (at £750 each) —Dowling, Wilkinson,
and M'Farland.
A Bulletin man in Melbourne is told that
several years after the Mt. Rennie massacre the
late Judge Windeyer received as a Christmas gift
in a neat box a hempen noose. The donor was the
well-known writer “ Anon.”
Shipowner M‘Eacharn threatens to be next
Mayor of Melbourne—not lawyer Sam Gillott.
The old style of councillor is dropping out of the
business, anyway, and the old style of joke re civic
greatness will have to go, likewise.
Should Attorney-General Want resign and
go fishing for good, who would replace him in the
N.S.W. Ministry? Possibly Premier Reid would
act as Attorney-General himself. With a good
Crown Law staff, there is really little to do.

Charles Randwick De Clouet, now under
arrest at Bathurst charged with shooting his wife,
was being put to bed by his mother some 30 years
ago when the bushrangers Ben Hall and Gilbert
entered the bedroom and forced Mrs. De Clouet to
hand up the cash-box. The De Clouets of that
date were hotel-keepers and owned the Sports-
man’s Arms in Bathurst.
King O’Malley, the untamed Yankee in-
surance canvasser of S.A., is the first formally
declared candidate for the Federal Parliament,
The Bulletin moves for a special clause in the
Constitution declaring that O’Malley, Dan O’Con-
nor, Boy Politician Caulfield, Mearher and
Tommy Dodd shall be permanently disqualified
from sitting in either Federal House.
Dr. (say) Mabel Fixemup, Mosquitoville’s
sole female medico, having lately recovered from
a severe illness, and deeming a change desirable,
requested her lady companion, Miss A., to wire
to the proprietor of a certain hotel at a fashionable
seaside resort for rooms. The telegram ran as
follows : “ Can Dr. Fixemup and Miss A. have a
room at your hotel?” The answer was: “Yes;
but keep it dark.”

To apotheosise Windeyer as Woman’s
Friend ” is to sin against fact. “He never
doubted a woman’s word,” say certain glorifiers,
while the cry of the judicially-tortured woman in
the infamous Butler-Davis-Rofe case rings in the
public ears. And there’s a catalogue of such.
One of the young ladies who accompanied
the Australian Premiers in their Jubilee mission,
says the Queen has “ a peculiar brown complexion. ”
It’s the constant toasting, The Bulletin supposes.
Hard-up Bishop Green, of Grafton and
Armidale, N.S.W., whose pecuniary woes have
had wide circulation, has happened on a ripe
windfall, valued at about £1545. An English lay-
man left £150,000 for church purposes, and Bishop
Green comes under the covenant to the above
extent.
Old Charles Hardie Buzacott, quondam
editor of Brisbane (Q.) Courier and now of Rock-
hampton Record, having taken to bikinsr for his
health’s sake, was heard to remark to a friend, one
day: “You must excuse me, old man, for not
having acknowledged your bow in the street, but
the fact is that one day, while on my bicycle, I
took off my hat to a lady, and the next instant I
found the machine a-top of me.” Which recalls
the story of the German swell who, meeting his
friend on a horse in the park, hailed him jovially.
“Please don’t speak to me,” said the friend, who
was just managing to hold on; “ don’t you see 1
am busy riding ! ”

A circular dated Sept. 4—the first anniversary of
the vag. s death—solicits subscriptions towards a
tombstone for his grave in Melb. General
Cemetery. The prime mover in the matter
appear to be Tom Kennedy’s wife, and Harry
Short, editor of the Leader will act as treasurer
of any cash that may come in. A subscription-
boom is not very probable. Journalists, as a
rule, will excuse their indifference to the appeal on
the ground that Julian Thomas was not the man
to want a tombstone.

“Well, read it right

laws will allow him,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “A Major-General and
the 53-year-old scion of the oldest and wealthiest
Hungarian family, the frail, gentlemanly old chap

tread, and, made of the stuff of heroes, ignores
alike tumbler-throwing episodes, the scandalised
indignation of his relatives, and the flimsiness of a
Yankee divorce, and is down to be led to the altar
in Vienna at an early date. Albert Edward,
when requested to interfere by the aristocratic
family, with his usual good sense replied that
Esterhazy was old enough to look after himself,
so now the love-sick Hungary-Austrian haunts the
race-meetings seeing bridals, titbits, and dead

The amiable Sim, Labor-member for Car-
pentaria (N.Q.), is bald-headed through too much
earnestness. A peculiarity of his baldness is that,
whenever Sim gets pleasurably excited, his scalp
glistens with more than its ordinary lustre. It
always shines. When he becomes thoroughly
roused, it is phosphorescent. Hansard and press
reporters, at every likelihood of a late sitting,
watch Sim s head. It is their thermometer or
barometer, and foretells to a certainty whether
there is going to be a late and lively sitting or
not. ron often hear them say: “Better order
supper; Sim’s head is lighted up ! ”

Windeyer—suddenly as it dissolved at last—can-
not be better exemplified than by a simple tabula-
tion of the temporal aspects of the infamous Mount ’
Kennie trial, thus Monday, Court sits 11*
hours : Tuesday, 14* ; Wednesday, only 10 hours
(Judge has to dine out); Thursday, also only 10
tor the same reason. But Friday compensates
with 18 2 hours, right off—up to 3.30 on Saturday
morning . And Saturday’s proceedings proper,
commencing again at 9 a.m., have left the
exhausted jury less than five hours in which to
eat, sleep, wash, and make generally a futile
enort at the all-round recuperation required prior
to listening to a ten hours’ summing-up from the
iron-bodied, iron-souled phenomenon on the bench »
TO BEN TILLETT.
Pale Christ of Labor, who have sought
To shame our wilted zeal;
Whose lava-tide of love has taught
Our deadened nerves to feel !
May kindly Sun, pulsed warmly through
Australia’s airy wealth,
In part repay our debt to you
With fresh bouquets of health.
A comrade merry in our play,
As staunch in time of war,
You’re not as most who helm our way
From Night’s uncertain shore.
Your limpid soul does not exude
Their oily patronage,
Nor leagued are you with harpy brood
To foul our heritage.
You’ve lit the holy fire of Hate
To melt the chains of caste,
To burn the poppy-flowers of Fate,
The garbage of the past.
It spreads among those fools who till
The toadstool beds of Rank,
And those who gorge their greedy fill
In stock-exchange and bank
Because you’ve bid our hopes re-rise
And trimmed our lamps anew,
They spirt their vitriolic lies
In frenzied hate at you.
For such as you warn such as these
Of coming earthquake shocks ;
O, loving-lipp’d Demosthenes,
Rienzi of the Docks !
Melb.
DANTOW.

Dear Bulletin, —Why should not the
Government protect the bush-worker’s health as
well as the Sydneyite’s? For instance, on one
station (a mortgage co.’s) they overhauled a flock
of wethers, which were sorted out into three
classes—(l) sound, (2) curable, (3) callous and in-
curable. These latter—the sight of which would
make Sydney people faint —were killed for, and
eaten by, the station hands ! On the station at
which I am shearing the shearers’ hut is 30ft. x
40ft. In this hovel, the cracks in which we vainly
tried to stop by pasting up newspapers, nearly

Editor Bulletin, —I am making a collec-

[ would like to meet the author of the above

When the Oatlands’ (Tas.) criminal sit-
tings were removed to Hobart, farmers complained
that they could not get a conviction for sheep-
stealing as every farmer called on the juries was
challenged. A man was brought up for that
offence at Hobart, and, the jury disagreeing, he
was remanded to next sittings. Another Judge
heard the case this time, and did his level best to
get a conviction. The jury were long “out, ’ and
finally found “ not guilty.” One of them being
asked why, replied that it was “an Irish verdict: it
was the big man agin the little, and we couldn t
see the fun of that.”
• • * *
“I always liked taking a rise out of a
squatter,” said Jim, meditatively ; “ s’pose it was
’cause I never had the chance of being a squatter
myself. Remember one time signing a contract
with a boss-squatter to bank up a dam which the
creek had washed away, and to grub a large tree
in the newly-made waterway, which was supposed
to ‘ drink ’ too much. Wheeling a barrow all day
makes one think of an evening, and ... it hap-
pened that a limb of the old red gum stretched
over the centre of our earthworks. Now, why
couldn’t we work a block, tackle, and windlass.
Carried unanimously. So me and my mate—he
was a digger too—swung each load instead of
wheeling to ‘top-up’ the embankment. But we
thought we would take down the tackle every
Sunday, when our friend the squatter called to see
how we were ‘ getting on.’ Next Sunday he was
flabbergasted at the work done, but more at the
‘ wages ’ we were making out of him ; and he rode
away with a sigh, saying, ‘Well, of course, you
chaps know the hardest job’s to come the
grubbing.’ Next Friday, however, some cattle
strayed out our way, and the squatter made an
extra visit, when over went the show. Mr. Squat-
ter took in the situation with a whistle,
ing, ‘ By the way, that is a good idea, mCsing use
of the tree ; however, we will keep it w future
needs ; you won’t be sorry.’ ‘ Not much,’ I said ;
‘ we’ve got to grub that tree out: it sin the con-
tract.’ ‘ Oh, well, I’ll let you off,’ says the squat-
ter ‘ and you can make me an allowance for it.
‘No blanky fear,’ says my mate ; ‘ tree s coming
out, if we blow her out with dynamite. 4 1th .
Well, I won’t be hard ; so we will cry quits.’
Then we pulled our little trigger. ‘You know a
good thing when you see it, but our price is •£•->—
or the tree goes.’ Well, you should have seen the
performance, and hear the pet names he called us !
Our troubles ! We took him down all right. I
always liked taking down squatters, ’ said Jim,
meditatively.

The man “ down for the show ” is usually
the least exacting of customers at a metropolitan
pub. or hash-house, but occasionally a brown-
bearded visitor stands out for his rights and some-
thing" over. A Melb. publican had one of this
sort on his hands recently. The morning after
Bill’s arrival Bung heard sounds of riot upstairs,
accompanied by reports as of the explosion of a
slang-dictionary. Bung hurried to save his furni-
ture and fittings, and found Bill raving m the
passage, and a housemaid having a fit in the
corner. “ What’s wrong? ” yelled Bill; “ heaps
is wrong ! Don’t I pav you the same as these
other fellers ? ” (pointing to the row of bed-room
doors). “I do ; well, why the blanky, blanky,
blanky ain’t I treated the same?” “ What’s the
matter, man? ” “ Why ain’t I treated the same .
That’s what. Look here ” Bill sailed along
the passage, kicking a pair of boots from in front
of each door all over the shop. “ You give each
o’those fellers a pair o’ crimson boots, why the
don’t I get a pair, too ? ”

Presently she looks up, when he catches her eye,
jerks his head backward and smiles alluringly.
She knows it is the much-desired invitation, and
as she rises and ambles towards him he crooks his
elbow and says : ‘Me ’n you.

“I remember a new-chum schoolmaster
placing on his bedroom wall half-a-dozen photos,
of his best girl in various poses” (writes “Steff ” to
The Bulletin from Fiji). “Next morning the cock-
roaches had bitten bits off his lady-love with quite
rude indiscrimination. I have seen them eat the
strings out of tennis-racquets, violins, and cricket-
bats, caught them eating the rubber off a type-
writer (probably on account of the violet copying-
ink flavor). I saw once a fat one fly out of a wed-
ding-cake. The only thing they won’t touch is
preen bamboo ; a few young bamboo shoots laid
in the drawers and boxes, and stood about the
room, will keep them away, but the bamboo must
soon be renewed, for when dry it loses its power.”
• • • •
S.S. : “The snake-story season is
coming and I want to get in first with
mine. It is the tale of a Swift’s Creek
miner who awoke one morning to find
he was sharing his bunk with a huge
black snake. One coil was about his arm,
and the end of the snake with the head
attached passed down between the bunk
and the wall. An occasional tug and fre-
quent oscillations convinced the victim
that the reptile was prepared for any
emergency, and he just lay there and
sweated. His soul dwindled to the size
of a pea, and his innards crowded up
into his throat, his hair grew dank and
stiff, and he could feel it creeping about
on the pillow, although he didn’t think
about that much, his attention being di-
verted to more important matters. He
swears he remained in this condition for
several hours, but it was perhaps an hour
after his awakening when his mate blun-
dered into the hut, and Bill cursed Jim
with low, intense curses, dreading lest
the snake should be disturbed at his busi-
ness under the bed. Jim presently dis-
covered the trouble, and, proceeding
carefully and cautiously, secured one of
Bill’s pillows, and jammed the serpent
whilst Bill flung himself out of bed in a
clammy, flabby heap. Then both men
peered anxiously under the bunk : A dead
snake hung there, with a mutilated neck,
and Bill’s great bla ck tom-cat, Satan, was
amusing himself with the mangled re-
mains. Bill has since turned grey think-
ing of the horrible peril he encountered
in his sleep. ”

“Blanky” rules in N.S.W. A
provincial bishop had just sat down after
a complimentary speech, and the dean
finished up by saying, “ But you know,,
my lord, we have very good deacons.”
Deacon Woolbales elected to speak for
the rest—perhaps because he says “blan-
ky” oftener than any other man in the
district : “That’s all right, my lord, but
you must remember that we have a blanky
good dean ! ”

* * * *
Mr. Hawlegs was manager of the Great
Squander mine, W. A., at a salary of =£looo a year.
Miss Lemons and Miss Threestar were both
barmaids at the Tindog Hotel. Miss Lemons
said that Mr. Hawlegs intended to marry “ one of
us girls,” and she added, “ It’s no good the two of
us bucking up to him at once. Now what I pro-
pose is to throw dice, the loser to hang back while
the winner plays her cards all she knows. 1 hen
they shook. Miss Lemons threw three China-
men, while Miss Threestar beat her by one ; this
was naturally followed by a coolness between the
two girls. Miss Threestar played her cards well,
and Mr. Hawlegs was on the point of proposing,
so she thought. It was Miss Tnreestar’s afternoon
off, so she took a stroll towards the railway-station
to see the daily train come in. Mr. Hawlegs
was there also, but he scarcely recognised her.
The train whistled as it approached. Hie next
thing Miss Threestar saw was Mr. Hawlegs
embracing a fair, fat lady of 40, with six children
and 18 large parcels. She made back to the 1 in-
dog Hotel in tears ; but the news had got there
before her. Miss Lemons poked borak as she
came in ; then followed a shower of glasses and
bottles, and only for the active intervention of a
policeman, a murder would have been committed.

Dear Bulletin, —Rabbits in the N.S.W.
Western districts do climb trees and do jump
fences ! I have soon ’em. After a dry stretch,
the dead bodies of bunnies may be seen by the
score caught in the branches of stTci dmi bed
trees, over a dozen feet from the ground. Hunger
gives the animal a power which, at ordinary times,
if ho possesses it, lie fails to exercise. Anyone
who has had experience of country ’tween the
Darling and the S.A. border will endorse my
statements. —Out West.

They were putting in a hole in a hot drive.
Bill was striking, Jim was turning, sitting with
the drill between his legs. Presently Bill noticed
something peculiar about Jim, and, looking closei,
found that he was fast asleep. After every blow
the drill jumped, and around it went as metho-
dically as a machine. Bill insists that dim re-
mained fast asleep until the hole was drilled, and
Bill is a honorable man.

Yindex writes from Wollongong
“Lex” is wrong in stating 1 that the N.S.V. TTowns
Police Act was extended to the whole province in
The prosecution for furious driving would be under Sec.
40 of 2 Vic No. 2. This Act could be extended by procla-
mation to any town. The Act 19 Vio No. 24, making
further police regulations, which was held only to extend
to proclaimed towns, is by 32 Vic. No. 7 (the Act referred
to bv “ Lex ”) extended and made applicable to the whole
province ; but 32 Vic. No. 7 does not affect the provisions
of 2 Vic No. 2 so as to make the 2 Vic. No. 2 in force ex-
cept in proclaimed towns. The objection raised by
the defendant’s attorney was, therefore, valid, and the
P.M. rightly dismissed the case.

Cabbage hat black velvet Band

A Steadfast Faith
A STEADFAST faith is necessary to steadfast
conduct. According to the faith is the life. If
that is uncertain and unsettled, fixed at no point,
the result will be unsteadiness : the course of life
erratic. The only safety is faith based upon
irrefutable evidence. This is the kind of faith
the Rev. ffm. Riding, Presbyterian Minister,
Zeehan, Tas., has in Warner’s “Safe” Cure, for
he says: “I have derived much benefit from
Warner’s ‘ Safe ’ Cure.” Your faith will be equal
to his if you follow his advice.
Warner’s Safe Cure is a Specific fob Liver
and Kidney Disease.
Detective Goulder, 14 years in the service,
dragged in “ police and civilians ” in his evidence
in a Sydney court the other day. At a military
court-martial police are always treated as civilians
—which they are.

It is astonishing how often people need to be told of the winning qualities of . . .
THE BEESTON HUMBER.
Year after year the records show beyond dispute that the very best man can only win when mounted on the very best machine.
The World’s Verdict is—“ The Beeston Humber is Best.”
Therefore, if you want to win, you will have to get a Beeston Humber. On this cycle the rider’s exertion all goes to speed ;
there is no waste through indirect strains or bending, springy cranks. You have a firm grip of a firm machine meaning
economy of strength, sweetness of movement and safety.
All BEESTON HUMBERS are of one quality, whether supplied for the recrea-
tion of royalty or the necessities of the artizan.

Liogia, &c.
Dear Bulletin, —The Logia attributed to Christ
which were lately exhumed in Egypt seem, as far
as they go, to bear out the old contention that the
highest and most spiritual doctrines of Christianity
were evolved by the subtle Hindu intellect
ages before they sprang into life in Judea.
One of the sayings, “ Cleave the wood and there
am I,” is full to the rind with esoteric Hindu
Pantheism, Aet it is to this old and mystical
land of India—the cradle and nursery of super-
sensual religions—that we send crude, half-taught,
but cocksure young missionaries—by way of teach-
ing our spiritual grandmother to suck cosmic eggs !
En passant, Emerson’s poem entitled “Brahma,”
which begins with the quatrain—
If the red slayer thinks he slays,
Or if the slain think he is slain,
They little know the subtle ways
I keep, and pass, and turn again—
had for origin an incident described in the Hindu
epic “ Bhagavadgita.” Two mighty armies are
facing each other on the battle-field, and (to quote
the words of an eminent Brahmin pundit) the
leader of one army, Arjuna, seizes the moment
before the actual shock of war to enter into
a profound Pantheistic discussion on the iden-
tity of the slayer with the slain. Imagine an
English general carrying on such high argu-
ment with the commander of the troops
opposed to him ! I think, speaking with
all due humility, that the root-cause of the com-
parative, if not absolute, spiritual failure of
Christianity amongst the conquering races of
Europe (the Teutonic races, especially) is that
they have tried to assimilate an Oriental creed
entirely unsuited to their natures. The altars of
Odin and Thor have been long overthrown, but,
for all practical purposes, their gods are still Odin
and Thor—and Mammon. The Celtic race—
probably because it was one of the earliest off-
shoots from the Aryan stock, and met with less
disturbing vicissitudes on its journey westward—
is perhaps the only one in Europe that is in
sympathetic touch with the mystical side of
Christianity. The legend of the trefoil and its
immediate reception by the ancient Irish as an
incontrovertible demonstration of the truth
of the Tria juncta in Uno doctrine could hardly
have been told of any other Caucasian people.
“ Thou and I are one,” or, in briefer form, “ I am
Thou ” is the essence of the teaching of Christ.
The large-stomached, ever-hungry, full-blooded
peoples who are now the propagandists of Chris-
tianity write unequivocally on the page of history
—“ laml ” to the nth power if necessary.
To return to the Logia. It is confidently ex-
pected that many more will be found. What an
Awful Joke (to the unregenerate) it would be if
some of them—proved to be, beyond the shadow of
a doubt, genuine sayings of Christ—denied (as did
Origen) the doctrine of Eternal Punishment !
And what a gigantic Reconstruction of the
Churches this would mean ! but, of course, the
Churches would never acknowledge such evidence
as valid. Clever shovel-hatted persons would ex-
Elain it away and say that Christ’s teaching had
een wickedly interpreted in terras of Neo-
Platonism.
This suggests another irreverent speculation,
viz. : Out of the 1,400,000,000 human beings cal-
culated to be now infesting the earth how many
are living on the Other World ? One to every 500
would be a modest guess. Yet it would
mean 2,800,000. But to this Regular Army
must be added the Irregular Troops and Camp
Followers— Obeah men and medicine-men of all
colors. Five millions would probably be an under-
estimate of the total strength of this army. Its
revenues (call them sinews of war against the
Devil) cannot be even approximately guessed at.
And all this vital force and wealth is lavishly and
continually spent in carrying to all the races of
the Earth the Gospel that it is good to be good !
Suppose that Satan had a similarly organised and
equally equipped Army? Not necessary, you
say ? So say the sky-pilots. But suppose that he
had ? I venture to say that the result would be
somewhat surprising. The Innate Cussedness of
Man would make him take to Virtues merely
because they were forbidden. If the whole Ten
Commandments —with the single exception, per-
haps, of the one against murder —could be broken
vulgarly in the public market-place, without inter-
ference by the police, Vice would become un-
fashionable in 24 hours. That is my opinion.
V.J.D.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Returner is anyhow not more
popular than Premier Turner.
A question for the National Ass : “ Which
was the Apostle of Thrift—Christ or Judas?”
asks Pastor Turnbull, of Melb.
It is most ominous that Melb. Argus now
offers up daily praise to Premier Turner. The
boa-constrictor slobbers before it swallows.
N.S.W. Attorney - General (Want) and
Colonial Secretary (Brunker—ye gods !) —draw
£450 a year more each in official salary than does
Reid, Premier and Treasurer—the former £lB2O
each, the latter £1370.
H. : “I heard Higinbotham, in Vic. Par-
liament, say Melb. was more Tory than London.
So it was, and is. Tillett only says in outraged
Melb. what he has said in complacent London.
Either Lord Salisbury or Archbishop Temple
would grasp his hand.”
Vic. ultra Freetraders, wooing electorates,
ooncur in assuring their audiences that Federation
has rendered the fiscal issue unimportant. Mackey
in Ripon, and Ilickford, at Brunswick, both
shining lights of the Freetrade Democrats, are the
latest assurers in this respect.
“Toowoomba (Q.) is anti-Federal,” cries
Fogarty, one of its elected Parliamentary repre-
sentatives. Toowoomba versus Australia ; nature
of complaint—the President of the Australian
Commonwealth would probably not use Redmud-
opolis as the Eden of his summer residence. Con-
vulsions !
N.S.W. Lands Minister Carruthers’ letter
from an alleged busliman, cracking up the Bogan
Scrub relief-work, should only have been pub-
lished with the name of the writer. Unkind
people hint that it was inspired or written by the
brother of a Minister, who is, or was, a foreman
or overseer at the Bogan.
Canterbury Ttmes (M.L.) London corres-
pondent, on the Jubilee procession : “ The closing
of the bridges and blocking of the East End
banished the rough element.” If the East Enders
--■the gaunt people of the slums —wore deliberately
shut out, no wonder Premier Reid didn’t see many
signs of poverty in the Jubilee show.
N.S.W. Electoral Act provides for the re-
casting of electorates five years after the taking of
the census. The Reid Govt., however, its enemies
allege, found that this would possibly involve the
loss of at least a half-dozen Ministerial supporters,
including one strong Minister. Hence, no steps
have been taken to gazette the alterations.
Billson, (secretary of the Bootmaker.'
Union, and a pal of Trenwith’s, is the nominee of
Vic. Labor-party for Jolimont against Theo. Fink
at next elections. As Fink shows tendencies at
times to flirt with Labor, he will think this savors
of ingratitude. It was in this electorate that
Randolph Bedford made his heroic maiden effort
(for one night only) last election time.
Premier Forrest, who raised an indignant
howl of “Rats !” when the Federal Convention
decided that the Senate should not have equal
control over money matters, was the first delegate
to lose his dignity and yell at his fellow-con ven-
tionist. The manners of the Convention have
hitherto,been so stately that that sudden shriek of
defunct rodent sounded like blasphemy.
The logic of Simon Fraser, of the Vic.
House of 48 Rich Landlords : “ The people rule in
Greece, and she went to war and got jolly well
licked.” Obviously, then, the people in Australia
shouldn’t rule. Also, the people in Turkey are
Mohammedans, and Turkey came out top-dog in
the scrimmage referred to. So, obviously, the
people in Australia should be Mohammedans.
S.A. Labor-party, disguise the fact as its
leaders may, is much weakened by the desertion
of Charleston, M.L.C., who was by far the ablest
man in its ranks. He is a sound Democrat, and
has ever dinned into the ears of the workers that
the land-question is the labor-question. The dis-
sension in the Labor-ranks is the first dividend to
the Stomach Party out of the Adelaide Hospital
squabbles.
Even while the echo of the Jubilee-reveller
still haunts the ear, the usually ultra-loyal S.M.
Herald goes back on its jingoism to such an
extent as to compare the desperate Sikh soldiers
at Saragari to the Swiss, who died in defence of
the Tuilleries on the 10th of August, and who
“ owed no duty to that king of shreds and patches,
Louis XVI., other than what was due for their
poor sixpence a day, but this bound them as
soldiers to die at their post, and they did it.
This, too, was done by the brave Sikhs at Saga-
gari.” Too! What, the Kaisar-i-hind a mere
sovereign of shreds and patches ? To whom those
Sikhs owed no duty ? O shade of Beaconsfield !
Why, the assertion literally reeks of constructive
treason ! Surely some enemy of Mister James
hath done this !
Bryant, one of the
candidates for the haugh-
ty electorate of .Toorak
(Vic.), angles for. the votes
of the toilers by making
pathetic remarks about
the law which prevents
the workman getting his
dinner-beer on Sundays.
The genuine Tory always
imagines he can get the
support of the working
man by saying some-
thing sympathetic about
beer.
The continual asser-
tion of the Sydney Foreign
trade press that there is a
great and constant influx
from Victoria of people
anxious to participate in
the blessings of shoddy
would go much better if
there were some evidence
to back it. The State re-
turns in the matter of sea-
traffic give no information
as to where people come
from or where they leave
for, and, as regards over-
land arrivals and depar-
tures, they lump together
Victoria,S.A. and Queens-
land in one item. Taking
the last three years to-
gether, the departures
from N.S.W. to these

Poor Melb. Argus attacks the Vic. Fac-
tories Act:—
It would be interesting to know what this Act has cost
Govt, in legal expenses during the few months it has been
in operation. Its drafting is so faulty that every week an
appeal has to be made to the Court to ascertain what its
provisions really mean.
To which Chief Inspector Ord replies that there
have been 200 prosecutions in six months, at a
nett cost of £3. As to faulty drafting, &e., the
department has so far won every appeal made to
the Supreme Court.
Old patriarch Adye Douglas, of Tasmania,
is the one Convention delegate who seems to have
a genuine pride in his island. The old man’s
scorn as he tells of the time when the early Tas-
manians came over and annexed the empty wastes
of Victoria, and laid the foundations of that up-
start place, is magnificent. “If we had known
what you would be like we wouldn’t have taken
the trouble to create you,” is Douglas’s attitude
towards the Vic. delegates. Douglas describes the
mild, hesitating Fysh as “ a man who never speaks
without conceding something.”
NOTES AT N.S.W. ART SOCIETY SHOW.
Premier Turner’s enemies aver that his
only cry when he “ goes to the country ” in Octo-
ber will be—“ Vote for the Man who Went to
England.” The Govt, is said to be afraid of the
land-tax, especially in farming constituencies. It
is also said to consider that women’s suffrage is
not wanted, and that the Leg. Council is in its
right place. Even the Argus takes heart of grace
to announce that Harper and Kennedy (two tame
Tories in Opposition) may be offered portfolios.
In fact, the aforesaid bilious rumor has it that
Turner, P.C., K.C.M.G., &c., has really and
truly been ennobbled.
“ TEA-LA, TEA'LA, TRA-LA-LA-LA-LA-LA !—(From PICK-ME-UP .)
As leader of N.S.W. Opposition, Lyne’s
chief characteristic—it would be cruel to say his
strong point—is his dull capacity for receiving
punishment. He is in politics what Burge was in
the prize-ring—“ Iron bark ” —and has only one
weapon, a left that isn’t by any means straight.
The jokeful Premier Reid, on the contrary, has,
metaphorically speaking, all sorts of weapons in
his armoury—the rapier, single-stick, and fist.
And on occasion he is not above smashing his
enemy’s skull in with a suddenly-seized bottle.
For advocates of the private ownership of
railways: Queensland railways, like those of
Maoriland and N.S. Wales, have now reached
what the paying point would be if the rate
of interest were the rate at which Queensland is
able to borrow money in London to-day. Total
money invested therein —about eighteen millions.
Revenue, £1,176,273, or £2 17s. 4d. per cent.—an
increase of 4s. Bd. per cent, on last year. The
Brisbane-Charleville line earned £3 14s. 3d. per
cent. ; Rockhampton-Longreaeh, £4 18s. sd. per
cent. ; while that magnificent property, the Towns-
ville - Hughenden line, brought in the pheno-
menal dividend of £9 19s. per cent.
The struggle
over narrow-
gauge railways
in Victoria
seems unend-
ing. The Go-
vernment fav-
ors them in
mountain re-
gions because of
theircheapness.
The railway en-
gineering auth-
orities oppose
them, partly
through fossil-
dom, partly on
the ground that
it is absurd to
start another
new gauge in
Victoria, and
make confusion
worse confoun-
ded, just when
an attempt is
being made to
arrive at uni-
formity, and
partly on the
very just theory
that the cost of
special rolling-
stock, and the
expense of load-
ing and unload-
ing and reload-
ing goods at junctions will make a large hole
in the paper economy. The Age is on the side of
the narrow-gauge ; the Argus and most of the
logic seem to be on the other side. And the
Railway Committee wobbles between two opinions,
as usual, and decides nothing.
Deakin is to b 3 opposed at Essendon by a
Labor-party nominee (probably Bishop, president
of the party) at the ensuing elections. Feeling
runs bitterly against him on account of his late
admission of his approval of the calling out of the
“ fire-low ” squad at the maritime strike.
Max Hirsch, the Vic. Single-taxer, sub-
mits a new scheme of Leg. Council reform. He
proposes that every man or woman should vote
when 30 years old without further qualification.
This would be a little better than the present
system—but isn’t it an insidious attack on female
suffrage? Also, it isn’t so much the qualification
for Leg. Council electors as for Councillors them-
selves that wants mending.
Some of the Vic. delegates at the Federal
Convention pose as representatives of the Austra-
lian Natives’ Association. The worst offender*
are Quick, Deakin, and Peacock. They have nc
such status. The A.N.A. has never at any time
passed any resolution referring to any scheme of
Federation. At any rate, the A.N.A. is a very
mixed body of young men, having no coherence
whatever from a political aspect.
According to the Queensland Registrar-
General’s statistics, out of 140 persons making
their mark on the marriage register 40 indi-
viduals (or 20 p.c.) were colored aliens. This
foreign element eliminated, the rate of illiteracy
among persons of European extraction would be
I'7B p.c. . . . The illiterate alien is, however,
fairly successful at leaving his mark on the popu-
lation, which is becoming every year more varie-
gated.
F. : “ The slave State threatens Federa-
tion, after all. N.S.W. politicians are credited
with anxiety for colleagues from Brisbane, so as
to settle the question of Sydney becoming Federal
capital. If Q. must come in, it should be a con-
dition precedent that all slavery shall be abso-
lutely wiped out within five years of the Federal
compact. Q. will be well able to‘afford’ this
(taking her own point of view), because she will
have a sugar monopoly.”
Premier Kingston’s wire from Sydney in
denunciation of Charleston, M.L.C., may have
been justified, but it was questionable policy. All
of Kingston’s political enemies rolled up and
voted solid for Charleston, and he got in by 700
votes. The S.A. Premier’s outspokenness is the
admiration of his friends, but it also stimulates
his enemies, and experienced S.A. politicians
reckon that that vitriolic telegram changed the
result of the recent contest.
The stated reasons which led the Federal
Convention to decide, by an overwhelming vote,
that senators must be elected by the people and
not by the State Parliaments, constitute a
solemn impeachment of the present N.S.W.
method of electing a Public Works’ Committee.
Even a couple of supporters of such bodies as
that committee voted against senators being
similarly elected, because of the log-rolling, back-
scratching and shameless intrigue inseparable from
the method.
Ben Tillett • “On an average the English-
man earned ,£lB per annum, while the Australian
earned £3l. How was it that the Australians
with £3l per head were borrowing off the English
with £18?” The Bulletin has been asking the
same question for a long time past. The Austra-
lian has two fixed beliefs : first, that his country
is the richest country on earth; second, that his
country is so poor that it can’t pay its way with-
out getting loans from countries which, he says,
are still poorer.
Charleston, M.L.C. (S.A.), was re-elected
by 762 votes over the authorised Labor-candidate,
Hutchison. This leaves things a good deal mixed.
Hutchison said, and the United Labor - party
bosses said, that he was the only genuine Labor
candidate, and that Charleston had fallen away,
and was effete and bogus and of no account.
Charleston said that he had always been a genuine
Labor member, only an ignorant and narrow-
minded section of the party had cast him out;
and the electors supported Charleston. It was
the question of Barabbas over again—with the
difference that no one seemed to know for certain
which of the two was Barabbas.
Francis Abigail and R. D. Meagher, of
the Dean Curse business—the robust, advertising
person who was going to retrieve his character by
patient industry in some foreign land—are re-
ported as candidates for next N.S.W. election.
The election is due in about 11 months at latest,
but unless the finances improve it will probably
come earlier. To go to the country with his
fourth consecutive deficit in his hand would be
taking a big risk, even for so superlatively cheeky
a person as Reid. Moreover, there is always an
advantage in taking the lumbering and unready
Lyne by surprise. That permanently dismasted
political vessel never steers well, but in a sudden
squall he steers a little worse even than usual.
Horace Tozer’s very latest Tozer is that
Queensland is absolutely yearning to send ten
popularly-elected delegates to the Federal Con-
vention, and Tozer himself can’t sleep at night for
his desire to peg in hard at the great cause of
unity, and if the Convention will only adjourn
some more the matter will be fixed up. First the
Tozer Government agreed to send ten elected
delegates. Second, it proposed to send instead
ten delegates chosen by the Assembly. Third,
being defeated by the Council which wanted a
voice in the selection, it decided not to send any-
body. Fourth, it proposed to send ten representa-
tives appointed by the Legislature generally.
Fifth, it decided to let the whole thing slide and
darn Federation. Sixth, it is going to pass a
measure for the election of delegates by the people,,
so that it has come out by the same hole it went
in at some nine months ago. Seventh, it will,
probably, either weaken on this new idea, or the
Council will throw the Bill out, and then the
Tozer Government will get back to attitude V,
and darn the whole thing once more. After that,
anything may happen.

TT— . .. TsTT^
sis
Australian, or for the matter of that, any
other, workmen, haven’t often shown up. better
than the Broken Hill men did on the day when
the fire broke out in Block 11 (writes The Bulle-
tin correspondent). It wasn’t, except in rare
1 oases, a question of rescuing comrades either—it
was simply to prevent the destruction of the pro-
perty that gave them or others employment.
■ Comparatively few men who went underground
to help locate the fire, and block up draught-
vents, and so on, escaped the effects of the smoke,
and the fumes from the burning Oregon, and
other gases. For hours one cage was kept at
work hauling up men who had been overcome.
A temporary hospital of timber and canvas was
built in the lee of another shaft-building, and
four doctors had their hands full restoring the
men—a process which occupied from a few minutes
to several hours. In the circs, the marvellous
thing is that there were only three deaths. At
one time there were over 50 men in the hospi-
tal. Some were brought up limp and pulse-
less ; others were raving. Yet the supply
of volunteers never eased. Some men, in
fact, after recovering descended again; but
as it was found that they were speedily over-
come it was ordered that no man who had
been in hospital should make the descent again.
Among the men who had to be sent up were
General Manager Stewart and Mine Manager
Horwood. Fortunately, Mcßryde, M.S.C. of
Vic., chairman of directors, happened to be on the
mine to see personally what these working men
sometimes will do. And if he reflected that an
immense amount of suffering and probably some
lives, not to speak of property, might have been
saved if the co had taken experts’ advice after the
last fire and purchased smoke-apparatus or simple
oxygen bags, all the better.
“ Tompkins on Klondike ” (Barry Pain, in
London Daily Chronicle)
Come ter the plice where they’ve got it right, come where
the treasure’s ’id,
Where a ’at-full of mud is a five-pun-note, and the clod
on yer ’eel is a quid,
Where it scratches the soil and it tumbles art, as much as
yer ’ands can ’old,
Where the ’ills above and the plines beneath is bulgin’
an’ crackin’ with gold.
Klondike 1 Klondike ! Libel yerluggidge Klondike !
Theer’s no chawnce in the street ter-dye,
Theer’s no luck darn Shoreditch wye.
Pack up yer traps an’ be orf, I sye,
An’ orf an’ awye ter Klondike !
Lots o’ chaps they 'as stawtid small, and awterwuds struck
it rich,
Barght their kerridges, ailed their yachts—nutthink’s too
good for sich—
Awmies o’ servants, miles o’ land, cuttin’ the toppicalart,
Bran-noo paliss to knock Park-line, then . . . whart was
I thinlcin’ abart ?
Klondike ! Klondike ! chinge the subjic’ ter Klondike 1
Theer’s no chawnoe in the street ter-dye,
Theer’s no luck darn Shoreditch wye,
Pack up yer traps an’ be orf, I sye,
An’ orf an’ awye ter Klondike.
Lots o’ chaps they ’as stawtid small, and finished it smaller
yet,
An’ the gold as yer warnts ain’t got fur nix twixt ’ere an
’ell, yer bet;
Froze ter death, or stawved ter death, or shot in yer
tracks yer’ll lie.
Fur one ’ull pick an’ oome ’ome agen, but twenty 'ull pick
an’ die.
Klondike ! Klondike 1 It sticks ter its own, does Klon-
dike.
Theer’s no chawnce in the street ter-dye,
Theer’s no luck down Shoreditch wye,
An’ fur all yer eats yer ’as ter pye,
And yer’ll find it thesime in Klondike 1

In the world’s cycling championships run at
Glasgow on 2nd August, three out of four events were
won on Dunlop tyres. Platt-Betts previous to sailing for
Australia cut down all world's records from half-mile to
30 miles. Riding a Dunlop tyred machine, and paced by
Dunlop teams, he did the half-mile in 47 3-sscc. ; mile in
lmin. 37 3-sseo.; 5 miles in Bmin. 53 2-ssee.; and 10 miles
in 18min. 2 2-ssec.*
_ Piles and How to Cure them without opera-
tion—a pamphlet describing the new discovery,
‘‘ Cannalin.” Sent free to any colony on applica-
tion to the “ Cannalin ” Dep6t for Australasia,
257 George-street, Sydney.*
No greater luxury can be imagined than a
cool drink to a thirsty animal, and, apart from the
humanitarian view of the matter, Pitt, Son and
JBadgery, Ltd., are making their water supply for
stock profitable to owners. It is surprising the
improvement in appearance of long rail-journey
stock when they get a good drink, and now the
hot weather is coming all stock looking dusty or
rough get a drink and a swim at their big dam.
This firm has spent =69000 in providing water.*
To test the endurance of the type-bars in
the Yost type-writer the framework of a machine
was set up, one type-bar and key complete placed
therein, and an apparatus applied tooperate them—
to produce upon the key a blow precisely equal to
a hard finger blow. Results at the end of 42
weeks of 59 hours per week: Total strokes,
46,000,000 = more strokes than the most used letter
on any machine would total in 36years ; yet while
the points of the type-bar were somewhat worn,
the type-bar itself was as serviceable as at the
beginning.*

. 0n ’ e of the great features of the new RED BIRD is
its bottom bracket. It is a triumph of modern skill
in 03 cle-building, and is a feature as far ahead of
bottom brackets in other cycles as the pneumatic
is ahead of the solid. The two cups which form
the bearings are turned from the finest grade of
steel possible to manufacture. They contain ten 516
balls Each cup is threaded to a steel sleeve or barrel
which adjusts inside the bottom bracket proper and
is held in place by two bolts. With the bearings it is
only necessary to oil same about twice a year
as they are thoroughly dust-proof and
the balls running in a continuous oil bath With
OTHER CYCLES it is almost impossible to get a per-
fectly trie chain alignment, whereas the chain and the
bearings, with the aid of this most important im-
provement, can be adjusted to 1 1000th part of an
inch.
This is only one of the many new and original fea-
tures on the Red Bird new models; the others will be
explained to you at our Show-room.
If you are interested in the construction of the
finest bicycle on earth, drop a line for the Red Bird
catalogue. Our catalogue, like our cycle, is better
than others.
The Red Bird guarantee is the insurance policy of
the wise wheelman. We are the makers of Bed Bird
cycles, and our guarantee is therefore backed with a
reputation of 20 years’ standing-. An ounce of pro-
tection <ruarantee is a pound of satisfaction, therefore
Red Bird riders are the satisfied cyclists.
Wm. Martin, mounted on his Red Bird fitted with
our new oil-bath bearings, rode a mile, unpaced
straightaway, in the marvellous time of 1 min. 10 2-5
sec. His wheel was geared to 136, so you can form a
little idea of how lightly the new Red Bird runs.
Martin said : “Run easy? Well, I guess it dub I
can make some other records drop on this wheel.”
OTHER CYCLES are being reduced in price, but
Red Birds continue to sell for more than other wheels,
and they’re worth it.
Tiif, Red Bird, with its new oil-bath bearings, inde-
structible frame, and adjusting chain alignment
bearings, is as superior to other cycles as the pneu-
matic tyre is ahead of other tyres.
The new Red Bird is fitted with the perfect bear-
ings, with the little oil bath and adjustable chain
alignment. Examine these important improvements.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Positano’s unaccountable failure in the Metro-
politan has to be contrasted with the consistent form of
Coil, Holbrook, and Delaware in the two races—Spring
Stakes and Met.—where they met, the English horse.
Delaware and Holbrook ran prominently from the start
on both occasions, and Coil finished third each time.
Also Holbrook, who tired at the finish of the Spring
Stakes, took advantage of his 91b. better handicap and
beat Coil by a length in the Metrop. Positano, on the
other hand, showed a splendid contempt for handicap-
ping principles by losing about a dozen lengths (on his
previous form with Coil) when his burden was reduced
by 181 b.
• * • •
“ Positano was bustled too early in the race,”
was the best explanation that Ribbleden, of the Austra-
lasian, could find for the Metropolitan fiasco. If bustled
means hustled it is a disingenuous way of putting the
case, and if it simply means “flurried ” the excuse is
not more reasonable than the average newspaper yarn
offered to the public when a glaring reversal of form has
to be accounted for. Positano won the Spring Stakes at
Rand wick by three lengths, in 2min. 36|sec., beating Coil,
Holbrook, Delaware, and Fadladeen on weight-for age
terms. Take the report of that race —“ Hopscotch, Hol-
brook, and Delaware entered the straight almost in a line,
with True Blue and Positano coming fast. At the dis-
tance the latter had them all beaten, and coming on won
by about three lengths from True Blue, who beat Coil for
places.” Three days later the Metropolitan was run over
the same distance, the time being 2min. 35sec., or ljsec.
faster than before. Take the report of that race —“ At
the half mile Delaware was six lengths in front
and going away, whilst the most prominent of the
others were Amberite, Holbrook, Survivor, Positano, and
Coil. Delaware held a clear lead into the straight, the
followers being Amberite, Holbrook, Survivor, and Coil.
. . . Survivor beat Holbrook by a neck, then a length
away came Coil.” Among the followers of the placed
horses came Fadladeen and Delaware, well in front of the
hot-pot Positano, who now met Holbrook, Coil, and Dela-
ware on better terms—ranging from 10 to 16 pounds—
than when he ran away from them on the previous Satur-
day. And the Australasian horse-writer gravely sug-
gests that Positano’s extraordinary defeat in the Metro-
politan was due to “ bustling ” in the early part of a 1)4-
mile race which was only faster than the race he
had won so easily.
* * * *
Hot and windy was the last day of Randwick
Spring Meeting. Eos and Standard were picked upon for
the High-weight Handicap, but the former failed to get a
place, and Standard won by a short neck from Australian.
Burleigh was made favorite for the Members’ Handicap,
but he finished nearer last than first, and Phillip, the next
best inquired for, won from Voma, who led from the
jump, until passed 100yds. from home. Plenty excite-
ment over the Steeplechase, during the running of which
Whitefoot 11., Cornet, Reckless, and Runnimede fell,
leaving Ditto and Parnell to fight a finish, which ended in
favor of Ditto. Eairvhouse, the favorite, the only other
horse who finished, did not run as on Metrop. day, and
finished out of the hunt. Agate, who had had many ad-
mirers all through the meeting, won the Waverley Handi-
cap, and the Carbine horse Fucile won Randwick Plate at
the odds of 10 to 1, the heavily-backed Holbrook finishing
second. At the sheds, Holbrook seemed to have an un-
mistakable lead, but Mat. Harris, fairly lifting Fucile every
stride, passed the groggy Holbrook 50yds. from home.
This left the public with but one race to suffer a recovery
on, and they went manfully for Vivian until 5 to 1 was
the best on tap, but it was not to be, as Aurea, at 12 to 1,
won by a couple of lengths from The Chief.
• * * *
Fucile (Carbine —Venice) proved in Randwick
Plate that he is a top-noich sticker, and as he is well in he
should be regarded with respect for whichever of the two
Cups he is spun for. The good performance of Amberite
and Fucile must send up Carbine stock considerably.

Report lately killed the crack jumper Fairfield,
but the chestnut showed himself very much alive at
Albury the othei day, he won the high jump with
6ft. Sin.
* * * *
According to W. P. Crick, M.P., there are 700
“ tote ” and 2000 “ double ” shops in Sydney and suburbs,
and more horse-racing around Sydney than within a
radius of 40 miles of London.
• * » *
Despite tlie many police raids on the biggest
of all Australian tote-shops, located in Collingwood,
Melb., it still flourishes with renewed safeguards against
official interference. The extensive escape-door system
has been so completed that capture is well-nigh im-
possible, unless an army of police were to surround the
entire block ; telephonic communication between head-
quarters and different points of vantage has been estab-
lished,and nobody unknowm, or unintroduced by a reliable
patron, is admitted. There are two barriers, so that if a
concerted rush were successful on the outer one the
inner would be shut and locked automatically before any
detective could reach it. But, should the worst come to
the worst, and the detectives gain the yard itself they
could arrest nobody concerned in the shop but the gate-
keepers. The actual tote-runners are attired in
black trousers and blouses all alike, and have
their heads hidden completely in masks and veils.
They stand on a platform under a galvanised-iron shed,
surrounding which are heavy timber bars extending
to the roof. Through these bars the clients have to hand
their money and make known their fancies, for the four
funereal figures in the cage speak not a word, and are
ready to flee through the trap-doors at their backs at the
slightest alarm. As far as the shop proper is concerned,
it is kept fast closed with the shutters up, the whole of
the tote business being transacted in the backyard,
reached from another street, and enclosed in a high fence
literally encrusted with barbed wire. The proprietor of
this tote was five years back an artisan, working in a
repair shop at a wage of 30s. a week. In a happy moment
he decided to relinquish the leather business for a tote,
and to-day he stands with a reputed cash capital of
£30,000.
♦ # * *
What a reflection on the moral character of the
Australian turf was the gullibility of an amateur Melb.
bookmaker, who laid against Maluma for her Newmarket
Handicap because he “ saw a letter from the stable stating
that they were going to ‘ deaden ’ Maluma and win with
Trentina.” Cronk practices are so common that even a
“ bookie,” if he is rather soft-headed, will believe any
statement of dishonesty.
The Veriscope presentment of the Corbett-
Fitzsimmons fight, at Sydney Royal, much discounts the
Yankee press reports. It shows that Fitz. forced the
pace from the start, seemingly content to take Corbett’s
left-hand jabs with the object of keeping his opponent
going at railway pace, and trusting to his (Fitz.’s) condi-
tion and stamina to stand to him at the right end of the
mill. In the sixth round Fitz. is down on one knee
whilst eight is counted, or rather until Corbett, who has
been standing right over him, is hustled back by the
referee. Once upon his feet, however, Fitz. is at his old
game of pumping Corbett, giving him no rest, and so on
until Corbett begins to stand off as if not liking the job
set him. In the 14th and last round the ’scope mulled the
knock-out blow, which cannot be seen, the first one knows
of it being Corbett on the floor until counted out, when he
suddenly jumps up and, rushing Fitz., roughs him
through the ropes. From the veriscope point of view,
which “ can’t lie,” after the eighth round it looks a thou-
sand to one as if Corbett was hunting for a soft board to
drop upon. Unfortunately, the instrument was placed on
Corbett’s corner, and as cunning Fitz. allows the ’Murkan
to walk across at the commencement of each round—
nearly all the fighting being done in Fitz.’s corner, which
is farthest from the audience—much of the mill is very
indistinct, as it is only when the figures approach the
side of platform nearest to the ’scope that the move-
ments and features can be followed with distinctness.
Corbett is a scienced man of the first water. In the open-
ing rounds his foot-work and clever escapes when
apparently cornered and his short lead to draw his man
out, reminded more than one old ring-goer of champion
amateur Seale in his palmy days—barring, of course,
Seale’s right foot first.
* * • *
A Sporting Life critic, who is in a position to
judge the real merit of M.C.C. bowling and batting
averages—apart from the question as to how they compare
with county match records—declares that Albert Trott is
one of the best all-round men in England at present.
Which amounts to another warning for Australian cricket
writers not to indulge in over-estimates of the coming Eng-
lish Eleven before it arrives. On Albert Trott’s play, as it
appears to English critics, one of the best all-round players
in this country is about equal to any of the best at ’Ome.
Put the matter this way. If an Australian expert were
asked to join with an English ditto in selecting- an All the
World Eleven, they would probably agree to mix it in the
proportion of six Englishmen to five Australians. And
yet we have individual performances cabled out from
’Ome, as though they were matters for special astonish-
ment in this savage country.

* » * *
The Vic. football soul is incensed against Dr.
Neild for having referred to murderer Hall as “a low
and brutal type ”of person. Bendigo sentiment re the
doctor’s remark can be gleaned from the gallows elo-
quence of a Melb. ’Erald reporter : “ All was over. The
man who, owing to his football fame, was a year ago the
idol of the hour in this district, the man who was known
as ‘John Bull’ by reason of his pluck and skill, and his
reputed manly qualities as an athlete, swung lifeless at the
end of the rope.” _A barracker naturally objects to hear
that his ideal Bull is low and brutal, although the state-
ment is in perfect accord with outside opinion on the
barracker’s bloomin’ self.
• • • •
From an athletic point of view, it was a terrible
waste to sacrifice Hall, the Bendigo murderer. Though
he stood but sft. 4in. and weighed 125 t,., his proportions
were so excellent as to compensate entirely for his low
stature. He was in no way muscle-bound, and was by far
the quickest and most agile footballer in the Bendigo dis-
trict, if not in all Victoria. In fact, he was so splendid a
player that, had his crime occurred five years ago, when
arts, letters, laws, politics, and everything else in Vic.
were very small potatoes compared with football, an in-
dignant populace would probably have stormed the gaol
rather than let him be hanged like a common person.
The populace would have had some precedent, too, for
did not the Pope of the day decline to interfere, despite
the frequent outrages and murders by Benvenuto Cellini,
on the ground that the community could put up with
such idiosyncrasies from so great an artist. And Hall was
just as great a footballer as Cellini was a painter or gold-
smith.
* » * *
The most extraordinary incident in the Vic.
100-mile road-race, run last Saturday with half the dis-
tance in the teeth of a cruel North wind, was the fact that
a collision between Carpenter and Johnstone, whilst
descending that terrible hill, “ Pretty Sally,” tore seven
spokes out of the former’s back wheel, and left him to
finish wobbling and with the tire scraping his back stays.
Each of the others expected Carpenter to break his neck
every turn of the pedal ; but he didn’t, he won—by
inches, in 6h. 32min. 41sec.; Johnstone second, and
Broadbent 34sec. behind him.
The race leaves E. Melb. the'premier road-team,
with Victory second and Fernside third. The three events
composing the premiership, viz., 25, 50 and 100 miles,
have been won respectively by “Jack” Green, A. C.
Middleton, and “Jimmy” Carpenter. of
Footscray club, is the man who has ridden best in all
three races. He is the lanky rider who used to ride at
the end of every long track-race last season with a hu”e
figure 1 glaring sarcasms from his back.
* * * *
Percy Cavill easily beat Englishman Tyers in
the first match of the series arranged between them, over
a distance of 1815yds. The Britisher seems to have taken
his gruel badly, and entered a protest that Cavill had
gone outside the finishing flags, but the objection was
not upheld. Tyers has offered to make a match with
Cavill over short distances, but the Australian is not
likely to take on any short distances, and will probably
refer Tyers to Jack Hellings.
• » * *
Nuttall, in his race with Ernie Cavill, set the
world a record of 6min. 38sec. for 500yds. Finney’s pre-
vious best of 6min. 43£sec. has stood since 1860.
* * * ■*
Bilmartin is credited with wheeling a straight-
away mile on Sydney Centennial Park, slightly down
grade and with a breeze behind him, in lmin. 19 3-ssecs.
If the distance travelled was really 1760yd5., and the
timing correct, some idea of the pace may be judged from
the fact that the Yankee horse Salvator, who holds the
record, galloped his mile in lmin. 35Jsecs. Martin, how-
ever, ipust have been “ poking borak ” at the crowd
when he told them that he hoped to do a minute dead
next try.
• » * •
Flounder: “ I see nothing to cavil at in one
Cavill being a pro., the other amateur. Rather a sign of
our superior arrangements as compared with the English.
It would be a kind of scandal for W. G. Grace to be pro.
and E. M. Grace amateur, but that is what it ought to
have been. Our sensible rules label the man by his
actions, not his blood or breeding. Thus, on the turf, one
brother may be an amateur steeplechase jock, another a
pro.”
* * * *
Jack Hamilton, M.L.A., winner for the sixth
time of the Q. revolver-shooting championship, had not
handled a rifle or a revolver since the ’96 competition
until he started to practise just about four davs before
the commencement of the present contest! Which some-
what discounts the assertion of some crack marksmen
that, to maintain proficiency in revolver-firing, a man
must practise for one hour daily and never miss a day.
On the last day of Q. Rifle Association’s meet-
ing, last Saturday, Hamilton, aforesaid, in the revolver-
contest, made 122 out of 126, including the full possible
tally at the longest distance, 50 yards, which latter is a
world’s record and one point better than that of the
American champion Winans, at Bisley, in ’94. Hamilton’s
scores were-at 20yd5.—(776667) 39; at 30yds. (777677)
41; at 50yds. (777777) 42. The shooting was done in a
gusty wind.

In the absence of the lanky Herbert, of -Ballarat,
W. Cummin”', of the Melb. Harriers, carried off the Vic.
10-mile cross-country running championship at Caulfield.
There was a huge attendance, as there always is at a free
show.
* * * *
S.S.: “ For deadly dulness is there any solemn
rite or function practiced bj' mail to equal harriers’ sports?
A crowd of goose-necked youths with legs like twin
bananas start away from a mark, canter out into the
waste and void, and are forgotten whilst sassiety cavorts
in the dust, and criticises its clothes, and says ‘ Haw ! ’
more or less. Yet there was a tremendous crowd at Caul-
field, Melb., the other Saturday, and the weekly papers
were filled with elaborate prints of the fashionable people
standing in clumps, saying ‘ Haw ! ’ I find that any sport
which sanctions the removal of a great deal of clothes
will pull in the crowd, and if the English cricketers can
be persuaded to play in a penny cummerbund and a little
grease-paint, they will simply impound A istralia’s little
available capital.”
* * * *
The English Amateur Rowing Association hints
at a probable refusal to accept any Australian eight as
amateur if the expenses of a trip ’Orne are to be raised by
public subscription. In its collective official character,
the amateur-athletic conscience is subject to scruples,
which don’t seem to give any uneasiness to the private
individual conscience. The Gentlemen of England
cricketers, for instance, would scorn to take a pn'To
benefit as an eleven, whereas they are mostly prepared
to reap what private benefit they can get from the game.
In “W.G.’s” case, a shilling “testimonial” colled ion
was not held to be inconsistent with his title as
“amateur,” and everybody knows that the London
Rowing Club has found means of support for some of its
star “ amateurs.” Considering that there is a wide dis-
tinction between nutting money into an oarsman's pocket
and the mere “shouting” of a journey ’Ome for him, the
objection now offered by the E.A.R.A. seems rather
absurd. It is grounded, no doubt, on 'Varsityand public-
school snobbishness. The young fellows who perform at
Henley Regatta are mostly gilt-edged amateurs, inde-
pendent of everybody except “ theguv’nor,” even if they
have not already come into a bit of “ splosh.” Their idea
of the only pwopah amateuh qualification is a well-filled
purse.
* * * *
Tlie yarn reported by Brisbane papers as told
by sole survivor Kimber, re the swamping in Moreton
Bay of the yacht Zenobia, and the loss of five lives, is
inconsecutive and incomprehensible, except on the score
that Kimber was delirious when narrating. Four hardy
yachtsmen and a boy lost all heart in a few hours, and
pegged out, in lumber's arms, within 36 hours ! One of
the four men, known as “Scotch Bob,” was one of the
most expert and daring swimmers known to Brisbane;
the worst, seas had no terrors for him. The four are
described as all having gone insane. It is a curious story.
* * * *
The benefit to Elias Laycock promises to be the
best thing of its kind Sydney has yet attempted, judging
from the committee who have rallied round the old
“ Bushranger,” as the Thames watersiders used to love to
call the burly, Saxon-bearded Elias, when he was mowing
all down before him in the Hop Bitters Handicap.
Headed by Captain Cook’s old chum, Hon. Geo. Thornton,
all classes of the community are represented. Messrs.
Hassall, See, and Farnell from the Legislature; Bill Beach
and Mick Rush, the old sculling warriors; the three
Jacks, Beales, Deery, and Deebles, from the land of sport,
Larry Foley and George Seale, the ex-champions of their
classes; and one could imagine himself back in the
seventies and eighties with the old-time amateur oarsmen,
Messrs. T. Strange, S. Freeman, N. Lockyer, J. E. Myers,
C. Dobson, and others.

“ F.” to The Bulletin :
A monster meeting of union and non-union Melbourne
butchers is announced. Great, dissatisfaction as to wages
and hours exists. Now is the time to see why this is
thus, for the carrion-men have nominally a very strong
union at the Trades Hall. Is there really truth in the
rumor that it is merely a masters’ union ? Such a body
should certainly be absolutely forbidden from enterino
under the Trades Hall aigis, if the Trades Hall is to really
fight the employes battles. It suggests organised espioti
age of Trades Hall methods and plans, and, if it’s possible
in one trade, can one be sure that it’s not practised by
others? Also, is the men’s trouble one of the results ol
the live-meat boycott, which appears to have already re-
su ted in the shutting up of scores of small shops in Melb
suburbs?
The prosecution of Jules Joubert and
another on a charge of conspiracy to defraud in
connection with Brisbane “International ” Exhi-
bition, ended last week ; and a police-court bench
discharged defendants. There were undoubtedly
many irregularities in the conduct of business
and some of the transactions seemed fishy; but
proof was lacking both of criminal intent and of
defendants’ joint responsibility. And so much
personal animus was shown by a section of the
directorate, that a great deal of sympathy wag
expressed for Joubert during the examination,
and much satisfaction at its close.
A meat-famine threatens the Land of
Sleep-a-Lot. Oattle are deoreas’ng in number
and quality, and butchers pay fabulous prices for
any scrubber carrying hide and horns. Beef,
wholesale, 4 s per 1001 b. The season cannot be
blamed. Stockowners sleepily aver that they
don’t see any points in expensive breeding im-
provements when the stock-tax secures them
■£6 for a beast only worth ,£3 in Syd-
ney ! Bread in Hobart threatens to reach
8d the 41b. loaf, and the Derwenters’ main-
stay, barracouta, formerly two for fid, now sells at
Is. fid. per ’eouta. Sydney and Melbourne vege-
tables are imported by nearly every steamer and,
altogether, the living cost is higher in Hobart than
in any other Australian port. Also, wages are
wretched. Man, horse and drav, ss. a day; top
wages for laborers, ss. per day. Benevolent Society
relieving over 1000 persons every week !

Barori Ardilauri is tipped as the somewhat
uneasy Gov. Hampderi’s successor. This should
be as good an advt. for Guinness’s stout as Bux-
ton’s appointment for the famous beer. Ardi-
laun, or Guinness (the proper name is Maginnis),
traces his pedigree to the Plantagenets, though no
one knew it till his father restored a Dublin
Cathedral with money made out of stout. The
present Baron is of fair repute, on the shady side
of 50, and a sound old Tory. The Guinness
brewery, by the way, floated for six millions some
years ago, and hugely over-subscribed, has just
paid 18 per cent, for the year ; it has now a reserve
of over a million sterling.

dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
Writes : “I have bo scheme to extort money from
abyonc whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind • but,
thank heaven, I ain now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
Ctire known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*

E. LENTHALL OLDFIELD, Principal.
Gavan Duffy claims that it was he who
first introduced the late Chief Justice Martin to
Edward Butler, his future rival, and to W. B.
Dailey, who was to be Martin’s brother-in-law.
A. certain politician’s weevils are recalled by
recent incidents. In March of last year his firm
had a row about the importation of coriander
seeds condemned by the Health Department as
being full of weevils. The politician protested
that “ as the seed is to be ground up for sale as
curry-powder, no harm is likely to arise from the
presence of weevils.” Melb. Champion (now dead)
immediately quoted three biblical verses on the
subject:—
And the House of Israel called the name thereof manna ;
and it was like coriander seeds, white. —Exodus xvi. 31.
But some of them left it till the morning, and it bred
worms and stank. —Exodus xvi. 20.
And the people went about and gathered it, and ground
it in mills. —Numbers xi. 8.

Items of evidence as to the “ state of
mind of the deceased ” in a Sale (Vic.) will-case
William Rae deposed that she went about in men’s old
boots, a pair of loggings, and a man’s hat. He had seen
her stripped to the waist in the day-time, arid he had
also seen her on the verandah of her house with only one
garment on. ~ ,
William Tracey, actuary at the Savings Bank, said she
always carried her pass-book under her garments fastened
to a leather belt by tapes. When she came into the bank
she had to lift up her dress some distance to get at the
book. Sometimes she had only a pair of moleskin
trousers under her dress. Richard Ray said he had also
seen the deceased going about the yard very scantily clad.
Possibly the poor old lady was only trying to keep
herself cool. The present writer was once belated
on a bye-road not quite 30 miles from Sydney, on
I a hot summer day, and went over to the only
: house in sight, a God-forsaken wooden structure,
I partly hidden behind some trees, to inquire the
I way. As he got close to it there was a wild burst
of 'laughter, and a girl, apparently in her early
i teens, sprinted round the house chasing another
| girl of about the same age. The front girl had
nothing on except an old bell-topper and an apron.
The second girl was dressed in one brief petticoat.
Both of them left hurriedly with shrieks, and a
wrinkled old lady, who appeared a second after,
remarked that “the girls were just trying to keep
cool.”
