Wow Burns Was Dismissed .
Lithe and lusty, clean-limbed, vigorous and
alert, he came downstairs with springing step.
Having explained the object of our mission, he
■milingly said : “ Won’t you take a seat ? Here,
take this one,” and he pushed forward a soft and
padded chair. Noticing that his eyes were on us
as we produced our pencil, we gave expression to
the familiar lines, “ A chiel’s amang you takin
notes, an’ faith he’ll print them.”
“ I’ll be only too happy if he does,” was the
neat response.
But dismissing Bobbie Burns and bardio words,
the reporter had a concise little chat with Mr.
Charles Tate, who is employed as storeman by one
of the big produce firms in Sydney, but whose
address is better known amongst his friends as
“Ithaca Cottage,” Illawarra-road, Marrickville.
“Influenza,” said Mr. Tate, “was the malady
with which I had to struggle. Of course, I didn’t
want to neglect my work, so I went in just as
usual. But I didn’t feel just as usual—not by a
long bit. Prior to this I had never experienced
sickness at any time in my life. I'm naturally
strong and stalwart. But after thirty years’ im-
munity from trouble, influenza gripped me, and it
gripped me pretty tight. Without warning, I
suddenly became ill. I always had a first-class
appetite; it left me altogether. Positively I
could eat nothing at all. Heavy headaches at-
tacked me, and they continued to increase in
violence; a spasm shot across my chest and an
overpowering weight oppressed me there ; I felt a
sickening contraction of the skin and an ominous
gathering of phlegmatic matter that choked free
respiration ; my whole chest, once firm and sound,
Decame sore, tender, and irritable ; I found myself
unable to sleep, whilst on top of all these strange
and unaccountable sensations came a sharp and
persistent cough that sucked up all my strength
and stirred the phlegm, but failed to move it.
Dull, heavy, listless, faint and aching, I went to
work, and got through somehow. How I got
through, and how I felt afterwards, you can
imagine. I—can remember 1
“‘Take half-a-dozen boxes of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People,’ said a friend, ‘ and
you’ll be saved.’
“It was kindly said and kindly meant. The
pills had been spoken highly of before, and I took
I)
“ The advice ?”
4 Yes, and the pills I ”
“ Well ? ”
“ You’ve hit upon it,” he replied, laughing; “ I
did feel 4 well ’ just as soon as I had taken them,
•’m well now, and well I intend to continue.”
“ But how soon did they affect you ? ”
“ After a few boxes had been taken I was,
honestly, completely cured. I kept on taking
them a little longer, just to make doubly sure, but
ihere was no necessity. I was absolutely cured,
elt tip-top, and had bade farewell to influenza and
ts ills. Yes,” he said, deliberatively, “ the
marvel has been quicker than ever I had antici-
>ated.”
“ How did you take them ? ”
“ Oh ! just according to directions.”
“And you’re sure that all your ailments dis-
appeared ? ”
“ Yes,” was the good-humored reply, I m
dead certain of that. Influenza, headache, sleep-
lessness, want of appetite, listlessness, racking
cough, chest-pains, weakness and phlegm all dis-
appeared as if under a magician’s wand. I was
as right, as fresh, as healthy, and as high-spirited
as I ever was in my life.
“ Of course,” he added, conscientiously, “ it’s
only a fair thing that I should tell you this after
the benefits I’ve received. My case may not have
been as severe as some, but, it certainly gave my-
self and my family the greatest anxiety as my
cure gave them the greatest surprise and pleasure.
I give you these particulars exactly as they
occurred, without exaggeration and with truth in
every detail. Nor do I wish it to be exaggerated.
Indeed, there is no necessity ; the cure was a good
one, and speaks for itself. More or better I can-
not say.”
A. handshake. %
A cheery “ Good evening 1 ” >
And we passed out into the street 1

Melb. : “Trades’ Hall secretary Murphy’s
‘ History of the Eight Hours’ Movement’ has been
issued in a penny edition. It shows that the real
founder of the Eight Hours in Melbourne was not
James Galloway, as commonly supposed, but
James Stephens. Both were masons. Galloway
was the magnetic orator of the innovation. He
wa f ? years old in 1856, in the early part of
winch year the movement was carried to success.
The inscription on the fine monument to him
in Melbourne cemetery would seem to be mis-
leading, or, at all events, ambiguous, by Murphy’s
account, Yet Stephens was on the spot. Murphy,
seeming to take it for granted that everyone
knows, does not say whether Stephens is
dead or alive. I saw it in print, maybe
two years ago, that Stephens was alive.
He was a Chartist in England, and discharged
from his employment at Windsor Castle for being
S I UC “- Nevertheless, he afterwards wrought on
the Parliament Houses, Westminster, with 200
other Chartists, and, indeed, they could get
none but Chartist masons. When Stephens
came to Melb., the working hours were nominally
10 a day, but really nine and a-half, through two
intervals a quarter of an hour each, being allowed
for Smoko, ’at 11 and 3. In these gaps, Stephens
used to harangue on eight hours. He and Gallo-
way worked the stone-mason-trade up to action.
Most of the contractors met them in a fair spirit,
and Murphy refers in a kindly way to Amess
(afterwards Mayor of Melb.), Linacre, Holtom,
Mitchell (Melbas father), and Cornwell (father of
Madame Midas). Two obstinate contractors were
Cornish, with the job of Parliament House,
and Holmes with that of the Western Market.
However, the others amicably concluded the
night Hours arrangement with the trade. A
meeting m the Queen’s Theatre signalised this
agreement. Ga loway’s speech was electric.
Quickly, a few days afterwards, a procession of
i masons was organised. About 800 formed it.
1 hey marched through the city. An effect of the
walls of Jericho took place, both at the Western
Market and Parliament House. The masons
were all at work at both places. When the pro-
cession passed they threw down their tools and
fell in with it ! Cornish and Holmes only held
out a fortnight. Such was the dashing style
with which Labor went to work in that
rapidity which characterised
all the Labor operations of ’56, the Eight Hours
Union of all the trades was inaugurated.
Other English Chartists rallied around Stephens.
I romment was T. W. Vine, carpenter. He is
well remembered with his striking likeness to
Jirougham, clean-shaven in face, incisive in talk.
His three daughters made that time-honored
banner whmh has been carried in front of the
Melb. Eight Hours’ processions for 41 years, with
its motto— Eight Hours’ Labor, Eight Hours’
Recreation, Eight Hours’Rest.’ The letters are
white, on a blue ground, with red border. Hero
is the explanation : Galloway, who was Scotch,
wanted the flag to be somewhat of a replica of the
Blue Blanket of Edinburgh.’ This was a banner
given to the Guilds in 1482, by James 111,
ot Scotland, and yet preserved. Vine, who was
English, insisted on the red, white and blue. The
motto was taken from a speech by Dr. Embling.
Murphy brings Galloway before the mind’s eye—a
big, jolly, stalwart fellow, with curly brown hair
He also puts in effectively bits out of the speeches
“ Charles Jardine Don, the most famous of the
Melb. masses. In Don’s own words, he used to
pound bluestone by day at the Post Office, and
pound the squatters at night, as a Member of
Parliament. He was a man of culture, and spoke
much as John Burns does nowadays. When an
open-air meeting was held at Williamstown,
by Don pointed across the waters
of Hobson s Bay to the lights of Melbourne,
asking what had made all that ? ‘ Why gentle-
men, labor !’ Then there was noble ’Wilson
Gray, of whom Higinbotham said: ‘He never
compromised with an opinion. I wish I could say
the same of myself.’ Murphy shows how, even as
the masons led to the eight hours of all the trades,
80 , .r i j ei B*ht hours lead to the land convention,
which led to the formation of the corner in the
t , ssem blyt and the ultimate success of the
Liberal party. Of course the victory would have
come about under any circumstances, but these
are the circumstances under which it did come
about.’

FINKLER ON PAPAIN.
After giving the result of a number of experiments, Finkler goes on :—“Papain,
therefore, shows a more energetic peptonising power than pepsine, and especially so when
the proportion of the liquid to the albumen is small— i.e., in the proportion of concentra-
tion in'which food generally exists in the stomach and intestines. The great variability
in the quality of the commercial pepsine (some of which, under the most favorable circum-
stances, do not peptonise more than 20 per cent, of the albumen of the meat) gives Papain
( Paw-Paw ) also the preference, as it is always made of one uniform quality.”

Christchurch (M.L.) Weekly Press, a
vigorous Tory organ in a green cover, furiously
damns the Seddon Government for contriving to
run the post-office at a profit. “The present
Ministry,” wails this voice in pale green, “having
nearly exhausted other means of extracting money
from the public, looks upon the post-office merely
as a huge taxing-machine for the purpose of screw-
ing more revenue out of the colonists.” The same
Tory paper also damns the Government for not
making the railways pay, and says it is a stupid
spendthrift organization with neither manners nor
intelligence. So it seems the Tory person is
equally mad over things that pay and things that
don’t.

“ Eucalyptus Oil ” Bosisto, of Melbourne,
has been growing for some time at his Wimmera,
Vic., place a Central-India fodder-plant Tri-
folium Alexandrinum, or “ Bersin clover ”), which
flourishes hugely in rainless country. Baron von
Mueller suggested its trial in dry Australian
country in his wondrously suggestive “Select
Plants.” In the rainless districts of Egypt it gives
three crops a year, running 2ft. high. Scientist
Bosisto is ready to supply gratis seeds for trial in
the backblocks.
One clear-headed individual, at all events,
managed to make a rise at the Tasmania wreck. A
distracted woman remembered at the last moment
that she had left all her jewellery in her cabin,
and rushed back for it, but it was gone—some-
body, with an eye to making a fresh start in life
when he got ashore, had taken the lot. Someone
else rose to the occasion and “ made ” a watch ;
when a half-drowned boat was struggling in the
darkness a passenger passed up his ticker to the
officer who was steering, and someone peacefully
annexed it on the way back. The calmest person
in the half-demented crowd was a Maori, who
came on deck in the ship’s last moments with his
bell topper and black gloves on ; he was among the
saved, and went ashore with the black gloves
rather the worse for salt-water, but still looking
like a fine colored gentleman of the olden time.
Also, the most panic-stricken of the crowd was an
Assyrian, who had to be sat upon and have water
poured over him to prevent him going mad on the
spot.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Tales of tlie Austral Tropics.
In Praise of Humbug.
[FOR THE BULLSTIN.]
Hail, Humbug, hail! Sky-pilots, politicians,
And Fatted Fools who bask in high positions,
Kings, Kaisers, Queens, and Quacks bow down
before thee,
While courtiers all in concert sweet adore thee ;
Great backer of the brainless ! Friend of Fools !
Thy lore outweighs the learning of the schools ;
To thousands living thou art daily bread,
And fulsome epitaph to thousands dead.
Hail, Humbug, Hail!
Hail, Humbug, hail! Great Manager of Marriage,
Thou mak’st the coquette trip with modest car-
riage,
Thou givest ancient maids their arts so simple,
Their youthful locks, rose cheeks, and charming
dimple
Fair as the foliage on the verdant myrtle ;
Thy votaries shine with wine and festive turtle,
And, when they turn the scale at twenty stone,
Preach loudly “ Abstinence ! ” with pious groan.
Hail, Humbug, Hail!
Hail, Humbug, hail ! From half-a-million
churches,
From wheresoe’er a pallid parson perches,
From hustings, public platform, palace, gaol
The paean swells and surges, “ Humbug, Hail
Thy temple shines on Quackery’s sacred hill;
Thou sanetifiest bolus, sermon, pill,
The sweetest strumpet thou —the blandest belle
That ever strewed with flowers the path to hell.
Hail, Humbug, Hail !
Hail, Humbug, hail ! Thou crocodilean weeper
Thou crafty politician’s conscience keeper i
Thou lawyer’s lamp! Thou pure perennial
fountain
Of onion-gotten tears ! Thou lying mountain I
To thee, great president of “ boiling pots ”
Bow vicious, virtuous, sober men and sots;
Thy boots we kiss, regardless where they’ve trod,
And hail thee King and Christ and Lord and God!
Q.
Hail, Humbug, Hail !
P. LUFTIG.
Kobar : “Your Hebrew story of ‘ peez-
ness first’ recalls a Scotch yarn. Housewife:
‘ Baker, just look at this loaf, here is a cockroach
in it.’ Baker (a Scotchman) looks accordingly,
and sees a cockroach, but nips same between his
fingers and places it in his mouth, with the
remark: ‘Ye don’t ken a raisin from a cockroach ! ’
The woman is convinced, and continues to deal.”
A number of Q. country postmasters were
lately instructed to examine all the interprovincial
newspapers, and stop delivery of all which they
thought contained indecent advts. As the intelli-
gence and morals of some Q. postmasters are just
what you might expect for .£lOO a year or so, the
result was some sensational stoppages, which
caused great annoyance in Southern Congrega-
tional circles. (This particular religious paper,
being careful to tear out everything which might
bring a blush to the cheek of the Nastynice
family, escaped practically unscathed.) The Bul-
letin hopes that there are not many communities
of alleged white men which would meekly submit
their Sunday’s reading to the censorship of any
illiterate elder with a monkey-shave.
The Squatter ' s Dream.
[FOR THE BULLSTIN.]
The apparently-dying squatter was in a trance,
and his sorrowing relations and friends around his
bedside were sorely afraid that he would never
regain consciousness. After about two hours,
however, his eyes opened, and he looked round
with the pleased smile of a man who finds himself
among congenial surroundings. The doctor looked
puzzled, shook his head, and felt the patient’s
pulse.
“Strange,” he muttered. ‘lt looks like a
change for the better. He may recover, after all.”
The sick man smiled again. “ I feel a good deal
better, doctor,” he said, weakly. “ I’ve just had
an experience.”
“There now,” interrupted the medical man,
soothingly, “ don’t try to say too much. You are
not strong enough to talk.”.
“Oh, I think I shall pull through,” replied the
other, weakly, but confidently. “I’ve just been
to Heaven.”
The faces round the bedside looked blankly at
one another. Clearly, the patient was anything
but in his right mind. He caught the look, and
smiled again.
“ Queer enough place for me to get to,” he said,
** but I’ve been there. Your description the other
day was not very close to the real thing,” he re-
marked to the clergyman.
The latter could not think of anything appro-
priate to say to this, for the sick man spoke with
an air of assured knowledge.
“Not so bad a place at all,” he went on.
‘ Beautiful country. Deep, rich soil. And
grass ! You should see that grass. Water, too,
flowing round everywhere, just like I often said
I’d have fixed things up if I’d had my way.
Cattle and sheep— such, cattle and sheep ! All of
them prize; all rolling fat. The folks told me
that the country carried 50 sheep to the acre
easily.”
Most of the bystanders were pastoralists, and
the clergyman observed that they were listening
with a keener interest than he had ever noticed
them to give to his stories of golden streets and
jasper walls.
“ There were no fences,” went on the sick man,
“at least I didn’t find any. I rode a thousand
miles in a straight line on the run that was given
to me, but I never saw a fence the whole way. I
searched the pasture for three days and couldn’t
find a single Bathurst burr, three-cornered jack,
thistle, or poison-weed of any sort. The only
rabbits, wallabies, and dingoes I saw were stuffed
specimens in a glass case at the homestead. And
you ought to have seen that homestead ! Tennis-
lawn in front, big orchard at the back, beautiful
views. No flies, no mosquitoes, no dust.”
“ Ah ! ” the audience gasped approvingly.
“ You may not believe me,” said the sick man
as he continued, “ but I do assure you that what
I’m going to say is fact. The Govt, didn’t come
round for rent, or taxes of any sort.”
“Ah ! ” The audience sighed as if this was far
too good to be true.
“What’s more, the Govt, didn’t send round
any inspectors, nor experts to show us that wool
grows on sheep, and that pumpkins can be grown
on very rich land if you give them plenty of
attention.”
Another chorus of approval.
“ But ” said the sick man. And he paused
as with the burden of a great disappointment
upon him. The listeners sympathised. They
knew that earthly life was full of “ifs” and
“ buts,” and they felt that some of those incon-
veniences might easily have crept even into the
pleasant paddocks of Heaven.
“ I might have been there yet,” continued the
sick man wistfully, “ but I felt that there was
something wanting. I went about for weeks feel-
ing that something had been left out.”
He looked around him appreciatively at all his
old friends and relations, and they felt a glow of
self-gratification in the thought that in his far-off
Earadise he had not forgotten those among whom
e had lived and worked on earth.
“It seems incredible,” he went on, “but, do
you know, there was not such a thing as a mort-
gage or a drought on the whole property ! ”
Incredulity was clearly marked on every face.
“ No,” he continued. “ And as I couldn’t feel
at home under the circumstances I came back.”
“ Humph 1 ” muttered the doctor, “I’d have
thought you would have been sure of the drought,
at all events.”
Sydney.

Yes,” said the other; “ I thought that was it.

From Cloncurry. A selector’s horse came
home some few days ago, looking very unwell.
The horse eventually died and was being cut up
to feed the pigs when a small snake crawled out
of its nostril. The horse had evidently irritated
the intruder and lost his life in consequence.

GOULD’S TINY TONICS Again Triumphant!
A LADY IN ERSKINEVILLE (a Sydney Suburb) IS CURED AFTER DOCTORS
AND HOSPITALS HAVE FAILED.
. . Gases Fully Investigated by the Press. . .
A MIRACLE OP MIRACLES.
PHYSICIANS BAFFLED AND HOSPITALS
UNSUCCESSFUL.
Mrs. Messer, of “ Glenbrook,” of No. 6 Union-
street, Erskineville, Sydney, in answer to a
reporter’s inquiry as to her remarkable recovery
from a long and serious illness, said : “I have off
and on been ill for six long years, the last three of
which have proved a terrible experience for one
who loves her home and likes to supervise it. Dur-
ing this latter period severe pains in the temples,
that throbbed in angry tumult through my head,
inflicted awful penalties upon me. Bad head-
aches, acute indigestion, sharp pains in my back
and between the shoulder-blades, also distressed
me, but my most serious suffering was chronic in-
fluenza, which attacked me in virulent forms, both
in summer and winter, during the whole six years
of which I’ve spoken. The influenza and the indi-
gestion, together with pains and aches in various
parts of the body, were quite enough to prostrate an
ordinary woman, and at times I was compelled to
take to my bed for three days running. But I
fretted at being away from household duties, and
always tried to rise. I used, however, to be quite
unable to see the whole day out, and would have
rests of an hour each all through the day, so that
I could gather a little energy, and keep on as well
as was allowed me. I also suffered agonies from
mind, and although at times harassed with a
yearning craving for some particular food, when-
ever it was obtained for me I turned from it, and
could not eat it at all. What I did eat was very
little, and of the lightest; but no matter how
little or how light it was, I was unable to digest
it, and suffered indescribable internal torture after
each morsel. I continually had a horrid, sick
taste in my mouth, and sometimes could not tell
what food I was eating. Insomnia bothered me
also, and I’ve often been the whole night tossing
and rolling about on the bed, and thinking, with-
out ever being able to get a wink of sleep. This,
in the hot summer months, was particularly
weakening and disastrous. Thus I became so
ill that I went for a course of outdoor treat-
ment at the Prince Alfred Hospital. Here I
was treated for acute indigestion and severe
headaches, but never a bit of good did the
medicines do me, although I took them reli-
giously. Then I consulted my doctor—a well-
known physician in Erskiueville —and so seri-
ously did he regard my case that at the
beginning of this winter just passed he said to
me: 1 Mrs. Messer, you’re suffer ng from chronic
influenza. It’s no good for you to take medicine.
A change of air is the only thing that will benefit
you, and if you don’t go away you will never get
through the winter.’ But I have lived through
it! ” the lady remarked, with a smile, to the re-
porter, who was carefully taking a record of her
remarks ; “ and I hope to live through many more
winters yet,” she continued. “How did you
cheat the medical faculty ? ” he inquired. “Well,
one day,” was the answer, “ I read an advertise-
ment recommending Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver
Pills for cases somewhat similar to mine. I
thought over the matter, and concluded to try
them, and that’s the reason you are here to-day.
After a few doses of the x>ills I was surprised to
find my appetite improving. This went on, and
now to-day 1 have a first-class healthy appetite.
Then my condition improved altogether, my
spirits rose, my strength increased, and all the
pains from indigestion, and all the pains from
influenza slowly left me. Sleep came to my aid,
brightness to my eyes, and agility to my step.
Not even the headaches remained, and with every
trace of influenza and indigestion, and their
attendant maladies gone from my system, I felt a.
new woman revived with a new body. Gould’s
Tiny Tonic Liver Pills worked this marvellous
change, and worked it entirely, for I used no
other remedy. My husband and family and my
friends were not less astonished than myself at
this most unprecedented recovery, and their
delight and mine knew no bounds. With all
pains, faintness, and despondency gone, I look
forward to a long and happy life.” “Have you any
objections,” the reporter asked, “to these facts
being published, Mrs. Messer?” “None at all,”
she replied, with emphasis, “for with me it is a
matter of duty. I honestly feel that I have a right
to tell my case for the benefit of others who may
be suffering the same as I did.”
Mrs. Messer’s case is one of many, and it
honestly deserves that sensible consideration which
afflicted persons bring to bear on matters which
affect themselves. For ever hundred persons who
decide to give Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver Pills a
fair and impartial trial we guarantee ninety-nine
recoveries, with full assurance that our guarantee
will be established.
INFLUENZA.
Few people escape this terrible pestilence of the
nineteenth century, which sweeps over Australia
periodically. Some escape, but they are usually
the people who escape eyerything because they
are healthy. It is within the power of most
everyone to become the same. If you do become
a victim we would advise the following course :
For ordinary cases take Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Liver Pills as directed, and at the same time
bathe the feet well in hot water on going to bed.
If the case is very bad, the patient should remain
in bed, eating only light food and sleeping as
much as possible, taking at the same time small
does of Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver Pills to keep the
bowels open.

Darna Crossing.
[FOR THE BULLSTIN.]
In the winds a gum-tree tossing
Twisted branches to and fro
Marks where men may come and go
Through the Darna Crossing,
When the river’s low.
On the banks that hot winds harden
With a chime of purple bells,
Here and there a blossom tells
Of the fated garden
Where Aluna dwells.
Chains of snow have found undoing,
Rocking down the Darna bends,
Dealing death to foes and friends ;
Hark to his rough wooing
As the flood descends !
All in white a dainty maiden,
Waiting there as lovers wait,
Watches him go past the gate
To the brown lips laden
With his fearsome freight.
At the garden reins a rider
On a hardly-ridden steed,
Foam-white shoulder, flanks that bleed ;
Leaps to earth beside her
Courtly in the deed.
Love is a light thing for women,
Love to men is all the world !
Scorn the kissing lips has curled
While the lovelig'.its swim in
Eyes with tears impearled!
Lightest love this maiden’s dower ;
Love was all the world to him.
So she bade him Darna swim.
Just to pluck a flower
For a girlish whim.
Then he answered gently: “Royal
Here has toiled since Dawn’s first kiss,
Now the sun at setting is ;
Well you know me loyal.
Yet I cannot this.”
“Were I man,” said she with scorning,
“ And a love’s wish to fulfil,
Did she ask me ride until
Dawn to-morrow morning,
I would do her will.
“ Must I wait till floods have lowered ?
Is the last of knighthood dead ?
Here between us be it said :
Ere I wed a coward,
I shall never wed.”
Then the hot blood gathered purple
And he took the tiny hand ;
“ Where the wattle blooms we land,
Or where waters hirple—
Love, you understand ! ”
So once more he mounted Royal
And he turned him head to stream,
Where the gold blooms flash and gleam,
Calling, “ Hold me loyal
To my vanished dream ! ”
Down the roaring Darna river
Float the rider and the steed,
Ribboned with the river-weed,
And the brown banks never
Hear them pass, or heed.
Down the foam-whipped Darna river,
Twenty rocking miles or more,
Man and horse are washed ashore
To the one dark lover
Worth the waiting for.
Still the winds of old are tossing
Twisted branches to and fro
Marking yet where men may go
Through the Darna crossing
When the river’s low.
WILL. H. OGILVIK.
The Ashes of a Romance.
[FOR THE BULLSTIN.]
It wa g the loneliest shanty on that long,
lonely journey. The drought had lasted all
tl,rou^h <j he summer, and the rainy season had
come and gone without one fall to moisten tne
cracked ground. Our horses floundered along the
red track, where the dust—a foot deep—hid all
ruts and holes, which were only discovered as the
bump of a wheel jerked us up from our seats, or
the driver’s quick oath followed sharp on a horse’s
stumble. The dust had caked all over the poor
brutes, clinging to their growing winter coats and
it a p. rea t table of vivid green, but now there
w ' as , not a blad ? of grass to break the monotony of
the dreary landscape, and not a tree to cast its thin
shadow on the burnt ground-a dreary expanse
stretching unbroken to the horizon. In places
the red soil grew to dark, and here, as we
drove P ast > we could see th , e thick ’ bubblin £
blackness of those strange mud springs.
We were behind our time, and as we drew near
the shanty we could see the fresh team of five
horses waiting for us. We had a clump of salt-
bush to pass first, with its white crust on leaves
and branches, and then the sunlight on the iron-
roofed cottage flashed into our eyes. It had a
broken fence partly surrounding it, but nothing
grew in the small onc i osure . A cord was stretched
at the back with a few clothes hung up to dry. An
occasional blast of wind carried a red cloud of
dust over all. As we pulled up, the groom slouched
forward to unhook our horses. rhe name of
groom seemed given m irony, for the shaggy five
that awaited us could have known no grooming
for many a long day Very little grooming had
the man himself undergone, either, with his un-
kempt beard, and clothes hanging loosely on his
bent shoulders and shrunken limbs. He watched
me pretty narrowly as I got down from the dusty
coach to lean on the fence while I cut up my
tobacco. There was something in those furtive
looks that made me pause, with pipe half-
filled, and take a good look at the man. Surely
t h ere was something dimly familiar in the tall,
loosely-knit figure, something that recalled a time
when rheumatism had not bent the shoulders.
Behind the thickened features I seemed to see a
clear-cut face that once was handsome, and
beneath the beard a small boyish moustache.
Then I started forward and caught him by the
arm. My God, Carlton . 1 said, is it you .
* * * *
When our old general came back to India,
after his long leave in England, and astonished us
all by appearing with a beautiful young wife, o
course there were not wanting people at tne ni -
station to prophesy how it would end. rie
was not a popular man. He was a can-
tankerous brute to us youngsters, and, tnoug
he was a great “ladies’ man,” we at the station
had been wont to laugh at his efforts to captivate
the fair sex. But now we felt that we could laugh
no longer. She almost took my breath away when
I first saw her, with her warm beauty, the full
round curves of her, and her red lips. 1 pitied
her from the first, but with some indignation m
my pity that a woman, who could have won a hot
voung fervor of devotion, should join herself to a
worn-out rake. I feared the time when she
should find out her mistake—and that night,
Carlton, my fellow-subaltern and chum, burst into
my quarter, shaking all oyer as with ague, and
told me, his voice broken with sobs, that this was
the girl he had so often raved of to me, that it was
she he had worked and saved for, that it was her
letters that every month or so had sent him into
a rush of tempestuous spirits. When 1 heard
the boy’s voice tremble as he told of the tears m
her eyes when she recognised him, and grow wild
as he cursed the man who married her and swore
to murder him or put a bullet in his own head, I
felt that the scandal-talkers were more prophetic
than they imagined, and that coming tragedy had
already cast its shadow over us. It was worse
still after the first dance. Carlton would go, m
spite of my efforts to dissuade him, and of course
they had an interview and perilous explanations.
It was the old hackneyed story that she told him,
of worldly relations urging a match, of an unhappy
home, and all the rest of it.
Jove ! what a night that was, that night of our
regimental dance ! On the darkness of the veran-
dah, dimly-lighted here and there by colored-
paper lanterns, the night air gently rustled the
leaves of the great trumpet-flower that climbed the
posts, and breathed the soft hum of insect life.
There was a spangled starry sky outside with the
tall palms waving against it. The music of the
band, the softness of a girl’s warm arm, and the
fragrance of her breath were intoxicating tousyoung
fellows who had been stewing in purgatory, and
little time had I to think of Carlton, though I once
caught a glimpse of him as he went along the
verandah, and the gleam of a white satin dress.
It was Carlton’s hour, for gout had kept the
General a prisoner at home.
The great unrest of an Indian night was around
us. There was a clash of monotonous music from
the village and the dirge-like sound of tuneless
singing. The music and clash of tomtoms grew
nearer, and lights flashed along the road. A pro-
cession of natives came past dancing and waving
their arms, some carrying torches and others
brightly-colored lamps. The lights blazed on the
small-humped bullocks, and on the waggon they
drew, on which a high, gaudily-painted throne
was built, and there, perched far above the sur-
rounding crowd, sat two tiny children wrapped in
gorgeous silk and embroideries and bedecked
with shining tinsel ornaments that weighed down
their small shoulders. They clung nervously to
each other and to the gilded sides of their throne
as it swayed from side to side. This was their
bridal procession. It was a weird scene with its
medley of blazing color, gesticulating figures, and
loud, clashing noises, with the children’s frightened
faces above all.
“ Marriages are made in Heaven, aren’t they,
old fellow ? ” said Carlton’s voice at my elbow,
with a note of cynicism in it that was new. We
stood on the verandah-steps to watch the proces-
sion out of sight. I half turned as he spoke to
make some reply, when I saw the light from the
open door shining on the girl’s face that looked up
into his, and my words died away as I noticed its
ghastly pallor and trembling white lips. She
drew her cloak round her, but her hand shook and
could not fasten the clasp. Then she suddenly
turned to me, “ Please take me to my carriage,'’'
she said, “lam so tired.” She spoke no word as
we threaded our way through the guests, only now
and then her hand tightened involuntarily on my
arm. “Good night,” I saidas I closed her car-
riage door, but the only sound that came back to
me was a stifled sob. *
Carlton came down to my quarters that night.
He laughed and talked so loudly that I thought
he had been drinking. “You’d better turn in,
old fellow,” I said, but as he stood there with his
flushed face and heated eyes another idea came to
me. I caught him by the shoulder as he was
going. “Carlton, old man,” I said, “for God’s
sake don’t be a damned fool.” He laughed as he
shook off my hand, and his noisy song echoed
with the banging of my door.
Next day even the most inveterate gossip had
subject enough for talk, for two people were miss-
ing from the station, and these two were the
General’s bride and a junior subaltern.
* * * •
These scenes passed one by one before my
mental sight, and then I opened my eyes to the
dreary track, the broken fence whereon I leaned,
and the shabby, worn-out groom at my side, I
turned and glanced at the shanty with its iron
roof reddened with dust, and curved in at one
corner over a broken verandah-post. A woman
crossed the yard and went through the open door.
She had taken some clothes from the line, and
they hung from her arrn. She wore no hat, aild
the breeze caught and blew her grey-streaked hair
in wisps across her face and loosened the unbecom-
ing knot in which it was twisted. A loose,
draggled gown hung over her shapeless figure.
Carlton followed my gaze and laughed shortly.
“ Yes,” he said, “ she is here with me. Perhaps
you don’t recognise her.”

Can this be bony-fide? .
Newspaper proprietors have, when threat-
ened with actions for libel, almost invariably re-
fused to give up the name of the contributor of the
alleged libellous matter, yet it has always been
considered doubtful how far they were justified in
their refusal. Now, however, the question may
be considered settled. The English Court of
Appeal lately set aside a discovery-order, by
which the defendants in a libel-suit were, directed
to allow the plaintiffs to inspect the MS. of the
article complained of, the court holding that the
defendants ought not to be compelled to disclose
the name of the writer of the libel.
